Rodin on Art and Photography
The Red Page
How is it that a sculptor gives to a
figure carved from a block of marble the il-
lusion of life? How, especially, does he
manage to convey in a stationary figure of
bronze the idea of movement? This inter-
esting Question, among a host of others, is
answered by the greatest of modern sculp-
tors, Auguste Itodin, in Art (Ifodder and
Stoughton, per Angus and Robertson, 19s.
net.), a work that should be in the hands
of every artist in the world. Rodin did not
A\iite this book, but allowed a literarv
friend, Paul Gsell, to discuss with him the
meaning of his art, am. to collect these in-
teresting talks in this sumptuous and fully
illustrated book. In their discussions Rodin
fiequently mentioned pictures and statues
by other masters, and his analysis of these
classics is made easy for the reader by the
reproduction of the works criticised. But
Rodin’s own wonderful works form the bulk
of the 60 magnificently reproduced illustra-
tions.
Let us listen awhile to one of the most
interesting discussions between master and
disciple:—
Ait, Rodin pronounces, ’'cannot exist
without life.. How could the joy o"sorrow
lu f-t object—of a block of stone—
aftect us. The illusion of life is obtained
in our art by good modelling and bv move-
ment.. . Movement is the translation from
one attitude to another.
. simple statement, which has the
air of a truism, is, to tell the truth, the key
to the mystery. The painter or the sculptor
....represents the transition from one pose
to another—he indicates how insensibly the
first glides into the second. In his work we
still see a part of what was and we dis-
cover a part of what is to be. An example
wil enlighten you better. You mentioned
just now the statue of Marshall Ney by
.Iv' - ~ (> you recall the figure clearly?”
.A®’ says. “The hero raises his
swoid. Shouting -Forward’ to bis troops at
the top of his voice.”
“Exactly. Well, look at it s*till more
cioseiy. You will then notice this : the le'-s
1 wu and the hand which holds the
sheath of the sabre are placed in the atti-
tude that they had when he drew—the left
leg is drawn back so that the sabre may be
easily grasped by the right hand, which' has
just drawn it; and as for the left hand it
is arrested in the air as if still offering the
sheath. JNow examine the body. It must
have; been bent slightly toward the left at
the moment when it performed the act
which I have described, but here it is erect
here is the chest thrown out, here is the head
ruining toward the soldiers as it roars out
the order to attack; here, finally, is the right
arm raised and brandishing the sabre.
bo there you have a confirmation of what
1 have just said; the movement in this
statue is only the change from, a first atti-
tude—that which the Marshal had as lie
drew his sabre—into a second, that which
he had as he rushes, arm aloft, upon the
enemy.
. that is all the secret of movement as
interpreted by art. The sculptor compels, so
to speak, the spectator to follow the de-
velopment of an act in an individual. In
the example that we have chosen, the eyes
are forced to travel upward from the lower
limbs to the raised arm, and, in so doing,
they find the different parts of the figure
represented at instants, they have
the illusion of beholding the movement per-
formed.”
t studied then Rodin’s wonderful “St.
John, and saw “that the rhythm of this
figure led, as Rodin had said, to a sort of
evolution between two balances. The figure
leaning at first, all its weight upon the left
toot, which presses the ground with all its
strength, seems to balance there while the
eyes look to the right. You then see all
the body bent in that direction, then the
right leg advances and the foot takes hold
the ground. At the same time the left
shoulder, which is raised, seems to endea-
vor to bring the weight of the body to this
side, m order to, aid the leg which is behind
to come forward. Now, the science of the
sculptor has consisted precisely in imposing
all these facts upon the spectator in the
order in which I have stated them, so that
their succession will give the impression of
movement.”
Jt
“Have you ever attentively examined in-
stantaneous photographs of walking figures?”
Rodin suddenly asked Gsell. “Well, what
did you notice?”
“That they seem never to advance. Gen-
erally they seem to rest motionless on one
leg or to hop on one foot.”
“Exactly! Now, for example, while my
St. John’ is represented with both feet on
the ground, it is probable that an instanta-
neous photograph from a model making the
same movement would show the back foot
already raised and carried towards the other.
Or else, on the contrary, the front foot
xvould not yet be on the ground if the back
leg occupied in the photograph the same
position as in my statue. Now it is exactly
for that reason that this model photographed
would present the odd appearance of a
man suddenly stricken with paralysis and
petrified in his pose If, in fact, in in-
stantaneous photographs, the figures, though
taken while moving, seem suddenly fixed
in mid-air, it is because, all parts of the
body being reproduced exactly at the same
twentieth or fortieth of a second, there is
no progressive development of movement as
there is in art.”
“But, 5 ’ asks his friend, “have you not
IS ed „i many l imGS to me that th e artist
ought always to copy nature with the
greatest sincerity? Well, then, when in
the interpretation of movement he com-
pletely contradicts photography, which is an
unimpeachable mechanical testimony, he
evidently alters truth.”
• ISCf r .°l )] ilodin, “it is the artist who
io truthful and it is photography which lies,
tor in reality time Moes not stop, and if the
aitist succeeds in producing the impression
ot a movement which takes several moments
toi accomplishment, his work is certainlv
much less conventional than the scientific
image, where time is abruptly suspended,
it is that which condemns certain modern
painters who, when they wish to represent
horses galloping, reproduce the poses fur-
mshed by instantaneous photography.
(>ericault is criticised because in his pic-
ture hpsofti Races,’ he has painted his
horses galloping, fully extended, rfntrc d
fc/ve. throwing their front feet forward and
their back feet backward at the same in-
stant. It is said that the sensitive plate
nevei gives the same effect. And, in fact,
in instantaneous photography, when the fore-
legs of a horse are forward, the hind legs
having by their pause propelled the body
onward, have already had time to gather
themselves under the body in order to re-
commence the stride, so that for a moment
the tour logs are almost gathered together in-
the air, which gives the animal the appear-
ance of jumping off the ground, and of being
motionless in that position.
. “Now, I believe that it is Gerieault who
is right, and not the camera, for his horses
appear to run: this comes from the fact
that the spectator from right to left sees
first the hind legs accomplish the effort
whence the general impetus results, then
the body stretched out. then the forelegs
which seek the ground ahead. This is false
in reality, as the actions conld not be
simultaneous ; but it is true when the parts
are observed successively, and it is this
truth xalone that matters to us, because it
is that which we see and which strikes us.”
.* •*
Rodin’s criticism raises an interesting
problem for the photographer. How can he
represent a man walking so as to get the
illusion of movement in Rodin’s “Man Walk-
ing”? The. problem is not insoluble; for
the mechanism of the instantaneous camera
may, in expert hands, help the eye to see the
two movements on the same plate. An in-
stantaneous photograph at high speed is
taken by a mere slit in the blind travelling'
rapidly up the surface of the ex-
posed plate. Thus a high-speed in-
stantaneous photograph represents not
one moment of exposure for the whole
plate, but an exposure that begins and
ceases at the bottom of the plate, as the
slit in the blind starts to travel up it, a
fraction of time before the exposure at the
top of the plate begins and ceases. Evidence
of this defect is sometimes seen in instan-
taneous photographs of tennis players, where
the racquet striking appears to'have been
bent. What happened was that, as the
racquet was moving forward in a “smash,”
the top travelling much more quickly than
the handle, when the exposure of the handle
was made the racquet was perpendicular,
but by the time the slit of exposure had
moved to the top of the racquet, the top
had moved forward, leaving the impression
of the handle still upright, but recording
the later position of the top of the racquet.
The result is an apparently bent racquet.
Now. it should be possibly for a pho-
tographer, working with a slow travelling
slit that moved horizontally across the plate,
to catch in a man walking, or a horse gal-
loping, first the impression of the back foot,
or the hind legs, and on the same plate—
an • almost inappreciable fraction of a
second later—the impression of the new posi-
tion of the front foot, or the front legs—
a movement that had not taken place when
the impression of the rear part of the figure
was snapped. The thing is therefore theo-
retically possible, and the camera should be
able to reproduce not petrified movement
but the impression of movement actually
given by the eye and reproduced for us by
a sculptor like Rodin.
Wanted—A Place Poem
What is a place poem? It is a poem of
your own 'city, of your own township, of
your own selection, of your own suburb,-of
your own backyard. This page, for once
becoming parochial, offers one guinea for
oq 6 place poem received by February
It need not be topographical—
we are not asking for guide-books—but
it must celebrate the charm of your own city,
or your own dairy farm. If you live in
?. r Ploma, or Burrumbuttock,
or Sodw alls, or Puggoon, or
JNU^ a ’ - 0r Meri- ywinebone, or Moombooldool,
or Mugincoble, your poem may or may not
use these euphonious names for its refrain
but at least it must so indicate Califat that
it cannot be mistaken for Woolloomooloo,
S r-m a s tr 0 that 11 calls up no picture of
S»t. Kilda. Here is a chance for you to in-
scnbe the name of some obscure township
in the imperishable book of literature. All
you need to do is to forward your place
Poe “ ia T> an , eavel ?, pe priced “Competition, ’
to the Bed Page” editor.
A.L.G.
Adam Lindsay Gordon, and His Friends
in England and Australia, by Edith Hurn-
flhris and Douglas Sladen (Constable, per
Dymock’s, Sydney. 12s. (Jd.), turns out to
be very like Douglas Sladen. The preface
begins with this amazingly Sladisli asser-
tion : “At this moment every Australian, is
subscribing for a national monument to
Cordon, the poet of the Australian Bush” ;
and the first chapter Slades Gordon as “the
Byron of Australia.” Into this big and
well-illustrated book the editors have put
all that they know about Gordon and most
of what they don't know, the result being a
quite readable and very full biography, in-
terspersed with literary judgments that are
negligible. There is a chapter telling how
Gordon rode in Australia, a table of descent
of the Gordon family is given, the scene of
“How We Beat the Favorite” is described;
there is an account —so widely flung has
been the biographers’ net —of “Gordon’s
Lively Great-Grandmother,” and various de-
tails of the poet are related by his friends
and relatives. Worth reading, but not for
its literary judgments.
But the best appreciation is that con-
tained in Frank Maldon Robb’s Poems of
Adam Lindsay Gordon, in the Oxford Edi-
tion of standard poets (2s.). This is, in-
deed, a worthy tribute to the best-known
Australian poet. Of course, the book in-
cludes too many of Gordon’s lesser verses,
and perpetuates the vapidness of “Ash-
taroth” by republication in full, despite the
editor’s judgment that: “The whole poem is
a mistake, and very crudely written.”
Frank Maldon Robb, a Melbourne barris-
ter, has done his work sincerely and well ;
his criticism never genufiexes into Sladish
worship; and in the end he explains the
high place still allotted to Gordon by Aus-
tralia w due rather to the love Australians
bear him than to their critical appreciation.
“We in Australia read Gordon; we do
not pedestal him into the highest, or even
into a very high place; we love him and his
verse; and love is given to a man and a
poet and a book, not: for the things that the
world knows and names, but for the ‘novel
lights and darks undreamed of,’ and for
‘that which the world’s coarse thumb and
finger failed to plumb.’ You cannot explain
or analyse or weigh or ticket such love,”
In his account of “The Making, of Gor-
don” Robb applauds the critical dictum that
“he owed more to Byron and Browning,
Tennyson and Swinburne, than to Aus-
tralia or to anything Australian,” and ex-
amines critically these sources of inspira-
tion. The reader will find much that is in
agreement with the recent “Red Page” analy-
sis of Gordon’s work —statements like these :
“Gordon has no reliable sense of rhythm—
not much of rime; one can never be sure,
even in his most careful, conscientious arid
ambitious work, that his ear and taste will
not play him false. He is 'apt to drop
straight from the stars into the puddles.
His exceedingly limited and narrow range of
metres ineaps a' correspondingly nayrow
range of thought and emotion, and techni-
cally his verse is full of unpardonable and
mortal weaknesses.” “To the influence of
Browning. Australia—which loves the jing-
ling rimes, the living pictures, the rough-
hewn language of Gordon, while its predilec-
tion for Browning- is, to say the least, some-
what masked—owes more than it thinks.”
In this respect Robb notes the fact that the
titles of Gordon’s poems are singularlv
Browningesque, that in Gordon’s language
the resemblance is still more noteworthy,
that the subjects on which they love best to.
write prove them still more akin, and that:
‘The real affinity between them lies much
deeper than either their common language or
their common choice of subjects. Gordon has
derived from Browning habits of thought,
tastes, tendencies, moral and spiritual out-
looks, which have left their indelible and re-
vealing marks on tfll his verse.”
But the one became an optimist, the
other a pessimist. “Had Gordon learnt but
one more lesson from the great Teacher at
whose feet he had so often sat. there would
have been, no suicifle at Brighton Beach : and
the whole tone of the immediately succeeding
generation of Australian poets might have
been different.”
But in the section dealing with Gordon’s
debt to Australia. Robb makes the best of
what is left of the derivative Gordon —of
what Australia, which “in 1853 was one
huge inspiration,” did for the plastic and
impressionable youth.
This edition, at its cheap two ‘shillings,
remains the best to date. At five shillings,
however, there is T. N. Foulis’ fine Poems
of A'dam Lindsay Gordon, without preface
or biography, and illustrated in color, from
paintings by Cantain G. D. Giles. This has
the advantage of excluding “Ashtaroth.”
«- —
Collins' Diaries, over 160 styles and sizes,
are as usual welcome, for they are packed
with Australasian information.
For the music-lover who likes his melody
garnished, there is the second series of Days
with Great Composers, dealing with Chopin,
Wagner and Gounod (Hodder and Stough-
ton, per Angus and Robertson), illustrated
in color.
Fiction
■'There was Fleurette. That wasn’t her
real name—it was Marie-Josephine; but
people called her Fleurette. She looked like
a llower, you know. The most delicate
little flower you can conceive. Tiens, she
was a slender lily—so white, .and her hair
the flash of gold on it —and she had eyes—
aes yeux de pervenche, as we say in French.
hat is pervenche in English—that little
pale-blue flower?”
“Periwinkle*” said I.
“Periwinkle eyes! My God ! what a lan-
guage ! Ah, no! She had dets yetix de
pervenche... .She was didphune. diaphanous
.. . .impalpable as cigarette-smoke.. .a little
nose like nothing at all, with nostrils like
infinitesimal sea-sliells. Anyone could have
made a mouthful of her....l’ve never told
you about Fleurette. It was this way.”
No need to tell the name of the author to
the ' novel - soaked subscription library
habitue ; it is .plainly William J. Locke on
the sea-coast of Bohemia, with another be-
loved vagabond adventuring among women’s
hearts. This time it is The Joyous Adven-
tures of Aristide Pujol (Lane, per Dymock,
Sydney) ; and with such a hero one need be
disappointed neither in the adventures nor,
in their joyousness. In these slight stories,'
evidently written for magazine consumption,
Locke, whose workings seemed to 1 wheeze
with overwork in Clementina Winy, has re-
gained his old careless gaiety. There are
delightful touches in Locke’s old manner,
and his style is still a thing of charm.
The light handling, the tender pathos, the
bubbling humor makes this slight thing worth
more than Locke’s later more serious novels.
The Keynote, translated under this title
from Monsieur dcs Sourdines, by Alphonse
de Chateaubriaut (Hodder and Stoughton),
comes to us garlanded with the laurels of
the French literature, for to this author the
annual Goneourt prize was awarded. The
novel has a stylistic resemblance to Marie
Claire, a novel similarly acclaimed. As the
translator says: “Its merit lies, not in live-
liness of plot, wealth of incident, or pander-
ing to the elemental passions, but in the
extraordinary delicacy of its appeal to the
best and purest side of human nature.” It
is the plain story of the ruin that falls upon
a rich recluse by his decision to pay off the
accumulated debts of his spendthrift son,
rather than let that useless youth -go to
prison, and of the final reconciliation be-
tween father and repentant son] Beauti-
fully written, no doubt, but desperately dull.
The English reader cannot live on style
alone. These painstaking descriptions of
country life are as innocuous as butter-
scotch, and leave as lasting a memory.

More and more artistic grow the modern
kid s picture books. Three sumptuous works
stand out among this year’s gift books for
the good babies of rich but honest parents—
Bene Bull’s fantastic illustrated edition of
Ihe Arabian Nights (Constable, 10s. 6d.J,
Bill the Minder, a modern '“Arabian
Nights, ’ written and richly illustrated in
color and line, by, W. Heath Robinson
(Constable, 10s. 6d.), ahd The Biq Booh of
Fames, illustrated and decorated by Charles
Robinson (Blackie, 7s. Gd.). Each is a
delight-—but one wonders' whether a kid
really appreciates the line and composition,
the management of the blacks, the subtle
coloring and the gracious humor of these
lordly illustrations. Probably more to the
liking of the child is the cruder and fiercer
color found in such works as Blackie’s Chil-
drens Annual, or Ward' Lock’s Wonder
Booh.
The death is announced of Homer Lea.
from Los Angeles, U.S.A., author of those
remarkable prophecies, The Valor of Ignor-
ance and Ihe Bay of the Saxon. I-Ie was
only do. He was born at Denver, and re-
ceived Ins education at Western American
universities. Ilis military career began in
China 12 years ago, where he organised and
commanded four divisions which undertook
the rescue of the Emperor Ivwang Su Sub-
sequently he became Chief of the Staff of
feun Yat Sen s revolutionary armv. The
Valor of Ignorance was translated into
Japanese, and ran through 24 editions in
one month It is hot stated how many edi-
tions I her Day of the Saxon hurtled—or will
hurtle-into in Japan. A great force extin-
guished.

in the stimulating Selected Passages from the
Works of Bernard Shaw, chosen bv Char-
lotte F. Shaw (Constable, per Dymock’s

Rodins bust of the long-headed Irishman.
Here you have the absolutely right thing

everything from melodrama to motherhood
from the superman to the stage villain
Shaw s correspondence, his unpublished
plays and the squibs with which he so fre-
quently annoys the daily press have been
drawn upon for this delightful selection.

The Inky Way.
“Chuddah”: The London Times exults over No.
40,000. There is a kind of subterranean apology, an
undercurrent in the exultation— a sort of “You
know, it was impossible for us to remain as great
as we used to be.” Yet the Times ought to have
done so. Some trace the downfall to the Parnell-
Pigott case, in which Asquith developed as junior to
Russell; but that wasn’t it. What wrought the
downfall was the bribing of Sampson, the financial
editor. That broke the backbone, and got at the
spinal marrow. Sampson’s sister married Gabrielli,
the financier, and probably that helped Samnsoh to
fall into the hands of the Philistines. This Gab-
riclli visited Melbourne, by the way, having nego-
tiated a big loan in London for the Melbourne cor-
poration, which found itself stuck up for money in the
rapid development of population after the gold-
fields rush.
“Inkywaryun”: What is “Downward L ” (B
21 111 / 12) driving at / He starts by abusing Pitman-
ltes and sneering at our system, and ends by com-
mending it! Of course, all Pitmanites are bigoted,
as all devotees of anything that is best ought to be
f believe I am the oldest Pitmanite in Vic., having
got my certificate from old Izak in 1875. I have
tried some of the other shorthand systems, but still
use Pitman daily, because I find it to be the clearest
and the easiest to read. Plenty of stenographers
can write other systems more quicklv than I can use
Pitman; but they can’t read what thev have written
as quickly, and, in fact, sometimes they can’t read
it at all. Besides, for one shorthander who writes
any other svstem, there are 100 using old Izak's
phonetics. And, finally, with Pitman’s, tuition is not
necessarv. I taught mvself in 18 months with the
aid of the three original books. This par. is not meant
as the gift of a bunch of flowers to myself for clever-
ness, but is merely an illustration "of how really
scientific and accurate, and, therefore, how easily
acquired, used and remembered the old admirable
system is.
Another little stranger blew on to the Inky Way
the other morning—'Tiir Australian Journalist,
official organ of the A.J.A. It explains that it is
expected “to promoie tho.-e feelings of comradeship,
mutual interest and mutual reliance without which
it is almost impossible for such an association to
satisfactorily progress." It will breathe this into
tlie ears of the widely-scattered men who are buildiii"
up a national journalism for Australia, and generally
act as press agent of the hitherto unboomed news-
paper scribe. Subscribers to the Journalist can rest
assured that nowhere upon its pages will there appear
photographs of unmusical-comedy girls having earlv
tea (or maybe, beer) in bed. ' For this alone it
merits the respect of a long-suffering public.
* * * *
“Scribe”: With a long experience of the Inky
Way, I should describe the late Edward Ellis, who
has died in poverty in Melbourne Hospital (B.
5/12/’12), as the most truly unlucky member of the
profession. Winter, the old-time Czar of the local
Herald, said Ellis was the best journalist he had
employed. I thought he would have said it of
Walstab. In my view Walstab was a niche (which
does not imply much) above Ellis, who could only
surpass him in range. Of the two Walstab was the
brilliant, dashing Beau Sabreur, while Ellis had a
valuable London experience to start with, and, in
his day, that gave him some advantage over Austra-
lians. But nothing ever went quite right with him
for long, and. knowing him well, I can’t put it
down to anything but sheer ill-luck.
* * * *
“G.V.” : P. C. Freeth will, it is said, shortly wind
up his second term as editor of the Times, Welling-
ton (M.L.), and will thereafter earn a crust manag-
ing the Daily Times at Taihape—a main trunk
village, with glorious memories of malt and hops,
two-up and draw-poker. The Times is an uneasy
paper; in these latter days a large amount of its
energy and space is taken up in running advertising
competitions.
INK OR WATER?
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
There's a dissipated rhyth-m in the rattle ot the rain
(Of course no sober rhythm could be quite dis-
syllable) ;
As I sit and suffer silently, it’s washing down the
pane,
From a glass that seems inordinately spillable.
Oh, my rhymes are slopping over, as they always
seem to do,
For the wind is in the trees, and won’t be quiet:
And the song they will be singing
In my silly head is ringing,
And the sense from the rhyme is running riot;
Or perhaps it’s running through,
As the wind and water do,
Through the sylvan pipes of Pan or hamadryad.'
There are songs that sing themselves '
When the sibilating elves
Rustle round and make a noise among the herbage.
You may try to make them fit,
But you'll find it isn’t it,
When you’ve got them scribbled down in scrambled
verb’age.
You may listen how you will,
While their thrummings through you thrill,
Bike -Bolus a-hymning through a grater;
But, endeavor to translate
Them to words, you’ll find that fate
Reaches bottom, by translating the translater.
You will hem and ha and hum,
Strike a string, and find it dumb,
Beat your brain-box like a gong, and make a mortar
Of the thing you call your soul;
Then you’ll watch the rain-drops roll,
And conclude you'd better write your ode in water.
WAIF.
AN OPEN LETTER.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Dear Constant Reader, —Glad I am
To know you’re still a-reading ;
And still, I hope, the family pram
Holds infants gaily feeding;
Your worthy lady, Ma of Ten,
May all good luck delight her 1
You’ve helped me long, you best of men,
For I am Constant Writer.
No squeamish taste is yours, dear Con. ;
The pen may not be flowing,
But page on page you look upon,
And evermore keep going;
Yciu read the best, you read the worst,
You read the in-between things,
And at the last as at the first
You seem to think they mean things.
And if one tires, a little time,
Nor scales the high intention,
You still reward the prose or rhyme
With kindly look and mention ;
For niggling blame you do not stay,
Nor show pretended sorrow:
You know we did well yesterday.
And hope we shall to-morrow.
So, Constant Reader, you’re the man
I like to have a-reading;
For ever may your worthy clan
A peaceful life be leading ;
Of flowering or of budding pen
You’ve never been a blighter,
So flourish on, you best of men.
—Yours truly, Constant Writer.
N.B.W.
NORMAN LILLEY.

VARIOUS VERSE
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
An Island Statue.
Out there on Gabo, rock-ribbed and severe,
’Mid rush and tumult of opposing seas,
His noble image be it ours to rear
There on yon outpost isle that knows no
trees;
There at the Cape, “the parting of the
ways.”
Within his ken all ships that, toiling,
pass, .
Bound East or West, till merged in mist and
haze,
Or lost in vapors ’neath a sky of brass.
Ships from the North and West, whose
prows of steel .
At his firm-planted feet shall rise and dip
In salutation, ere they turn to wheel
Or West or North in passing, ship by ship.
There, at the wide-flung portal of the
Straits, . , ,
Whose crested rollers, hoarse with loud
acclaim
In sea-born thunder, rushing to their fates.
Pronounce to sea and shore his honored
name.
More fitting pedestal, O lonely isle.
Than thou there is not in Australian seas.
More meet than is for Memnon on the Nile,
The rock whereon he sits, with hands on
knees.
Here, from thy crest, the hero shall explore
Scenes of rude labor passed, of battles
fought
At odds, with wind and wave, ’tween shore
and shore,
Of days and darkness with, adventure
fraught.
’Tis his alone, O grey and surge-swept isle,
To crown thy height; and all the ships
that pass,
Or by thy lantern’s light or Day-dawn s
smile,
Breasting the wave, shall bless the ardent
Bass.
Bass, who stands sentinel beside the Gates
He found and forced; as, riding on the
tide, ,
He gave the world the highway of the
Straits, . .
And, radiant, flung those azure portals
wide.
On Gabo be it, then, our bronze to rear.
Lesseps by the Canal has pride of place,
Scanning the work that makes his title
clear.
But Bass on Gabo wears another grace.
VICTOBIA.
G. G. McC.
Rest.
Give me a book from which I may distil a
little rest *
A book of grave smooth-flowing prose I think
would be the best;
And if it deals with ancient things forgot
and put away, , , „
Then I will treasure it the more and gladly
for it pay.
This is a fevered age wherein good rest is
hard to find; ,
Unrestful people rush before and shove like
fiends behind;
And ev’ry job makes work. It seems as if
we’d all conspired
To toil and wear ourselves right out at
making people tired.
Who makes or manufactures rest? Who
sells the only thing
That people ever really want? I hear a
shop-bell ring,
And some sleep-thickened voice cry “Shop !”
with one reluctant breath.
The coin rings on a coffin lid ! The Voice
again : “Shop, Death !”
N.S.W.
E. FRANCIS.
The Feather.
Barby came down with a brown floating
feather
In her dark hair.
Under the trees in the sweet-scented weather
Life was most fair. Barby came there.
Western winds, laden with wattle-sweet,
stooped her
Tresses to touch.
(Who would suppose that a shining brown
feather mattered so much?)
Roaming alone in the grey winter weather,
Where the winds fret,
In the sere branches I found a worn feather,
Broken and wet. (Could I forget?)
Cold o’ the wind, will ye ask the storm
whether
It knew the fine touch?
(Who would suppose that a wet, broken
feather mattered.... so much ?)
VICTORIA.
PATRICIA MURCHISON.
Earth.
Green grows my grave in the grass,
Somewhere. ... ? Oh, let it be
Here in the land that I love,
My heart’s own Italy.
The bee will hum to the bud,
And the bud will whisper to me 1
Of the dawn and the dew and the flood
And the season’s mystery.
The song of the brook through the stones,
The song of the thrush through the tree,
Will mingle and marry and hush
With the music of moonlight and sea.
And mad with their musical chant
I know that my heaven will be
To go through the wild olden wood
Of earth-sweet memory.
N.S.W.
HUGH McCRAE.

TERMS TO SUBSCRIBERS.
(In Advance, Only.)
Per Annum .. . . ..£l6O

Quarterly .. . . .. 0 7 0
By sending direct to The Bulletin Office Twenty-
Six Shillings sterling you can have The Bulletin
posted regularly to you every week for a year.
Cheques, Drafts, etc., which should be crossed, and
all Business Communications to be addressed to The
Manager.
How to Address Letters.
Correspondents to The Bulletin would oblige the
paper (and, to an extent, themselves) by taking
some care as to methods of address.
Business letters (with regard to subscriptions, ad-
vertisements, etc.) should in no case be addressed to
the Editor, but to the Manager. To send them to
the Editor is to invite inattention.
It is not a good thing to address editorial letters
to anyone by name. “The Editor, The Bulletin/' is
the proper superscription for “copy.” Delay, and
even loss, is sometimes caused by addressing to ver-
sons instead of officials. The person may not be
there; the official always is.
Special Notice. .
Insufficiently stamped letters are liable to be re-
fused.
The contents of this paper are copyright by the
publishers. The right to reproduce or translate any
part of the contents of this number is hereby ex-
pressly reserved by the publishers.
The Editor will carefully read and, where prac-
ticable, acknowledge in the “Correspondence” col-
umn all contributions submitted, whether in the
form of Political, Social or other Articles, Verse,
Short Tales or Sketches (those dealing with Aus-
tralian subjects, and not exceeding two columns in
length, or, say, 3000 words, are specially accept-
able), Paragraphs, Letters or Newspaper Clippings.
All communications will be regarded as strictly confi-
dential.
The Bulletin will return all unsuitable MS.
when the address is specified and stamps are en-
closed. But no MS. will be returned, or preserved
unless these conditions are observed. When MS. is
forwarded without stamps for return it will be un-
derstood that the copy is to be destroyed if not ac-
ceptable. Personal applications for return of MS.
cannot receive attention. The Bulletin desires,
further, those sending MS. to distinctly understand
that it is not responsible for the preservation or
transmission of MS.
To Amateurs in Black and White.
Anyone supplying to The Bulletin original
humorous or other topical drawings in line or wash,
of sufficient merit and interest and adapted for
direct reproduction, or any interesting photograph
illustrative of a current event, will be paid for same
on acceptance. Any picture containing a good idea,
but unfitted for direct reproduction — i.e., any pic-
ture which has to be re-drawn —will be paid for as a
suggestion only. The conditions under which such
artistic contributions will be received are those
applying to MS,

THURSDAY, DECEMBER 26, 1912.
COMPULSORY TRAINING, AND THE
FOOLS AND TRAITORS WHO ARE
HAMPERING IT.
Last week the news came by cable that
Keik Haedie, the posturer who makes a
speciality of advocating every lost, not to
say demented, cause that is going, had
started a fund in the United Kingdom to
fight the compulsory clauses of the Austra-
lian Defence Act —just as though some Cen-
tral African savage had inaugurated a move-
ment in his tribe to abolish early closing in
Albury or Bourke. It was stated, in the
same message, that a Keir Haedie emis-
sary was already at work in the Common-
wealth addressing meetings and organising
societies in opposition to the Act. The im-
pertinences of these outsiders are not likely
in themselves to injure the Defence move-
ment to any great extent. Unfortunately,
however, their uncalled-for efforts are
warmly aided by the local Peace Society
enthusiasts, in whose ranks are to be found
not merely the local anarchists, but num-
bers of normally law-abiding and rational
citizens. For example, the Rev. Professor
Macintyre, of Sydney, spread himself, the
other day, on the question, in a manner that
doubtless caused the wild-eyed revolution-
aries who regard Patriotism' as an offence
against democracy, to leap with joy. After
denouncing every form of military service
as immoral and opposed to the tenets of
Christianity and brotherly feeling, he hedged
to this extent: —
If Australia was gratuitously attacked, he did not
suppose any member of the society would consider
himself outside his rights in defending it.
It is a point of view that has been taken bv
two classes of undesirables —viz., fools and
traitors—since the dawn of history.
The pacificist who is not a congenital vil-
lain, who would hate to see his country
seized by a foreigner amidst scenes of carn-
age and rapine, and who is sufficiently
proud of it to recoil at the thought of it
being trailed in the gory mud of defeat be-
fore the eyes of the world, buttresses his
case by two forms of special pleading, both
of them glaringly fallacious enough to arouse
the scorn of an intelligent aborigine. The
first is to the effect that if Australia, or auy
other nation, refrains from annoying its
neighbors by a display of militarism, and
remains defenceless and civil and obliging,
the latter will be so edified that they will
not harm it. Despite the recent horrible
example of the Koreans (about the most
humble, inoffensive and ill-armed people on
record) and of a dozen others that might
be cited, these queer logicians continue to
claim that the one requisite for national
safety is the absence of an army and a navy.
Deny yourself the fierce delight of paying
for battleships and the maintenance of
troops, they say in effect, and your banks
and shops and businesses will be safe for
all time. Remain weak and no one will
ever think of forcibly settling on your fer-
tile lands or violently occupying your vast
coastal cities. By the simple and inexpen-
sive process of basking unobtrusively in the
sun you will avoid the fate of Assyria,
Rome, Byzantium, Carthage, Babylon,
Macedonia, and all the other Empires whose
remains are strewn along the path of Time.
This type of controversialist is fully cap-
able of propounding the theory that the
Aztec Empire might have been saved by a
policy of friendly inertia. Let it be men-
tioned to him that accidents and rows and
occasionally violent hatreds occur in the best
regulated families ; that it is the unexpected
which happens, more especially in interna-
tional affairs; that, in fact, war is always
a possibility even where it isn’t a pro-
bability, and he so far yields, like Pro-
fessor Macintyre did a few days ago, as to
use the second string to the Peace bow. It
amounts to this. Assuming the ravening
foe has arrived and is shelling the cities
and _ towns; laying waste the landscape:
blowing up bridges; “commandeering”
clothing, provisions and so on; and in
general raising particular Sheol, in accord-
ance with the time-honored traditions of
warfare, then and not till then is it permis-
sible for the convinced anti-Defencite to rise
up in pained surprise and do something.
Having no arms, nor any knowledge of how
to use them if he had, it is difficult to see
just how he would contrive to beat off the
enemy. The deadliest weapon in the armory
of a clerical member of a Peace Society
would probably be his umbrella. Yet, as a
means of warding off a bayonet thrust,
directed by a trained hand, it is safe to lay
it down that an umbrella would be the rot-
tenest of failures.
* * * *
The International Socialist, or Industrial
Worker of the World—-he who believes in
the brotherhood of man, and communism,
and the abrogation of all laws, and the effi-
cacy of red ties and whiskers and dirt and
blither—sees eye to eye with the Peace
Society crank, as regards the merits of anti-
defence, in every respect but one. He does
not believe in so much as shaking a hostile
umbrella at the invader. His attitude is
that everything should belong to everybody,
more particularly to the Industrial Workers
of the World. So lons as it doesn’t, he is
indifferent whether he is governed by Japs,
Chinese or Australians. Accordingly, if
any foreigner, white, black or brown, fancies
the Commonwealth, he is in favor of handing
it over without protest or other unpleasant-
ness. Pride of race—the sentiment which
causes white men to kick violently at the
idea of being dominated by Asiatics —is no
part of his philosophy of life. Nor is regard
for the honor of his women kind. Appar-
ently he is even unable to see that existence
would be considerably harsher for him per-
sonally if the Commonwealth were occupied
by (say) Japanese. He is, of course, as
averse to military training as Professor
Macintyre, or any of his associates; and
day by day and year by year, he consistently
does his little best to hamper the Defence
movement. So far, he has been a somewhat
futile obstructionist, owing in part to his
scarcity in point of numbers, in part to the
wild unreasonableness of his attitude. But
since it is not in human nature for a people
which has never had its nerves shaken by
personal experience of warfare, to rejoice at
the hard, dull work that is bound up with
drill and so on ; and since, furthermore, taxa-
tion (and more especially Defence taxation,
which yields no visible return in the way
of roads, bridges, etc) is necessarily irk-
some to the multitude, many thousands con-
ceive a sneaking affection for his tenets. On
the extremely ancient principle that men be-
lieve most readily what they want to believe,
there is a fairly widespread inclination to
support the policy of the International So-
cialists with the arguments of the_ dreamy
individuals who drift into Peace Societies.
Thus it is that persons who are ordinarily
patriotic, and who, in any case, have every-
thing to lose by a successful foreign invasion,
are to be found everywhere contending that
perhaps, after all. the Defence business is
being overdone. They point out that trouble
is unlikely; that the Defence bill of a
modern nation is a hideously large affair;
and that, in any case, Australians are good
natural fighters, and so can dispense with
many of the war preliminaries that may be
essential in the case of their inferiors.
Summed up, these folks don’t advocate no
Defence, so much as less Defence, and in
this respect, for lack of a more definite
pronouncement, they have all the irreconcil-
ables with them.
It will be a bleak day for Australia if the
combined efforts of its traitors, fools and
loafers succeed to the extent of putting any-
thing like a serious check on its perilously
belated Defence movement. Russia lost
Manchuria, Port Arthur, the Kwanglung
Peninsula, and half of Saghalien, seven years
ago, and was forced to recognise Japan’s
sphere of influence in Korea, not because
it was poor, not because its troops lacked
the courage they have always shown, but
because its preparations for war were as
casual as its yellow enemy’s were careful
and complete. Events had been inevitably
leading up to the Russo-Jap trouble ever
since the Saghalien incident of 1875. Dur-
ing over 30 years the Japanese army in-
creased steadily in size and efficiency. In
the same period a navy of a high order came
into being. Meanwhile Russia drifted. In
1903 the Czar’s vast Empire was hopelessly
unprepared for a conflict of any importance.
By all the rules of the game, as laid down
by the Peace Society people, Japan should
have held off and done nothing in face of
such monumental inoffensiveness. In point
of fact, it eagerly seized this particular mo-
ment to get busy. There is no need to
recapitulate what happened. Russian naval
officers of all ranks knew what was in store
for them from the first. The Port Arthur
squadron was forbidden to put to sea by
Admiral Alexeiev at the very beginning of
the war, and Semenov mentions that in the
Vladivostock Squadron all but the most be-
sottedly ignorant appreciated that their first
encounter with Kamimuka’s cruisers meant
certain death. Russia, as a result of its
partial reliance on Peace Society principles,
may be said to have been beaten before the
fighting began. Spain lost practically the
last of its colonial possessions the other day,
owing to the same cause. The effete old
nation had battleships enough, and its sailors
were brave, but the former were out of date
and the latter were untrained, and carnage
and overthrow followed automatically. If
there is one respect in which history repeats
itself with more deadly monotony than an-
other, it is in the matter of the fate of the
unprepared nations. Not one of them but
has had to face its Calvary. The Turkish
soldiers are going through the immemorial
agony at the present moment, because for a
decade or more it has been no one’s business
in particular to train and clothe them, to
see that proper commissariat and hospital
arrangements were made, and to nrovide
them with modern weapons of precision. A
German officer with the Turkish army pays
a tribute to the daring of the rank and file in
the London Daily News (9/11/M2). But
he points out that the Ottoman troops never
had a hope of success. One reason was “the
overwhelming superiority of the Bulgarian
guns to those used by the Turks.” The
highly modern Creusot weapons used by the
Bulgars were irresistible. _ _ Also, the Turks
were starving, the ammunition was defective,
and their artillery was badly served, so much
so that “many of the guns came out of
action with, the covers still on, and as clean
as they went in.” It is a grim tale of laissez-
faire and resultant inefficiency, leading up
to a colossal tragedy—-the sort of tale the t
is always told of a nation when it slackens
up in the race of armaments. Just as
in the ordinary walks of life, so in the
great gamble of war, those who are to
have a sporting chance must be schooled
and organised to the last unit. Half-
measures are well-nigh as disastrous as none
at all. The officers must know their jobs
as familiarly as competent barristers know
the procedure of the Courts; the men must
be so disciplined by ceaseless handling that
they will be ready to attempt the forlornest
of hopes; and there must be efficiency every-
where, from boots to battleships.
THE WHEEL AND THE HOLIDAY.
Men of science have conjectured of the sun
that in addition to revolving in the midst of
its flaming whiskers, it is travelling some-
where on its own account, dragging its
planets, comets, asteroids, moons and other
encumbrances to some possible goal utterly
beyond the limit of research or observation.
It is just possible that the world of trams,
breakfasts, oysters, smiles, beer, newspapers
and policemen is doing the same thing, and
that eventually it will really get somewhere
and afford politicians an opportunity of
congratulating everybody on the success of
evolution. On the face of things, however,
this doesn’t seem very probable. The ear-
liest artists of Egypt sketched things much
as Nokman Lindsay does, the last king of
Babylon devoted his life to reading and
translating the inscriptions on ancient tombs
and monuments, the lake dwellers of 15,000
b.c. had skulls the shape of our own, and
doubtless persons in Memphis and Tyre had
to run for the boat and pay cab-hire just as
we do to-day. As a plain fact, Babylon was
a finer city than New York, Alexandria
only lacked a picturesque harbor to make it
better than Sydney as a place of residence,
and, at its best, Thebes, with its 30 miles
of granite temples and palaces, would have
made London look like a cluster of tempor-
ary humpies. Yet the wheel of the world
is turning steadily, and our splendid modern
achievements fill us with a hope that in a
century or two we may come within range
of some of the heights attained in ages that
are gone. This may be good business or the
reverse. Happiness may lie at the bottom
of the revolution or at the top ; but our own
view of the wheel’s rotation is apt to be a
sordid and personal one. The hunt for com-
mon, necessary bread-and-butter goes in a
circle; and the ancient law which lays it
down that you can’t have your cake and eat
it too keeps a considerable number of us
steadily on the run. No sooner have we got
the loaf of our desire than the urgent need
of the pat of butter forces itself upon us,
and when by speed and dexterity we lay
hands on that also, we are suddenly made
aware that the baker’s product is already
demolished and that another bun or roll must
be snared on which to spread the otherwise
useless product of the dairy. So the business
goes on and on, with monotonous regularity,
until the thing gets upon our nerves and we
want to break away, or smash each separate
one of the Ten Commandments, or run amok
across the crowded avenues of civilisation,
not because we really wish to harm or de-
stroy anything, but simply because our
nerves are out of order.
Very large numbers of people, particu-
larly in this favored Australia, never suffer
in this way; but when they do, doc-
tors usually recommend change. Instead of
adding up figures, or trying to sell houses
or float mines or write poems or wash clothes
or burgle houses, they advise us to get away
into the country and live like the sheep or
the tomato until we are sufficiently braced
up to go back to our place on the wheel. If
we can follow the advice in a regular sort
of way, so much the better. Week-ends m
the bush and the like may be part of the
wheel; but as long as we don’t notice that
they are, the benefit is just the same. The
slow, brutal, spider-like pastime of fishing
is useful for degraded persons in whom the
primal savage instincts have been merely
held in check by the outward forces of re-
ligion. Shooting live creatures for the mere
joy of base and treacherous murder may
help others to get back their lost power to
endure their normal fate. To persons of
higher ethical stature and nobler moral
growth, it is enough to sit with Cissy under
a gum tree while the breezes play hide-and-
seek in the errant tresses that have been
kissed into living gold by peroxide, and the
red lips assist the sweetly feminine system
to absorb delicacies in the midst of the sun-
glad wilderness. According to Byron,
“Each beast has his nature —a king’s is to
reign.” There is something kingly about
the splendid sloth that can bask in sunshine
and idleness and be happy about it. But
whatever form the relaxation may take, the
great thing is to get away from the wheel,
or what is just as good, seem to get away
from it. This is the philosophy of holidays.
Human evolution, ground out on the wheel
of the Potter, or the Boss Diamond Cutter,
or whoever it is that shapes our ends, runs
so close; to the thing men call insanity that
a break is absolutely necessary, and life —
the real life that counts to the individual—
is the part of existence spent outside the in-
fluence of the everlasting revolutions.
Australia is, above all places on this
planet, the land of the holiday. One of its
most popular bards has said that “it is the
land o’ lots o’ time along the Castlereagh.”
The lots are equally numerous in other
districts. At Christmas time other peoples
try to shake themselves loose from their
shackles, and have what they regard as a
good time. Australia has a good time, or it
is its own fault if it doesn’t. The sun does
its best; bush, sea and sky join in the
glad conspiracy; and if the human element
in the proposition doesn’t come up to the
scratch, it is entirely its own fault. Europe
crouches round a big fire and eats heavy
substances difficult of digestion, until a
weighty merriment is produced which it has
the courage to label joy. Australia can
beat that sort of thing out of sight, and
there is a new note of gaiety in its laughter,
clean and unfettered as the surf rolling in.
or the brown limbs of the nymphs that tumble
in the breakers.
And it is just there that the troubles of
this big island mostly come in. It is so
good a place for getting glad that, with a
huge section of the people, life is all tangent
and no circle. The sun is so bright, and
the tangles of hair so numerous
that men find it difficult to take the old
wheel of life seriously. That it must go
round and that somebody must supply the
motive power is evident, but the weather is
altogether too pleasant to seriously tackle the
question as to who should get busy about
the job. When bread-and-butter is urgently
needed, and the quarry is in sight, someone
will get up and chase it to its lair. In fact,
most people will take at least a lansruid
interest in the hunt. But the pursuit is
mainly urged with a view to more holidays
when the immediate necessity is past, or to
getting rid of the animal once for all in a
grand battue which may make it possible
for the retired hunter to sit in the sun, or
the motor-car, or the corner of the pub, as
happy and idle as a pagan deity or a Chris-
tian cauliflower. This is the chief reason
why things are not done. Our politics and
big national concerns conflict with the holi-
day idea, and can only get forward in a
holiday spirit. There is the matter of a
White Australia, of an army and fleet to
defend it, of straight railways of uniform
gauge to make that defence possible, of popu-
lating the rich country that lies most open
to occupation by a possible enemy, of secur-
ing a better distribution of population in the
more closely settled States, by opening up
new ports, of limiting the possibilities of
commercial plunder, of safeguarding indus-
tries and those engaged in them by increased
tariff duties, and of lightening and improv-
ing the machinery of government by the abo-
lition of Upper Houses, State Governors and
other non-ornamental encumbrances. All
these matters have points about them that
are urgent and want attending to without
delay. Even the national picnic cannot well
be maintained if they are longer neglected.
But the sun is warm ; the gum leaves turn-
ing up to let the light fall through are en-
ticing ; and—let the confounded things
wait. The Roman Saturnalia was properly
of two to five days’ duration : Roman prac-
tice made it last for seven. The Australian
political Christmas holiday may officially
last for a week or six weeks : in practice it
seems to extend over 52. Who cares ? As
long as there is beer for John Smith and
ice-cream for his daughter Cissy, and a
sunny place to get warm and a shady place
to get cool, why worry about the fact that
the machine of politics seems wobbling to a
stand-still? Some enthusiastic Asiatic may
yet make the old wheel spin again, and John
and Ctssy may be underneath! But such
a possibility is quite outside the realm of
the practical. It can’t possibly hapnen before
next week! So, again, in the language of
the man at the “What’s the
hurry ?’
A ROYAL COMMISSION.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Sit you down, discerning brother, let us un~
derstand each other.
And push forward with a little scheme
apace.
It concerns our Constitution and the “threat-
ened revolution,”
As the heated Tory quaintly puts the case.
It concerns these referenda and how far we
ought to go.
And the very vital question: Are we voting
Yes or No?
You are sick, as I am, brother, and indeea
full many other
Men of sense and perspicacity (like us)—
Sick of ev’ry quin and quibble, and the parti
cries that dribble


From the politicians; sick of all the fuss,
Of debates that lead to nowhere, and the
howls of mock distress,
And the most transparent blather of a very
biassed press.
Wherefore, friend, with your permission, let
us form a small commission.
Just a very small and quite select affair.
You and I alone comprise it; if no others
recognise it
We can let it go at that. Why should we
care?
'Tis a matter that concerns, in brief, the
votes of you and me;
Bo we'll waste no time in fretting if some
others don't agree.
We’ll assume, as time is flitting, our com-
mission now is sitting.
It comprises two plain men of common
sense;
And, though others may ignore us, we shall
iveigh the facts before us
And report correctly on the evidence.
Our first witness is the man of whom the
daily papers bleat —
He's the “average elector" or the “person in
the street."

man, like many other
Fellows of his type, is in a perfect maze —
What with politicians’ capers and the
wrangling of the papers,
This way and that he oscillates and sways.
He doesn’t know which way he'll vote,
though vote he feels he must.

To compel replies more definite than his.

But we’ll weigh his evidence just as it is.

“You represent the Democratic Spirit?”
“I do.”
“Do you favor the extension of national
power ?”
“Why should I not? Is it undemocratic
to trust the people?”
“The Federal legislators might misuse
these powers?”
“They are controlled by the people.”
“But why rob the States of their rights?”
“The States possess no rights that the
people do not possess.”
“Or privileges?”
“It is a section —a small section —of the
people who are privileged through the ex-
istence of Houses of Property.”
“The Legislative Councils?”
“Yes.”
“But are not these controlled by the
people?”
“By only a privileged section.”
“Then the extension of Federal power
would help to break the power of privilege?”
“Undoubtedly. It would be taking the
power away from the privileged and placing
it with the people.” .
“One moment, though. Are not Legisla-
tive Councillors wiser and freer from pre-
judice than the people’s representatives?”
“You will have your little joke!”
“The witness may retire.”
Now, good friend, a light is breaking while
this evidence we're taking,
For this witness does not hesitate or
hedge.
Judging by his answers solely, it appears
the issue wholly
Rests between the People's Rights and
Privilege.
But let us hear the other side and ponder
that reply.
The Honorable Toryphat will forthwith
testify.

“You represent Wealth, Privilege and the
Reactionary Spirit?”
“I have that honor.”
“But not necessarily Intellect or Wis-
dom ?”
“Pardon me. To become wealthy one
must necessarily be intelligent; and the man
who has made a success of his own business
is best fitted to look after the business of his
country.”
“To the detriment of his own business?”
“I don’t see what that has to do with it.”
“You do not believe in any extension of
Federal powers?”
“Most emphatically no! The Labor party
with these powers”
“Then you think the Labor party will re-
tain office?”
“Certainly not! Our party has every
chance of beating them next elections.”
“Then why object to putting these powers
into the hands of your own party?”
“While there is the remotest chance of
Labor ever getting back again, it is unsafe.”
“Then you would extend the power
of the Federal Parliament?”
‘'Never! It is unsafe.”
“Each of the States has a ‘safe’ House?”
“Thank Heaven!”
'“What do you mean by a ‘safe’ House?”
“I mean a House that will always vote as
we wish it to, and will not brook any undue
interference with Private Enterprise and
the rights of the better classes.”
“So, when you speak of States Rights
you really mean the rights of the Legislative
Councils —the right of Capital to veto legis-
lation ?”
“Put it that way if you like.”
“And the question fines itself down to
this: Federal rights are the unqualified
rights of the people; State rights are also
the rights of the people—the same people—
qualified by the privileges of the Legislative
Councils; and the forthcoming fight will
really mean, the Legislative Councils versus
the people?”
“Perhaps. But the people will never per-
ceive the real issue."
“The people are not fools?”
“My dear sir, the people are fools; and
there are ways of persuading them. I have
said there are some smart men in our party.
Also, there are the newspapers.”
“You mean to cloud the issue?”
“Why not? Our cause is just.”
“The witness may stand down.”
Report :
On the evidence before it —though some part
was apt to bore it —
This Commission now reports to those
concerned:
It has come to the conclusion that State
Rights are an illusion;
And amid the crowd there's much to be
unlearned.
That some may disagree with it 'tis ready
to confess.
As for itself, its vote will be a very solid
“Yes."
VICTORIA.
DEN.
ENGLISH.
The Failure of the Party Machine.
After a reasonable trial in N. S. Wales,
organised Labor has not succeeded in carry-
ing the measures set forth on its platform,
has not succeeded in making good in the
eyes of the general public, and has not
broken the power of the forces arrayed
against it. On the contrary, its represen-
tatives in Parliament have conducted the
affairs of the State very much after the
fashion of other party representatives who
went before them, and the community does
not appear to have reaped any greater bene-
fit from Labor rule than from that of the
old extravagant placemen and private-in-
terest servers whom it supplanted. As a
plain fact, Labor and Liberalism have been
playing the ancient game of Tweedledum
and Tweedledee, and to place Tweedledee
in office and keep him there is a precious
poor achievement to show as the sole result
of Labor’s close-knit and iron-bound organ-
isation. The weakness of the Labor method
lies in its neglect of the prime factor in all
great movements. It ignores the import-
ance of the individual. Somewhere about
the middle of last century Tennyson de-
clared that “the individual withers,” while
Carlyle shouted at the top of his large
Scottish voice that the individual was about
the only thing that counted or ever could
count. Up to the present Carlyle’s
opinion is still making good, and all the
reformers who have been chasing the poet’s
notion are forever bashing their heads
against that awkward proposition which
will get in the road —the man with an in-
dividual opinion. He may happen to be a
big man of wide view, or a small man with
a narrow outlook and a large enthusiasm,
or he may be just an ordinary man with a
special endowment of pigheadedness or
ignorance, or of that pignorance which is a
combination of both; but, in any case, the
result to the close, iron-bound organisation
is the same. In N.S.W. Beeby has gone
and others may follow. The whole team is
restive under the party lash, and no one can
think of anything else but the lash to fit
the occasion. The history of the party in
Queensland is a long record of dissension
and the casting of prominent men into the
outer political darkness. In Maoriland the
Liberal-Labor machine which Ballance
started, and Seddon drove, and Ward tried
vainly to lubricate with Cohen’s gold, has
practically broken to pieces after a run of
two decades; and all who watched its pro-
gress admit that it ran as long as it did be-
cause it was a loose organisation which left
any amount of room for the individuality of
its engineers. It looks, then, as if the time
had come for sane supporters of progressive
movements in Australia to sit down and do
some solid thinking on the whole question of
party. The Labor machine, with the very
best intentions and enough enthusiasm to
carry it a long way, has been bitterly disap-
pointing to its warmest supporters. Clearly
the fault does not lie with the intentions or
the enthusiasm, but with the machine which
has been trying to work out the legislation
and reform. On the other hand, were indi-
vidual freedom granted to members of Par-
liament, and if Ministers were elected by
Parliament, and if the Referendum with
Initiative were brought into force, no indi-
vidual or combination of individuals could
by any sort of contrivance thwart the public
will or long delay the carrying out of its
demands. And then it would be utterly im-
possible to look back upon two years of
pledge-breaking, muddle and general failure
to do the thing most needed.

A Billet for which None is Good Enough
but the Best.
Scandalised and grief-stricken at the Gov-
ernment’s amendments of the Judiciary
Act, Melbourne Argus came out with a
brace of typically ’‘Liberal” propositions.
They may be stated briefly as follows: —
(1) The work of the Court does not demand the
addition of a fresh Judge or Judges.
(2) If democrats are appointed, it follows that
they will be lawyers of inferior standing, and in
consequence the standard and authority of the Court
will be lowered in respect to all the general domain
of Law.
The statement that the Court needs no rein-
forcement is palpably idiotic. Even when
Justice O’Connor was alive and in good
health the Bench was scandalously under-
manned. Overwork helped materially to
hasten his end; it laid up Justice Barton
for the best part of a year; and it threatens
to fell C.J. Griffith before long. The
business of the Court is many months in
arrear, and things are growing worse month
by month. The Court does not merely re-
quire new judges; it demands them insist-
ently. So far as the political aspect of the
matter is concerned, the Government would,
of course, be as foolish as it would be im-
moral if it proceeded to pack the bench with
men of its own way of thinking, regardless
of all other considerations. It would also
be mad and wicked if it gave the job to a
man because he chanced to be a Roman
Catholic, or a South Australian, or a Jew,
or, indeed, anything whatever but a man of
first-rate capacity. If what the Argus calls
“lawyers of inferior standing” are appointed,
there will indeed be a public uproar; and the
community will do well to be scandalised.
But has this bland assumption, that every
democratic barrister is necessarily incom-
petent, any basis in fact? Anyone pos-
sessed of even a moderate acquaintance with
the Courts will smile broadly at such an idea.
The plain truth is, most of the conservative-
minded men who are mentioned in the daily
press as warm favorites in the race for the
Bench, know little of Constitutional law, and
less of law generally. And though an expert
in Constitutional law would be a valuable
asset to the Court, if he lacked other qualifi-
cations he would be an anomaly and an en-
cumbrance. The Court, it must not be
forgotten, deals with only one or two Con-
stitutional cases a year: the rest of its time
is occupied with appeal work of a compli-
cated nature. Only one type of man is
wanted for that onerous job—a first-rate
lawyer in large practice. If the Government
disregards that essential, and appoints some-
one because of his religion, or because of his
State, or because he is an opponent whom it
would be more comfortable to have out of
the way, or because he is a Tory, and hence
acceptable to anti-Laborites, it will be mak-
ing a very ghastly mistake. The best avail-
able brains are the only sort that Australia
wants in the Chief Court of the Common-
wealth, and they should be secured with the
serenest indifference to the political views
of their owner.
•
A New Measure, and Toryism’s Natural
Exasperation Thereat.
It was to be expected that the Fisher
Government’s proposal to educate the people
in regard to the meaning of the proposed
Constitutional amendments would make the
Opposition furious. The idea of the Minis-
try is, in the words of one of the anti-
Labor dailies, “to introduce a Bill to pro-
vide for the issue of a pamphlet containing
a statement by the two sides in politics of
the view which they take of the amend-
ments.” A sufficiently fair and reasonable
scheme on the face of it, one would think.
Yet Sydney Telegraph condemns it
haughtily (18/12/T2) as “an extraordinary
notion,” and “a scheme which is grotesquerie
itself,” and waxes bitterly sarcastic on the
subject of “errand-boy literature,” and the
huge stocks of waste paper that “the work”
will be responsible for. The true Conserva-
tive always mistrusts the education of the
masses in political or social questions. His
policy flourishes best in the dark. And this
is peculiarly true with regard to the Refer-
enda questions. A negative vote is a cer-
tainty next year if the electors can only be
kept as ignorant as they were in 1911. It
is natural that people should vote conserva-
tively on those issues which they do not un-
derstand. In Switzerland, the most daringly
progressive of European countries and the
home of the Referendum system, this has
always been so. Most of the measures
which have since been found to be highly
beneficial were rejected two and three times
and occasionally oftener. When the Nation-
alisation of Railroads campaign was first
started the Cantons found their repre-
sentatives taking two widely-divergent
points of view. Those who were for
the maintenance of the status quo,
and who considered _ that the time was
unripe for any enterprise of the sort, argued
primarily that the inauguration of a system
of Socialistic railroads amounted to robbery
and anarchy, and would operate to drive
vast masses of capital out of the country.
They also claimed that a gross wrong would
be done to the Cantons; that the National
Government would be empowered, if it
were given control of the railways, to dif-
ferentiate injuriously between the various
units of the Confederation, and that it
would not hesitate to do so. And, of course,
they contended that under Federal owner-
ship the railway service would be infamously
bad (the good old match and cigarette in-
dustries of France being dragged in to point
the moral), and they prophesied that the
wages of the railway servants would be
awful. The case for nationalisation rested
on such dull and complicated issues as fin-
ance, the terms on which the roads and
rolling stock might be acquired from the
private companies that owned them, and
the likelihood of the Federal Government
being able to lower rates and provide im-
proved services. What wonder that the
vague melodrama of the reactionaries won
the day for a while? In that campaign,
as in the struggle to achieve a
Federal Factory to found a State Bank,
and to establish an alcohol mono-
poly, the party of progress only won when
it had driven the meaning of its proposals
into the collective head of the people by
sheer force. The thing was done by a pam-
phleteering campaign of the very sort which
the Fisher Government provided for last
week. There was no favoritism. The
Hebrew railway magnates, the manufac-
turers and the publicans who disapproved
of the Swiss proposals had the same oppor-
tunities to lay the merits of their cases,
such as they were, before the electors, as
the monopolists and low-wage enthusiasts of
Australia will have under the measure which
came before the National Parliament last
week. In the end the Swiss mono-
polists went down. And the moment
the Commonwealth grasps the signifi-
cation of the Referenda questions the
same catasirophe will happen to the local
monopoly party. The Telegraph sees the
downfall coming, and in its capacity of true
and faithful servant of the monopolists’ in-
terests, it is naturally annoyed.

An Object Lesson in Nation-building.
“Dingo” from Capetown :
I wish you would send Bruce Smith to S’Africa.
I understand that he’s one of those extremely modest
men who don’t think half enough of either them-
selves or their opinions. If he would only spend
a few months here he would see how experience sup-
ports all his theories. As Mr. Myth knows, three
things a country needs to become great and pros-
perous— (1) plenty of colored labor; (2) plenty of
foreign capital; (3) plenty of Freetrade. S’Africa
has all three. (1) For every white person there are
five colored. (2) There is so much foreign capital
that the real owners of the country, who can even
run the Empire into a ghastly war, live in Park
Lane, Berlin, Paris and other haunts of the Noses.
(3) The tariff is mostly of the revenue-producing
description that Mr. Myth loves. And this is a
glimpse of the “jolly and fat” condition of the
country under these blessings. There is one banking
corporation with headquarters for S’Africa in Cape-
town in which there are about 60 employees. Of
these, only 14 receive salaries allowing them to
marry. Reductions in salaries were made by this
bank about six years ago on account of hard times.
Tellers were then in receipt of the exorbitant salary
of £l3 6s. Bd. a month. From that 10 per cent,
was deducted owing to the shrinkage of the dividend
on the foreign capital. The banks are all foreign;
all dividends are paid outside the country, and so the
directors don’t hear the protests of the overpaid
clerk. Insurance companies are almost as bad. They
too are mainly foreign, including under that title
the Colonial Mutual and the National Mutual,
which are Australian. There is one big local insur-
ance co., which is relatively as strong as is the
A.M.P. in the Commonwealth; but its salaries are
not much better than those of its foreign competi-
tors. The only manufacturing industries, bar a little
furniture-making, are devoted to cigarettes, soap
and dynamite. In the cigarette and soap-making,
colored girls do nearly all the work, and foreign
Jews own the factories. One big tobacco factory
proudly shows a list of 400 employees receiving m
all about £5OO wages a week. The average clerk’s
salary is £7 a month. Flappers are working at
30s. a month in most of the business houses. Rag-
shop hands are worse off. In Capetown alone over
1000 girls who are just nearing the age of potential
maternity are learning stenography and type-click-
ing. Three of one insurance co.’s servants are now
awaiting trial for alleged embezzlement of pre-
miums, etc. —one in Jewburg, one in Durban and one
in Capetown. But juries are beginning to argue on
these lines: “If accused has diverted the company s
money into his own pocket, he has simply reimbursed
himself for the moneys the directors have stolen from
him by way of salary.” Such reasoning may not be
sound, but it is prevalent here. Quite naturally, the
Australian is not a popular man in the country. He
is a good worker, it is admitted; but he wants to
be well paid—and to want to be well paid is an
enormity when there are allegedly-cheap niggers
around, and foreign capitalists to feed, and a tariff
that permits the cheap foreign factory to pour its
stuff into the country.
How a Large Amount of Capital is Kept
Out of the Country.
The directors of Italy’s biggest hat factory
recently issued a printed statement of their
achievements in the arena of commerce,
the occasion or provocation being the Brus-
sels Exhibition of 1910. It makes sad read-
ing for those who are interested in building
up Australian manufactories and adding to
the sparse population of the Commonwealth.
This firm is easily the biggest individual
rival with which the local felt-hat builder
has to contend. It has secured such a grip
on the men’s soft felt hat trade of the Com-
monwealth that in 1909 (the last year for
which statistics are supplied) it was able
to export to these parts over 80,000 of its
wares. As 1910-’ll-’l2 were all bumper
years, it is safe to put the imports to Aus-
tralia of this one maker’s lids at 100,000
per annum during that period. Wages
Boards, Arbitration Courts and similar de-
vices are unknown in the land of the Dago,
and labor is consequently dirt-cheap. The
firm itself supplies figures to show that the
average wage per head in these, the highest-
grade mills in Italy, is 10s. per week, while
the average wage-cost of each hat is 9d.
Now, the employees in Australian mills can,
without difficulty, earn £4 per week. What
development of the hat-manufacturing busi-
ness can be expected under those circum-
stances with a duty of 35 per cent. —a duty
which, as above mentioned, lets in a matter
of 100,000 hats per annum from one foreign
firm alone? In 1913 there will be upwards
of 100,000 men in the Commonwealth wear-
ing this one firm’s felt hats, for which they
will have paid an average price of 12s. each.
If these had been fashioned on the spot, the
labor charges, at a conservative estimate,
would have been 2s. 6d. per lid. So that
the one Italian firm removes, in a manner
of speaking, £12,500 worth of workers’ wages
from the country every year. And that is
without taking into consideration the dam-
age done by the rest of the overseas hat-
exporters. The bogus Laborite who, by
persistently dodging the tariff issue, is re-

The Servian Minister in London writes
intimating that his wife is making an ap-
peal “for the wounded, sick and destitute
Servians in the war,” and asking that con-
tributions be sent to the legation, 40 Pont-
street, London S.W.
The unaccountable lighthouse smash at
Port Adelaide has been “sat upon” by vari-
ous courts, and the more it is discussed the
more unaccountable it becomes. The light
over the old Norma wreck is supposed to
have scared the Dimsdale’s captain off his
course, but how it was possible to sail clean
into a brilliantly-lighted beacon nobody
knows. The collanse of the structure (which
cost nearly £20,000), leaving the ship prac-
tically uninjured, is another strange point.
The ultimate verdict on Captain Jones, for
the loss of the light and the lives of the two
good men who kept it, is a fine of £5O and
suspension of his master’s-certificate for a
year; but the probable ruin of a career is
the heaviest penalty.
A send-off:—
John Hughes, M.L.C., solicitor and some time
Vice-President of the Executive Council of N. S.
Wales, died rather suddenly last week. He was a
good type of citizen—one of those steady-going char-
acters who don't do anything with dazzling brilliance,
but do everything moderately well. Environment
(he was the son of a well-to-do local merchant) and
education (he was a product of Stonyhurst, Eng-
land’s biggest R.C. school) did much to make him
a “Liberal.” But he had few other faults. Of him,
as of the late Abou Ben Adhem, it might be written
that he “loved his fellow-men” ; he was a generous
and broad-minded soul, and as straight as a die. His
one definite weakness was for the ornate uniform or
livery that he wore at Government House receptions
and other functions of the sort. And, certainly, h'is
handsome 6ft.-odd looked its very best in this bizarre
and eye-arresting costume.
General-manager Knox, of the C.S.R.,
wept in the papers as soon as the Sugar
Commission’s report was issued. The very
idea of standardising the price of sugar
harrowed Mr. Knox. How monstrous it
would be to fix the price at (say) £2O when
the co. might 12 months before have bought
the same sugar in foreign parts on a £25
basis! It would be heart-breaking, of
course; still Australia might struggle to
grow all its own sugar, and the co. might
encourage it to do so. Over this home-grown
sugar there could be no trouble, because the
co. buys its raw material (either raw sugar
or cane) at a price subject to market move-
ments. But, apart from that, when Mr.
Knox wept over the losses the co. might
make by buying foreign sugar on a £25-per-
ton basis and having to sell it at £2O, he
might have mentioned the awful wrench it
would be to make a profit bv buying sugar
at £l5 and being compelled to sell it at £zt).
“The Ratcatcher’s Daughter” :
On Saturday I went up to Sydney Government
House grounds with Col. Ryrie, M.H.R. ; Tom Hen-
ley, M.L.A. ; a few of the Liberal Ladies’ Associa-
tion, and some others of our set, to discountenance
the vulgarity of the lower orders at the taking-over
exhibition. But, la, I thought to have sunk through
the sacred soil for shame. We sang the National
Anthem, of course, in the teeth of the canaille, but,
when we came to the second verse, this is how we
did it:—
O Lord our God, arise,
Scatter his enemies
And make them fall;
Confound their politics,
Frustrate their knavish tricks;
On Thee (the Daily Telegraph printed it
with a small t) our hopes we fix,
God Save the King.
Of course the last line should have been, “God save
us all.” It would even have been better to have
sung it, “On Thee our hopes we fix, After the ball.”
That would, at least, have rhymed, even if it did
nothing else.
Concerning a poor, distressed co.:—
In the C.S.R.’s “reply” to the Sugar Commission’s
report the fat co. announced once more that its
prices are regulated by the world’s markets, and it
hinted that, much as it would sometimes like to
reduce prices and thus cut down its profits, it
simply couldn’t do it on account of this bogey of
the world’s markets.” It may therefore be interest-
ing to notice that “first marks” were selling at
£2l 10s. f.o.b. Hamburg on Sept. 25, 1911, the
highest point touched; and when the price came
down to £l6 ss. f.o.b Hamburg on Dec. 19, no
alteration was made by the C.S.R.—evidently because
there was nobody else in the market to compete with
the philanthropist. Following the quotation of £l6
ss. f.o.b. Hamburg on Dec. 19, the market slightly
advanced to £l9 f.o.b. Hamburg on Feb. 16, 1912-
from there it gradually declined to the level of
£l7 18s. 4d. on March 5, back to £l6 15s. on
April 5, still back to £l5 10s. on June 5, and so on
down to £l4 15s. on July 5. This continuous fall
went on till, on August 31, the price was £l3 13s.
4d. f.o.b. Hamburg. On that date the C.S.R. actu-
ally lowered, its prices only 10s. a ton, although
the world’s markets had dropped from £2l 10s.
to £l3 13s. 4d. As Mr. Knox says the C.S.R.
prices are regulated by the world’s markets, he
might explain why his price, which was £24 per
ton on Nov. 1, 1911, should have been lowered to
the extent of only 10s. per ton on Aug. 31, 1912,
followed by 20s. per ton on Oct. 5 last (30s. per ton
altogether), as against a decline of £7 16s. Bd. per ton
in the foreign (Hamburg) market in the correspond-
ing period. In view of these and other facts, it is
urgent, not only that sugar cultivation should be
encouraged in the North, but that a refinery should
be established in Queensland in co-operation with
the “central” mills.
