A first-time-in-Australia Marlowdrama
was produced at Sydney Adelphi on Satur-
day night, under the title of “The Monk and
the Woman.” In keeping with the chief
business of ordinary life the pursuit and
possession of a woman is the backbone of the
play, but, on account of her extra-special
qualities in the way of looks and get-up and
passionate eye-flash and intelligence and
noble character and a few other feminine
traits, the pursuit of Liane (who is Miss
Nellie Bramley in the ordinary course of
things) by the King of France, a monk or
The Trials of a Hero , as seen in “Her For-
bidden Marriage," Melbourne Princess's.
two and a prince is unusually strenuous. To the
forces arrayed against the virtuous Liane
add Miss Nellie Fergusson, who, for the time
being, is the worst hussy in the harem, or
something equally bad, and the difficulties of
the distraught female become simply stag-
gering. The King (the usual 16th century
kind) is a host of reprobates in himself, and
his pal, Henri de Montrale (Mr. Tom
Lloyd), is a high-roller in all those things
that the Wowser never stops thinking about.
Thus, were it not for a calm, heroic ecclesi-
astic named Paul, this story would have had
a shameful ending. But the villains fall
out. The King is pushed through the win-
dow of things by Henri, and just when the
said Henri is about to blot out the aforesaid
ecclesiastic and swallow the girl, the effi-
cacy of prayer is given another chance.
Paul, the monk, who is George Cross in dis-
guise, calls upon Heaven, in a loud voice, to
help the good cause, and particularly to lay
it over Henri. The which is promptly
done (with the assistance of the stage-
manager) ; for whereas Henri was a moment
before safe and sound in his chateau, he now
finds the walls tumbling to ruins in an earth-
quake, while a volcano floods the vicinity
with fire and fried mud and lava and gen-
eral Gehenna. That settles Henri, and the
gallery on the opening night had only one
opinion about it: Good enough for the cow!
The incidents of the piece are more sub-
dued than in the ordinary run of Adelphic
drama, and from a scenic and stage-carpen-
tering point of view is away ahead of any-
thing the Marlow management has done.
The earthquake scene in the last act, where
Henri gets it in the neck, leads the good
Christian, at this season of peace and good-
will, to ruminate on what happened to
Sodom, likewise Gomorrah. In distributing
rewards for character representation, Mr.
Tom Lloyd, who made his first appearance
at the theatre, is called on first, with Gerald
Kay Souper (the King), Nellie Fergusson
(his Majesty’s unofficial wife), Nellie
Bramley and C. R. Stanford (Father Igna-
tius) close up. The others are from fair to
medium, or vice versa, or something to that
effect. “The Monk and the Woman” will
continue until further intimation, and
matinees will break forth on Boxing Day
and New Year’s Day.

“Milestones,” now triumphantly running
at Sydney Criterion, contains no bril-
liant dialogue, no dramatic climax; but the
details of the setting and the admirable cast
get the pervasive charm of the slight thing
over the footlights. In one respect the
authors have erred. The Victorian era was
the period of big families; but here are two
families with only a “pigeon pair” of chil-
dren each. In fact, there is a clear case of
race suicide in the Rhead and Sibley fami-
lies, for after two generations there are only
three grand-children from four ancestors, one
of whom never married. The evolution pro-
ceeds on these lines : kerosene —gas —electric
light; tight trousers —bell-bottomed trousers
—tubes; clean shaves to the ears —side-
whiskers —clean shaves; hansom cabriolets —
omnibuses —motors ; downcast eyes—fluttered
glances—the cold, glad eye; forehead kisses
—unexpected embraces —prosaic kisses (off) ;
kneeling proposals—standing proposals—sit-
ting-on-the-stairs proposals; crinolines—
bustles —sheaths ; iron ships—steel ships—
reapers and binders ; crocheting—knitting—-
idle hands ; swoons —tears —snorts ; ringlets
—fringes—waves ; book-muslin —glace-silk —
satin de soie; cast-iron corsets —whalebone
corsets —elastic corsets.
“Florodora” is the Christmas meal at Her
Majesty’s. Sydney, and theatre-goers are
fairly wolfing it. When the coffee and
cigars stage of the banquet draws nigh this
paper will notify the fact; at present the
public doesn’t seem to have got beyond the
fish course.

After a successful tour, and a good look-
in at Adelaide, Allen Doone brought “The
Wearing of the Green” back to the Sydney
Palace on Saturday night. Since The Btjl-
letin saw the show last
(July, 1912), it hasn’t al-
tered its opinion that it is
merely a pleasant trifle, cal-
culated best to attract (a)
the young person who likes
her leading actor to be (1)
cold and unapproachable, as
one consumed by a Great
Sorrow (it’s mostly indiges-
tion, but that by the way),
(2) brutally strong and mas-
terful (due. often to long
battling with the bailiff and
lowering portmanteaus from
the boarding-house in the
dead of night). (3) debon-
air and handsome; and Cb) the ultra-
emotional Irish folk. The young person is
cheered up with Doone’s effeminately good-
lookingface and his make-up, and the Irish
will stick to him if only for the way he
sings the songs that reach their heart. The
piece opened to big business, and will not
be changed for a week, anyhow.

At the National Amphitheatre La France,
a fair damosel of French extraction, is ap-
pealing to the public’s aesthetic sense—that
elevating sentiment which causes surf-bath-
ing to be the popular spectacle it is, and
encourages the business of selling opera
glasses and telescopes to Wowsers to go up
by leaps and bounds each summer. La
France makes her appeal iu a series of poses.
She is great. Ben Hassan, an Indian fakir,
has arrived, and is baffling the multitude by
the perfection of his conjuring. He wishes

kindly just prior to last N. S. Wales elec-

Curran, Jules Garrison. Barnes and West
cropping up on every side.

Christopher Columbus has been demanding
attention at the Crystal Palace this week.
Columbus, Esq., has already had one season
at the Town Hall, but evidently J. D. Wil-
liams feels that something more is due
to the badly-used old man for discover-
ing America; for no America would
have meant no J. D. Williams, . and
no J. D. Williams would have meant no Cry-
stal Palace. There are some dramatic
moments in the show, as, for instance,
when the half-naked mariner picks up the
floating weed that tells him land is near.
“Williams’s Weekly” at the same filmery
contained the usual number of interesting
local happenings. “The Oath of Stephen
IIuller,” at the Lyric—a sequel to “The
Unwritten Law” which recently flicked at
that theatre —and “The Veiled Prophet,”
founded on Thomas Moore, at the Empress,
were the best of the other Williams films.

e?ny u as po ? slb l e » that “Puss in Boots” is
still happening at Her Majesty’s. The ex-
travaganza follows on the musical-comedy
lines of the last Williamson panto. Al-
though, as usual, strong on the spectacular
side, its. first act is less notable from a scenic
standpoint than the first part of the “Sin-
bad production. Fairies, elves, etc., are
not unnecessarily numerous, having been
chased off the premises by the spirit of mod-
ernity, which is responsible for a pleasing
scene in the female dormitory of Dame
Shortly s hut or palace, where a lot of nice
girls sit up in bed to watch Fred Leslie and
• shake their agile understand-
ings. The same militant up-to-dateness must
be held accmintable for the inclusion of a
large quantity of ragtime gargling, or
hustle music, which need not be understood
to be appreciated. Interruptive turns are
provided by the Gaudsmiths, clown acrobats,
who receive valuable assistance from a tribe
of hounds; and Collins and Hart also make
their living acrobatically. A feature of the
second part is a hunting ballet, brought from
Paris.
* * * *
On the comic side the show is well pro-
vided for. George Miller is a Dame whom
to know better will probably be to love; Pip
Powell, an extraordinary monarch whose ex-
istence is at least nine arguments in favor of
republicanism: and Fred Leslie, a casual
larrikin, entitled to loud “hear, hears” for
his dancing. Miss Violet Loraine, the prin-
cipal lad, is a rather lightweight blonde, who
is more masculine without being less femin-
inely alluring than last year’s “boy”; Miss
Alice Russon is a principal airl who, on a
first acquaintance, fails either to impress or
depress; an “old girl” in the nerson of Miss
Dorothy Firmin makes a pretty fairy ; and
that decorative Australian, Miss Marie
Eaton, is a gold-stick-in-waiting who would
grace any court, just as Ivy Schilling’s danc-
ing (not to mention her mere presence)
would make even a Government House ball
worth the price of admittance. And, finally,
O. E. Lennon’s diverting cat Slyboots gives
some promise of growing up into a zoologi-
cal classic like the Sosh Tiger and the Dry
Dog.

The latest offering to the gods at the
Princess’s is “Her Forbidden Marriage,” a
Marlowdrama in which the hero wears the
snowy thatch of eld, and the chief male
wrongdoer mostly behaves like a young
man predestined to be named Percy.
Since the advt. confidently avers that
those who see the play “will surely
get married,” it might prove a good
business move on the part of a local matri-
monial agency to hire advertising space on
the Princess’s curtain, and employ agents
in the gallery to distribute coupons, entitling
their holders to special terms. The theatre
will be occupied on Boxing Night by “The
Girl Who Took the Wrong Turning”—
understood to be half-sister of the damsel
who was recently Driven to Destruction.

At the Theatre Royal on Boxing Night
the sceneshifter and stage carpenter are to
be given an innings, with instructions to
hang the expense. The glory that was
Egypt and the grandeur that was Rome will
be suitably utilised to display the magnifi-
cence. that is Asche and the beauty that is
Brayton. A sub-edited version of “Antony
and Cleopatra” is to serve as an excuse for
this prodigality. The scenery has been
painted by Joseph Barker, the music com-
posed by Christopher Wilson, and an enter-
taining libretto supplied by William Shaks-
peare.

Nowadays the Opera House programme is
largely devoted to forecasting the arrival of
De Biere, who is due on Saturday next. In
the meantime there are the Lancaster Opera
co., whose unexciting vocalism is entitled to
a fair measure of respect; My Fancy, who
danced recently before the Throne, and so
wins the applause of all who are not pro-
Boers ; Claire Romaine, male impersonator;
Minnie Kauffmann, one of the trickiest of
lady cyclists; and the Ilallis and Wilson
Trio, whose acrobatic turn is provided with
“human interest” through the screams of an
invisible infant.
** * *
An all-green entertainment, “Ireland To-
Day,” opens at the Town Hall on Boxing
Night. The show will consist mostly _of
scenes and songs of a strictly Hibernian
character. The advt. puts forward the in-
vitation to Irishmen to “Come Back to
Erin,” but in the meantime Erin has come
most of the way to meet them. Boxes of the
most distressful country have been brought
out, and the soil will be spread down the
passages between the seats, so that the sen-
timental exile can carry away on the soles
of his boots some of his own, his native land.
** * *
The Smart Set still carry on business at
the Tarts’ open-air theatre on the St. Kilda
Esplanade (Vic.). The Smart Setters are
seven, and the prevailing raspberry of their
attire hints at a sobriety which their jests
and jingles fail to dispel. Judged by such
representatives as Walter George and Miss
Georgie Martin, they are amusing without
lapsing into frivolous behavior, and they can
warble the latest tunes and airs in a pleas-
ing if unimphssioned way. They possess
very passable songsters in Oswald Preston
and Miss Emily Kroll, and an eccentric
comedian and ventriloquist in Edward
Elliott. This whimsical citizen, who has the
outlines of a walking stick or a fountain
pen, is a very popular feature, and there
seems a disposition on the part of the
audience to call him Edward, or even led.
Another chapter of Captain Scott’s doings
in Antarctic regions has been unfolded at
West’s Melbourne house. The present series
is even more alluring than the last, and gives
some idea of the things you ought to do be-
fore you take Gwendoline for a stroll to the
Pole. The pictures showing the arrange-
ments for board and lodging on the icy
slopes are especially interesting, and when
the audience is confronted with the methods
of making a meal, it learns something new
in domestic economy. _ In the fiction depart-
ment the main suit was “Britannicus
Tragedy (after Racine),” revealed with in-
tense gesticulation by a selected crowd from
the Paris Odeon. A more skilfully acted
item was “Coronets and Hearts,” a sort of
pictorial tornado with a lovely hero, several
bandits, and the inevitable girl.
* ;]: *
The P’l-an Lyric (Vic.) last week bio-
graphed a long story of criminal misbe-
havior in which an elusive spieler called
Handsome Charlie has many exciting and
picturesque moments. Charlie is aided in
his wickedness by a podgy gent, and a
buxom Flossie. The trio plan a midnight
robbery, which ends in the arrest of Flor-
ence and Podge, and the demise of the prin-
cipal man. The picture is an unusually
clever piece of detective fiction, and the last
chase of Charlie, unconsciously heading for
the undertaker, is a spirited bit of make-
believe. “Algy—Did He Deserve It?” was
an innocently giddy contribution, and “Her
Awakening” was a modern seaside version of
the tale of the reptile in Eden.
*# * *
In a hot and quiet week, Adelaide’s chief
dramatic event has been “The Tyranny of
Tears,” staged by the Appendreena Society.
This is the Haddon Chambers play whose
witty, inconsequent dialogue aroused a
strong suspicion that .Oscar Wilde had a
hand in it. Its one fault is too much talk,
too little action,, Miss Ethelwyn Robin, as
the weepy wife, and Len Chamberlain (the
cricketer), as the strong, good-natured hus-
band, did good work in parts a little outside
their usual range. As the lady secretary (Mrs.
Brough’s old part) Miss Kathleen Suther-
land was just about It. The rest of the
small cast was in safe hands, and Clement
May—yvho coached —-obliged with a neat
sketch of the butler. But it would be a
better play if ruthlessly “cut” by 10 to 15
minutes.
Nellie Farren, the Gaiety girl, is in the new vol.
of the “Dictionary of National Biography.” It is
admitted that she was neither tall nor beautiful, nor
had she an agreeable singing or speaking voice, but
“the charm of her individuality triumphed over all
stage defects, and her good humor and ready stories
of drollery gave her an omnipotence over the average
theatre-goer.” Omnipotence!

“Harrison O.”: I haven’t seen it mentioned lately
that George Rignold once played Romeo to five
different Juliets during one performance. It was in
N’Yark, at a benefit show. The Misses Capulet were
Fanny Davenport, Aila Dyas, Minnie Cummings,
Maudie Grainger and Marie Wainvvright. Said'
George, upon being called before the curtain, “Youi
see, I have survived it.”

“Haresfoot”: Just as I’m certain that the inter-
state steamboat combination is good for passengers:
(contrast our coasters with the English, and even
the American), so I do think the Williamson-
Clarke-Meynell conjunction is working out all right'.
Look at the 1913 programme: Quinlan, heaps of
novelty by the new comic opera companies, a new'
Gilbert and Sullivan co., Waller in “Henry V.,”'
Asche in “Midsummer Night’s Dream” and “Air-
tony and Cleopatra,” Shakspeare galore, and 1
“Faust,” promised in tip-top style by the regular
dramatic co.
A note from John Galsworthy: “I see that a, cor-
respondent (B. 5/9/’l2) has given you what pur-
ports to be Mr. Haddon Chambers’s reply to the
critics who noticed a likeness between his ‘Passers-
by’ and my play, ‘The Pigeon.’ Mr. Chambers says
‘The Pigeon’ was produced in London some months
after ‘The Passers-by.’ I’m afraid I must confess,
to my regret, that I have never seen or read ‘Pas-
sers-by,’ or indeed any play by Mr. Haddon Cham-
bers. ‘The Pigeon’ was finished just before ‘Pas-
sers-by’ was produced. Your readers may rely on it
that, if there be similarity, there was no plagiar-
ism.”
“Grandpa”- What commonplace affairs are your
theatres of to-day! I mind visiting the Theatre
Royal in Castlemaine in ’54 to see G. V. Brooke
play “O’Callaghan on his Last Legs. It was a
charity affair, and all the rest of the performers
were amateurs of the first water—or some other liquid.
i\j c O the renowned lawyer, played the Irish at-
torney. Two citizens were deputed to keep him sober
during the day; but when the performance began the
two guardians were lying helplessly full in the
Cumberland cellar, and Mac was not to be found.
He was at last discovered and pushed up on the
stage. He scrambled up on a barrel, and began to
fiddle. Then the top fell in, and he couldn’t get
out till some one chopped the barrel open. When
that had been managed he came to the front of the
stage and said: “Gentlemen of the jury, you re a
lot of sausages, and those of you who haven’t been
in the logs are certain to be there very soon.
Good night.” Then Lola Montez came on to dance
the spider dance. Yum! Yum! Afterwards she
came to the front and said, apropos of nothing in
particular, that she had Spanish blood in her veins.
One of the audience cried out: “You lie. You are
Never mind what he said. It was very rude.
“I’ll shout for all hands if anyone will show me the
in an who said that,” cries Lola. A carpenter l lo * ll
Campbell’s Creek got on the stage and repeated the
-words There was hooting and crying out and pelt-
ing of things at him. The crowd was going to
lynch him, and began to rush the stage. Sergeant
Fleming, of the police, pushed his way forward, but
was thrown off, and had two ribs broken. That, of
course, set the police going in earnest. Using their
handcuffs as weapons, they swung themselves around
and soon cleared the theatre. Ah, there was some
joy going to the theatre in those days !
“Hamer” : The Actors’ Union is finding the course
of arbitration about as smooth - as a footpath of oyster
shells. Twelve months ago it first made applica-
tion for registration as a union under the Arbitra-
tion Act, and the High Court decided a week or so
ago that the Registrar might view acting as an in-
dustry and consider a second application, the-first
registration having been cancelled in consequence of
a technical error in the date of the issue of the cer-
tificate of registration. J. C. Williamson, Ltd., and
•other metropolitan managers, tried to secure from the
High Court an injunction restraining the Registrar
from doing anything of the kind, with the main re-
sult that J. C. Williamson, Ltd., and others will have
to pay everybody’s costs. And as costs in the High
Court are usually considerable, the Actors’ Union
these days wears almost the first smile it has ever
been guilty of.
Still another new picture theatre, the Empire, is
to be built in Bourke-street, Melbourne, a few doors
west of. the Eastern Arcade, the site of the former
Haymarket Theatre. Yet the thing seemed to be
overdone already; neither the Majestic, in Flinders-
• street, nor the Snowden, over Princes Bridge, has
come up to expectation, the Exhibition pictures have
failed, and the Saturday and Sunday shows at the
•Cyclorama, in Victoria Parade, have been dropped.
“The Chee-ild” : I love being put right, especially
by people who are capable of putting me all wrong,
and most especially when they do it in the nice,
superior-person style of reproof adopted by “C.
•Ockney” (B. 28/l'l/’l2), who tells me how inno-
cent I am regarding the one-act play or curtain-
raiser in London. The point of my par. was that
•elsewhere than in Australia (e.</., London), theatre
patrons expect and generally get a “raiser” as an
introduction to a short play, and I merely instanced
"■‘Alias Jimmy Valentine” as of the type calling for
ithis support. This roused “C. 0.” to the summit of
Ihi* patronising satire. “When ‘The Chee-ild’
■(prattles,” says he, “of bright curtain-raisers, I
'would like to ask where ? Not in London, surely!
There nowadays the piece of the evening is usually
preceded by Mr. and a piano, or a l ady
mimic.” lit was a nuisance to find that my experi-
ences in two trips to Europe and America were
mere illusions, but I determined to get rid of them
at once, and therefore turned up the latest file of
the London Sporting Times. There in the advt.
columns of the shows now running in Fogtown, I
was struck by this disclosure:—-
Haymarket.—“Bunty pulls the Strings,” preceded by
“Their Point of View.”
Wyndham’s.—“ Doormats,” preceded by “The
Quaints.”
■Criterion. —“Ann,” preceded by “The Poetasters of
Ispahan.”
Same house (promised).—-“Tantrums,” preceded by
“The Fortune Teller.”
Comedy.—“A Scrape o’ the Pen,” preceded by “All
Men are Fools.”
New Theatre. —“Ready Money,” preceded by “An
Object Lesson.”
"Playhouse.—“Hindle Wakes,” preceded by “Make-
shifts.”
Same house (promised).—“The Little Cafe,” pre-
ceded by “Aunt Bessie.”
Vaudeville. —“Little Miss Llewellyn,” preceded by
“The Maker of Dreams.”
Duke of York’s. —Three Short Plays.
Prince of Wales. —“Art and Opportunity,” preceded
by Mr. Warwick Price.
You will see I have not missed the lonely “Mr. W.
Price.” He may be “C. Ockney’s” “Mr. at
the piano” and the lady mimic rolled into one. Of
the other standard theatres (excluding Shakspeare,
musical comedy, leg shows and bellowdrama) there
remain a beggarly three apparently playing one piece
on its lonely, and none of them necessarily short
plays: “Drake,” “Milestones” and “Zaza.” Isn’t it
up to Mr. “Cockney” to get back to his native
Shoreditch to learn what they have been doing there
the last 100 years?
“Tara”: Apropos the Irish Show for Christmas at
Melbourne Town Hall. Once upon a time there were
two good Irish panoramas in Australia at once. The
Hibernicon was the name of one, conducted by
Ainsley Scott; the other was the Hibernica, under
little H.A. B. Williams. The latter’s comedian was Tim
Cohan —related, I fancy, to Cohan, author of “Get-
Rich-Quick Wallingford,” and to Josephine, who
•came to play in that bustling piece.
“Busk”: And “Fedora” (B. 5/12/’l2) was acted
in Melbourne by Mrs. Bernard Beere. Standing
made a frightful mess of Ipanoff, and so distressed
her that an apology had to be given. Otho Stuart
replaced him as leading man, whereupon Standing
;sank like Jack Murray behind Watt.
“Private Boxer” : London soon had enough of the
Shaw-Pinero-Barrie triple bill. It ran for three
weeks, and was then succeeded by a new play by
;Stanlaws, hitherto known as a black-and-white
•artist of the Dana Gibson school. Barrie sur-
vives. His one-acter, “Rosalind,” is retained, but
not as a curtain-raiser; it is to follow Stanlaws’s
piece, “Instinct.” The three-star triple-bill has
proved another of Charles Frohman’s costly experi-
ments. It has also proved (what everyone knew be-
fore) that Barrie is the most reliable of the present-
day dramatists. Indeed, if it had not been for the
little Scot’s contributions, Frohman would have pro-
bably shut up shop in London long ago.
, “Hassan”: Luna Park, J. D. Williams’s latest Mel-
bourne venture, comprises two gentlemen who have
surely discovered the very quaintest possible method
of making a living. They make their crust by pan-
dering to the degraded emotion in mankind which
takes delight in the misfortunes of others. You
supply the cash, they supply the misfortune. They
sit upon jointed perches, one on each side of a huge
face, the eyes of which are the targets. The Neros, in
■disguise, buy so many shots at so much, and throw
tennis balls at these' targets. If a ball hits the
•eye the platform on which the man perches parts
suddenly in the middle, and the victim drops into a
cold bath with a resounding splash. The night I
attended Luna Park was very hot, and the two dicky-
birds seemed quite happy. But I’m wondering what
will happen when a cold snap comes. Mr. Hunt will
"have, at least, two profoundly interested readers of
his weather prophecies during the next few months, I
think.
“Hullo”: There are only two types of Australian
drama that make money for their carpenters: ( )
The Australian melodrama, which, except in the
stage scenery, isn’t Australian at all; and (2) tne
Australian farce-comedy, in which there is»o plot
at all, but much good character-drawing. There are
only two examples of the latter class, On Ou
Selection” and “Mrs. McSweeney ; and from their
great popularity I judge that Australian audiences
will overlook the absence of plot or story as long as
Dad and Mrs. McSweeney will discourse on themes Aus-
tralian. Herein I see a high artistic culture in Aus-
tralian audiences. They are interested in character
and not in watching things merely happen. they
ought to take kindly to the modern discussion-play
when it arrives.
“Screen”: The Kalem Co. has just completed its
cine.-pictures of the Life and Death of Jesus, taken
on the historical spots, including Gethsemane and
Calvary. The cost lias run into £20,000. But now
comes the question, Will the public stand it. In
London, curiously enough, while the Christian
Herald approves the show, the Daily Mail censures
it. There is, at any rate, Ober-Ammergau ior a
precedent—if precedent is wanted.
“Theorbo”: Strauss’s new opera, “Ariadne in
Naxos,” has been produced in Stuttgart. A strange
freak it is, for which a hint perhaps came from the
vogue of the “Tales of Hoffmann.” Composers no\\-
adays seem to want to do something bizarre, like
this or “The Girl of the Golden West.” Strauss
clearly wrote for a unique voice, that of Madame
Siems. She is not of the calibre of Melba or Tet-
razzini, but can do more gymnastics. Thus Strauss
is like Sardou wrapping Fedora, Theodora and iosca
round Bernhardt. The plot is most eccentric, lirst
comes funniment by Jourdain, the theatre manager,
with his music master and dancing master. lhe
music master is tp furnish him with an opera, re-
lating to the loves of Bacchus and Ariadne. the
dancing master is to furnish a comic ballet, relat-
ing to Zerbinetta, a coquette. Jourdain insists that
he must see these two together. So there is a
jumble, illuminated by the music of Strauss. But
the affair may ultimately drop, like Mozart s Magic
Flute” and Weber’s “Euryanthe.” It overleaps the
rational bounds, just as Rostand’s “Chanticleer did.
“Lud”: Covent Garden’s history is more import-
ant than Drury Lane’s. Rich, the harlequin, in-
ventor of the modern pantomime, built and opened
it in 1732. Its first notable development was Han-
del’s production of his operas, from 1735 to
followed by the oratorio “Messiah” in 1743. Lold-
smith’s “She Stoops to Conquer” was first performed
there (1773), and Sheridan’s “Rivals” (1,75). Mrs.
Siddons there won her first London success (1780);
Garrick had played there 44 years previously. In
1805, after Trafalgar, a one-act piece called Nel-
son's Glory” was positively written, rehearsed and
acted in one day. In 1808 the theatre was . ~
stroyed by fire. New Covent Garden was opened in
1809. Drury Lane was burnt the same year. The
new Drury got hold of Kean ; the only new man of
weight Covent Garden could secure was Macready.
The latter’s subsequent lesseeship was noticeable for
“The Lady of Lyons” (1838) and “Richelieu
(1839). Then came the management of Matthews
and Vestris, giving Boucicault’s “London Assur-
ance.” The theatre was burnt for the second time
in 1856, after a Bal Masque which ended a two-
days’ gala under “Wizard” Anderson, who was
making ready to leave for Australia.
“Stargazer”: Notice how amorous inference grows
in the music halls ? The disposition of the stereo-
chronic, the man who sings, the inflamed comedian,
and the sketch artist to lug in saucy suggestions of
conduct only mentionable in divorce courts and
medical handbooks continues and accumulates. 1
was at a kaleidoscopic vaudeville parade the other
evening, and the sole wit. of five turns depended
upon he, she or it, while using' Sabbatarian sen-
tences, really insinuating startling love episodes and
conduct unbecoming in the unmarried. Are people
so greedy for this sort of stuff that they are not
nauseated after a procession of, say, four assorted
turns in which the performers strain their terrible
nerve to produce libidinous ideas? Apart altogether
from Wowserish considerations, it may be alleged
that the subject isn’t funny. A healthy mind per-
ceives it, indeed, to be the most serious on earth.
“F.”: “Ben Bolt” came to Sydney like this. In
the early ’so’s a company of minstrels, the New
York Serenaders, gave a series of concerts at the
Royal Hotel, George-street. (The room is now used
as Dymock’s book store.) One of the members, Mr.
White, by trade a compositor, sang “Ben Bolt, and
it became so popular that the A.S.N. Co. gave the
name to one of its steamers. “Jim” Kitts, after-
wards a theatrical agent, and mixed up with William
Saurin Lyster in operatic matters, was one of the ori-
ginal New York serenaders. So was J. M. Foans, who
afterwards kept the Shakspeare Tavern, opposite the old
Victoria Theatre in Pitt-street; both ancient institu-
tions are now wiped out. The tavern has been
supplanted by Baumann’s, and Mrs. McCathie’s
occupies the theatre site.
“Monkbarns”: A Melbourne bookseller has a Shaks-
peare folio of 1623, presented by Montgomery to
Hoskins. There is the following inscription by Mont-
gomery on the fly leaf: “Presented by Walter Mont-
gomery to William Hoskins, July 10th, 1869, in
pleasant recognition of one of the most enthusiastic
and worthiest students of the bard, and in happy
memory of a successful and delightful engagement in
dear and appreciative Sydney.” Montgomery did
well in Svdney, although that city did not worship
his Hamlet like Melbourne, where the season spurred
on the production of a book under the title of “Was
Hamlet Mad?” with preface by Haddon, editor of
the Argus.
DISAPPOINTMENT OR THE FLYING STOMACH.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
There were many excellently arranged items in
the programme, well carried out, but some disap-
pointment was felt that the Desperado feat, in which
a young man is purported to dive from an 80ft
scaffold and to land on his stomach on a hardwood
disc was necessarily cut out. — Sydney D. 1.
We are not often moved to grief,
Or overwhelmed with woe,
Our sorrow is, in general, brief,
And scanty our tear-flow ;
But there are times (and this is one of them) when
anguish isn’t fleeting; when we could out-
weep the mutest mutes —every mother’s son
of them—and give Niobe, Rachel and Eugenie
Duggan ten pocket-handkerchiefs and a beat-
ing !
What resignation, what despair
That may be, or has been,
Can with this horrid blow compare,
This disappointment keen?
To think that such a show, so loudly lauded, should
dare to have defrauded a faithful, anxious
gathering which paid its cash, not to hear
base clowns blathering— hut to see the smash f
Oh, take your riders all away,
And send your tigers back !
However great and bright and gay
Your Circus, there’s a lack —
A lack indeed, which will be deeply felt—the fall,
the rush, the sickening thud, the welt — the
human like a hummock! This— this is absent
from the show ; lienee we declare we will not
go until we definitely know we’ll see the
Flying Stomach!
K.S.W.

IN THE DAYTIME.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The kissing of ladies by strangers in Sturt-street
cannot be tolerated, especially in the daytime.
— Mr. Pearse, J.P., of Ballarat (Vic.).
There is a time to embrace and a time to refrain
from embracing. —Book of Ecclesiastes.
No, the daytime isn’t playtime for an amorous
bacillus ;
Little Cupid’s very stupid while the sun is blazing
high ;
But the night time is the right time for yearning
thoughts to fill us,
And a cuddle’s but a muddle till the stars are in
the sky.
Down at Coogee when the smoogy start their five
o’clock embraces,
Then it vexes and perplexes every expert at the
game;
For caressing is distressing while you see each other’s
faces;
When it’s darkened, seek the park, and Love will
burn with brighter flame.
When a flapper has a chap, or when a spinster has
a suitor,
Then the daylight is a gay light, but it’s not the
time to spoon;
Let the gloaming find them roaming; for there’sj
nothing that is cuter
Than seclusion from intrusion when the clouds have
hid the moon.
There’s decadence in the maidens who would hug in
daylight glaring ;
’Tis unpleasing to go squeezing till the Night her
veil has flung;
And if Sadie, Sue or Haidee in a daytime spoon is
sharing,
Well, the Preacher ought to teach her: she’s un-
pardonably young.
N.S.W.

From a Tasmanian in the Bung business:
Ethically, dog doesn’t eat dog, but I’d like to have
a snap at some Sydney hotelkeepers— not of the variety
the Wowsers call “drink shops,’’ but top-notch hotels,
My grievance is this. I spent a few days in Sydney
lately and, though not at ail fastidious, I was first
y b™£“! h«rz
liquor spoilt. Barmen and barmaids alike seemed
to glory in parading the horrible germ-laden rags
on their arms or shoulders. Surely there’s a Board
of Health in Australia’s Queen City!
The Horticultural Society, of Melbourne,
and the Victorian Horticultural Improve-
ment Society are not playing speaks with
Treasurer Watt. The coolness arises over
a request for “assistance” in repeating the
show held in Melbourne early this year. To
that a ? ail „ the Government contributed
£3OO, the Horticultural Society £5O, and
the Improvement Society £35. This was
nice and kind of all parties, and when the
balance-sheet showed a credit of £l6O, an
innocent citizen might have expected the
amount to be parcelled out pro rata , each
party bearing its share of the loss. But the
two societies weren’t taking any pro rata
division. They ignored the Government and
its contribution, handed back the Horticul-
tural its £5O plus £l5 bonus, the Improve-
ment its £35 plus £lO bonus, and whacked
the odd £5O between the manager and the
secretary of the show—which was also very
nice and kind. But now, with another show
in view, these persons wish to bite Watt’s
ear for a second £3OO, and Watt is giving
them the glassy eye. There are occasions
when the glassy eye is full of merit.
‘Wahgite”: If “Larrakeah” knows any-
thing about the Northern Territory he’ll re-
cognise that whenever the two tribes had a
dispute the Wahgites gave the Larrakeahs
lophet. It s time this “Larrakeah” got
Tophet too. The work the Aborigines Pro-
tection Department has done is too good to
be allowed to suffer at the hands of a fool.
Ghief Inspector Beckett has seen and known
more niggers than “Larrakeah” wots of;
he. was in the Territory in ’B6, and spent
three years among tribes in Kimberley be-
fore white men were there, and has seen
more of the Territory than “Larrakeah’s”
tribal boundaries cover. The new Abori-
ginal Department • has cleaned up China-
town, made a decent village and large gar-
dens, built a hospital ward and made war
on the red plague, largely stopped enforced
prostitution and comboism, driven the lubras
out of Chinatown, and is now building a
school. The hawking of children by old
bucks—practically a slave market—is known
no more. Yet this “Larrakeah” has the gall
to attack the organisation which already
has done these thines —the most meritorious
department in the Territory.

“John Phoenix”: What miserable shanties
are provided as for children
attending country schools! Indeed, in a
great many grounds the shed never answers
to “the roll” at all. In others, four poles
are roofed with bark to provide nippers with
a retreat from the snow-laden blasts of win-
ter, the drenching rains of autumn, and the
bite of the summer sun. In the cities elab-
orate structures are provided for shelter.
Isn’t the country kid worth a bit of protec-
tion while he bolts his mid-day bread-and-
jam?

BROWNE: “Did you ?"
BROWN: “Yes, they did."
The State Cow of the Northern Terri-
tory walked into the forefront of things last
week, when a correspondent, writing from
Darwin to a Melbourne paper, alleged that
a man got 10s. a day for milking the animal,
while a nigger received 3s. a day to lead it
to pasture—probably with appropriate
music. He also stated that its food cost
255. per week. (Either the pastures weren’t
knee-deep in grass, or else the animal spent
the long, lone hours waving a defiant tail at
Japan.) Josiah Thomas, Minister for Ex-
ternal Affairs, hurriedly called up Adminis-
trator Gilruth by telegraph, and that emin-
ent horse-doctor, who is burdened with the
partially-employed title of “His Excellency,”
shoved other things to one side and ex-
plained that the allegations about the fear-
fully expensive cow were incorrect, and that
it had never cost more than 10s. weekly. It
was one of a few sent from the South for
demonstration purposes, and was kept at
Darwin to test the grand old assertion that
cows could not exist there. It, in common
with other Australian cows, had done so
well that two hotelkeepers had now two
cows apiece providing the stuff that goes so
well with tea and rum. As an example of
how careful Government House was of the
Departmental animal, His Ex. added that
on several occasions when no certificated
milker could be got, the milk-pump had been
worked by the Director of Agriculture in
person or by his own secretary. Then, having
dealt freely and frankly with the domestic
matter, His Ex. resumed his most severe
cocked-hat manner, and went out to tell the
wilderness not to howl so loudly.
The opening of the North Coast (N.S.W.)
railway to Taree will push one of the oldest
Australian coach lines out of a job. Dur-
ing the late ’3o’s a mail service was insti-
tuted between Stroud and the Manning
River by the A. A. Co., Stroud being at the
time the co.’s headquarters, and the whip
has cracked over that route ever since. The
early Jehus have apparently all gone; but
only about three months ago. the widow of
one of the first of them rejoined her long-
deceased spouse in the land where all travel-
ling is done per wing.
The tremendous advertisement of Jack
Johnson seems to have done a certain amount
of good in helping the white race to under-
stand its own racial feeling. Following the
announcement of the big nigger’s matrimonial
adventures, a Bill has been introduced into
the Georgia (U.S.A.) Legislature, forbid-
ding the marriage of black with white It
is a marvel that this is not already law in
o\ei\ white man’s country. The chances
are that if Rome had enacted it in the time
of Augustus, or earlier, the 133rd Caesar
would still be coilducting the concert of
Europe from the old stand by the Tiber.

“T.F.M.” : The Fisher Government has de-
creed that the naval station at Western Port
is to be known as the Flinders Naval Base.
But Flinders never entered Western Port;
and, in justice to the memory of the man
who was the first to do so, the time is ripe
for a ukase to the effect that Western Port
shall henceforward be known as Port Bass.
The present name was all right in the year
1798, when nothing was known of any ports
or harbors farther west, but to-day it is ab-
surd, and the alteration suggested, while
removing the absurdity, would be a tribute
to the memory of Bass, and not much of a
strain on the public purse.

THE MYSTERY OF NEVER TELL.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
In Australia Christmas has long been known as
the season of the drowning fatality. This pathetic
ballad is in line with old traditions.
The terraced garden, stooping down
To kiss the waters of delight,
Absorbed the shadows large and brown
Shed by the Summer robes of night;
Beyond, the energetic town
Was growing reasonably tight.
By various signs the star-beams knew
It was the happy Christmas Eve,
For numerous squeakers shrilly blew
A music fitted to bereave
Tf all their wits the giddy crew
Who strolled the streets of Make Believe.
But here upon the city’s skirt
A brooding silence seemed to lie,
Where earth-born lights were seen to flirt
With flashing glances from the sky,
And leaping waters tried to squirt
Their bright reflections in the eye.
The suburb of We Never Tell
Was full of whispers soft and clear—
Low-breathing notes that rose and fell
Like laughter sipping at a tear.
I thought the world went very well
Because the brown girl’s lips were near.
The garden creepers trailed above,
The waves were down beneath our feet,
It wanted but one little shove
To push us from our rocky seat,
And in the harbor damp a love
That, keeping dry, was very sweet.
The little shove arrived at nine.
We never knew what came behind;
Without a word, without a sign,
A something soft and large and blind
Butted our gladness in the spine
And made us think the world unkind.
The water round us all was wet;
The brown girl rose and shook her hair;
The rocks about the place were set
Up straight on edge and smooth and bare ;
I said, “I very much regret
There is no landing anywhere.”
She said, “Perhaps it were as well,
As we can neither of us swim,
To raise a little gentle yell
Before our parting eyes grow dim ;
I do not greatly like the smell
Of these cold wavelets sleek and slim.”
The loudest noise we could produce
Brought no one down to lend us aid ;
We found it not the slightest use
In such deep seas to try to wade ;
I said, “My soul is getting loose—
We’ll have to drown, I am afraid.”
The brown girl drowned at half-past nine,
Exactly where at first she fell;
Yet, circled by such love as mine,
She knew that everything was well;
She said that drowning was divine
Beneath the rocks of Never Tell.
Five minutes later I was dead,
Adrift upon the noiseless flood ;
I felt the cold wave steal the red
Out of my quite redundant blood ;
My large unnecessary head
Sank to its rest upon the mud.
The whole affair was somewhat sad,
But it was over by eleven ;
And, knowing death was near, we ha 1
Arranged to meet again in Heaven,
Where we arrived, and, duly clad,
Were called to prayers next morn at seven.
The suburb of We Never Tell
Was full of whispers soft and clear.
Low-breathing notes that rose and feli
Like laughter drinking down a tear,
But everything goes verv well
With Mabel and myself up here.
N.S.W.

“Foe” discovers a patriot:—
Gaud Mayor Clarke is not the only man who is
trying to straighten up Sydney. The other enthusiast
was found on a recent midnight in a street where
every third building is out of line with the two ad-
joining. He was trying to give the place a more
symmetrical appearance by putting his shoulder to
the protruding ones and shoving them back into line.
It took a lot of perspiration and grunting ; but when
his worn boot soles lost their grip of the paving-
stones and slid back, his red face assumed a beatific
expression. “Now she moves!” he would say. Then,
as he stretched out and rested on the tips of his toes,
“Whoa, there! Not too far back!” He told me he
once got a six-storey place fairly going, and had to
hang on to the front door-handle to keep it from
crashing into the back of the next-street skyscrapers.
He also whispered confidentially that, providing he
could hire a cab-horse, he intended to extract some
well-known pub from a main street-front, and tow it
away where no one would ever be able to find it.
The thought of what thirsty customers would say
when they came along for a wet drink and discovered
nothing but the dry foundations seemed to give him
an immense amount of joy.

bourne to two years and a dose of cat, said:
“I am perfectly certain that the lash has
good effect. I hold the opinion that whip-
pings are the only means to curb these vicious
indulgences.” And when the sex-maniac has
taken liis catting and served his two years,
and commits a similar crime on being re-
leased, Madden, in sentencing him to more

the same thing over again- Sq they go on
repeating their errors, criminals and judges
alike.
From a Good Australian :
Gradually the superiority of Australian timber is
being recognised by the people who own it. ine
new State Savings Bank in Melbourne, with perhaps
the finest banking chamber in Australasia, and bette.
than anything of the kind m London, is entirely
fitted with Australian wood. Tasmanian blackwood
of the fiddle-back variety has been used as the
panelling of the dado in the big public chamber with
fine artistic effect. The graining of the designs ha*
been splendidly done by Mother Nature, a wood-
worker of great antiquity. Australian oak and las-
manian myrtle are used for all the fittings of the
building, which stands as an object-lesson to aid the
infinite development of the home timber industry.
We have the best woods in the world here, and their
free use in architecture is only prevented by a. bad
but fast-dving tradition that the foreigner can always
do it better. I have seen some fine buildings built
and decorated with wood in foreign parts, especially
in California, and claim the right to say the new
Melbourne Savings Bank building has few superiors
as regards internal decoration. The contract was
carried through by a well-known Adelaide firm, which
specialises in this class of work. May one hope the
Federal capital Commission will spend a morning in-
specting this latest triumph of Australian workmen
and material. The pity is that none of our rare
marbles have been used in the building. Some day
we’ll have a palace of our own, built of our own
wood and marble, to excel in grandeur the Parthenon,
with St. Peter’s at Rome and the Congressional Library
at Washington filling second and third places.
At the Melbourne District Court a few
days ago, the wife of a Gippsland specimen
of the Great Backbone sued him for mainte-
nance. She informed the Court that she had
been starved and beaten by her husband.
She had had to eat wombats, porcupines,
wallabies and wild pigs. The Bench was so
overcome that, with scowls in its voice, it
ordered the backbone to shell out ten (10)
shillings a week for the upkeep of a whole
wife. Instead of porcupines and wombats,
the fortunate woman will now be able to
browse on rich soups, fish, fowl . and the
delicacies of the season —that is, if she has
one meal a week, and doesn’t pay for lodg-
ing and steals necessary clothing. If she
wants three meals a day she’ll have only
about sd. a time to see her through. Even
porcupines cost more than fivenence.
Complaint is made that the tariff may not
be high enough to exclude Japanese soan.
But need we be nervous —don’t they say it
won’t wash clothes?

“Kodak”: A man who was convicted of
stealing a parcel from a Victorian railway
station was recently recommended to mercy,
on the ground that the utter carelessness of
the railway officials in leaving the parcel
lying on the platform put more than a reason-
able amount of temptation in his way. His
Honor, commenting on this, said “the facts
disclosed a very loose method of dealing
with parcels.” “Loose” is the right word.
I once saw a gentle-mannered old man ap-
proach a busy Victorian porter with a
neatly-tied parcel which he wished to des-
patch to Gohang. He carefully affixed the
stamps and delivered the treasure into the
porter’s hands. That official looked it over
calmly and dispassionately ; then he howled
to a remote confederate, “Bill —Go’ang !” and
flung the thing over his shoulder. It whizzed
across the office, and, hitting the handle of
a churn, burst like a bomb and poured its
contents into a large basket. I shall never
forget the expression on the consignor’s
wrinkled face as he darted round the coun-
ter and rescued the fragments. The porter
merely looked reproachfully at the stooping
ancient’s back, and drawled, by way of reply
to his muttered threats, “Gawblime, Dad,
we carn’t pars things roun’ on trays!” On
the whole, it was the most dignified rebuke
I ever heard administered under trying con-
ditions.

E. Y. P.: " Oh , how sweet! And is the other

The High Court now lays it down that
the mere fact that there have been negotia-
tions between employees and employers is
not in itself evidence of a dispute warrant-
ing the interference of the Arbitration
Court. This places the principle of Arbi-
tration in the central edifice of Queer-street,
the main thoroughfare in the town of
Muddle. It affords the latest illustration,
also, of the way in which the law can make
a travesty of a good thing by its habit of
being an ass. Arbitration in industrial
matters was invented as a method by which
strikes might be avoided; and the decision
that a strike must occur before arbitration
can be brought to bear seems about the
limit in folly. A similar decision in regard
to the first Arbitration Act in N. S. Wales
placed a large number of unions out of
court, and pretty well settled the usefulness
of that Act; and it is scarcely wonderful,
seeing the way in which industrial laws
have been hashed by lawyers, that unions are
inclined to view the whole process with a
cold eye and a tired feeling.

“S.S.” : King George reads a chapter of the
Bible every day. I can show you the cable
to prove it. Having kept this up for 30-
odd years, George has now read the good
book through about 10 times. Why con-
tinue? His Gracious should know the whole
thing from Genesis to Revelation, yet he
keeps on doing it. Plainly George V. has
the pretty general English opinion that
Rible-reading is a sort of penance, only to
be endured because it is less painful than
whipping one’s own back, and not so awk-
ward as dried peas in one’s boots.

I have taught school
in most parts of Vic-
toria (by the way, the
technical term tor
teaching is “running
a tannery”), and it
has been interesting
to study the effect en-
vironment has on nip-
pers. The greatest en-
joyment I have got
from my job was i n
the slum districts o f
Melbourne. The young-
sters, ever ready to
pull my leg out of its
sockets if I let them
get hold of it, were at
the same time exceed-
ingly quick to see any-
thing 1 had to show
them. Some of them
had a wonderful com-

one or two were ex-
ceedingly expert with
an inkwell as a mis-
sile and me as a tar-

pleasure to teach
them. In agricultural
districts they were
generally stodgy, re-
spectable and well-
behaved. They were
always smoodging to
the teacher, and be-
lieved the yarn about
George. Washington. In
grazing lands they
were stupid and docile
as sheep, lingering
long and languidly
over their lessons.
The most trying of
all were children i n
mining districts. Most
of them were “point-
ers,” and the rest
were “slacker s,”
cheeky as members of
Parliament and truth-
ful as Abo-liars. In
cow country they were
blasf; at 14. The
mingling with the
“cow squeezers” o n
their fathers’ farms
had given them an intimate knowledge of certain sub-
jects not included in the curriculum. But, apart from
this, they were cow-stupid—talked cow, smelt cow,
lived cow.
On top of repeated “strikes” of scholars
at having to attend overcrowded and dilapi-
dated schools in Victoria comes a piece of
cool neglect which should earn somebody the
sack. By the Amending Education Act of
1911 the State undertook to provide 200
scholarships, worth £l2 each for three years,
for State scholars, to be enjoyed at public
or approved private secondary or technical
schools or colleges; and Director Tate and
the National Council of Education have
more than once urged the Minister to give
the necessary authority to go ahead. Yet.
so far, not one penny has been voted, and
both Treasurer Watt and Education Minis-
ter Billson seem as void of information on
the matter as though they had been dead
when the Act was passed. The whole policy,
in fact, is in line with Toryism’s habitual
attitude towards education, as crystallised
by the late Bent: “It’s all going out and
nothing coming in; therefore it doesn’t
pay.” Besides, when the clever son of the
poor man has the same chance of superior
education as Moneybag’s offspring, the lat-

SUMMER GOES MISSING.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Yesterday. . . .the southerly gale was blowing with
renewed vigor.— Daily paper.
The gale it whistles a dreary tune;
The fine sand flies on the wind-whipped dune ;
Summer has gone from the furrowed sea
And the white wave-teeth gleam savagely;
The black clouds, trailing their robes of rain,
Sail up like Grief from the south again,
And veil the rage of the troubled sea
That snarling runs like a pack set free.
There’s nothing doing on coastal bars,
Where king waves lift to the watching stars ;
The south-bound liners are charging through ;
The Summer boarder looks very blue,
For, lacking Summer, his life is vain ;
With nose pressed close to the streaming pane
He stands on watch, with a vacant stare,
And whistles a strange and dismal air.
In blue December ’tis very weird
To see each sea with a Christmas beard,
And hear the icy wind piping shrill
By wet-faced headland and sun-browned hill.
The Winter wraps from the moth snatched back
Are thick in the streets. Umbrellas black
Remind us of Wowsers, coughs, cold feet,
Where has it gone, our Christmas heat?
Oh! for a dentist, hearty and hale,
To draw the teeth of the Christmas gale 1
N.S.W.
FOE.
SEEING HIM OFF.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Good-bye, Old Year ! Good-bye !
What are you taking away ?
You wink your rheumy eye,
And tell some merciful lie,
Like a voyager wise and gay,
Who smiles while he says you nay.
Too well do I know the simple truth:
You’ve got a bit of my dwindling youth,
Another tooth and a hank of hair,
A few illusions I could not spare,
A heap of chances, alas, I’ve missed,
A friend or two and a girl unkissed,
A heap of money, a pile of bills—-
Such treasure and rubbish your kit bag fills.
Ah ! I would detain you and dig and delve.
But see! at the gates
The New Year waits,
And smiles and lifts
His trunk of new gifts!
Good-bye Nineteen Hundred and Twelve!
N.S.W.

“Fitzwilliam”: “Yarrum” (B. 5/12/T2)
is talking through his neck about the Kiama Blow-
hole. Of course, nobody wants to be told that the
action of water will erode rock in time, but the
Kiama rocks are as hard as the knocker of Hades;
and “Yarrum” and I will both be dust, and municipal
councils things of the remote and forgotten past,
long before there is any perceptible difference in the
Blowhole, bar earthquakes and volcanoes. What is a
very short time in a geologist’s estimation is a
dashed long one in anybody else’s. Further down
the coast at Kiama there is another blowhole that U
not so well known as its big relative. It is small
enough to sit on, if anvone is anxious to try foolish
experiments, but it is a lusty baby all the same. Some
years ago I was fool enough to try to fill it up.
I spent the whole of one long Sunday afternoon feed-
ing it with big stones, and after I had dumped in
between three and four tons, it apparently gave up
the ghost, and I piled a cairn over it as a sign of
victory. But a week later, though there had been
no storm in the interval, every scrap of stone had

I don’t know what the big Blowhole would be capable

what would happen.
“Eddyson”: They had been christening
the new mill of the Forty Five mine at Hawksnest
and the men were scattered on the shady side of 1
batterv house, an erect bottle by each recumbent
figure, discussing the world, the flesh and the
devil—especially women. The stranger was hover-
ing in the middle distance. He had been hovering
for over an hour, with two sorties Once, Hankin
the driver, knocked him over the knuckles with a
spanner when trying to nip half a bottle of claret
through a chance hole in the wall; once William-
son booted him into the blanket tub w^ en ae "‘I s
drifting off with his (Williamson’s) bottle up h-.s
vest He was a tallish, thin, dilapidated man
dressed in black. He had been clean-shaven as re-
cently as Sunday. This was Friday. His high-but-
toned vest had a clerical nick in it, and he was pa e
and woebegone, with the furtive all 0 a man
accustomed to adventures with dogs. He was ven-
turing nearer. After several signals of distress, and
with a propitiatory cough or two, he came within
range. “Gentlemen,” he said, “I stand sorely in
need of some refreshment, which I am willing to
earn.” “Want a job?” growled Williamson. “No,
no,” answered the weary fragment with some trepi-
dation, “but I will tell you what I will do. I will
preach you a very agreeable sermon for a bottle of
beer.” The suggestion offered divertisement, and
Weigall said “Done!” The waif and stray en-
trenched himself behind a large packing-case, ami
waded in. He preached an ordinary sort of second-
hand sermon, such as you might hear any Sunday,
giving our manifold sins and wickednesses the usual
three-minute dust-up. He attacked drink, and big-
amy, and wife-beating, giving each iniquity a brief
spar, and wound up with an eloquent peroration.
Then he took up his beer and bestowed himself
behind a tree. Everybody noticed a change in
Weigall. Previously he had been the hardest drinker,
as well as the most splenetic swearer of the bunch,
at Hawksnest. He had been credited with pasting
the missus with a shingle, too, and he was sudden
and quick in quarrel, and prompt to do evil. For
five weeks he was a moody and disgruntled wretch,
given much to solitary musings, sober, amenable to
discipline in the domestic circle, and guilty of
cavernous groaiiings in working hours. When it
was discovered that on three occasions he had
walked five miles to morning service at Piedisli, the
miners concluded that he- was very sick. Then came
a fine Saturday afternoon and an outbreak of man-
slaughter at the back of the hut. Ten men rushed
to quell the riot, and found Weigall and the weary
fragment entangled in argument. The wandering
minister had returned to Hawksnest to his own
undoing. The decadent preacher it was who was
making all the noise; Weigall was too full for
utterance and too busy working mischief all over
the hapless theologian to waste energy in superflu-
ous exclamation. When his mates tore Joseph off
his prey the ex-cleric was just a demoralised
bundle, the distinguishing feature of which was two
black eyes. “Lemme at him!” yelled Weigall.
“Don’t let him go!” he roared. “I’ll kill him!
I’ll break his blinded neck!” “Hold up, Jo,” said
Williamson. “What’s the pore bloke done to you?”
“What’s he done?” thundered Weigall. “What’s
he done? That’s the knock-kneed
bleeder what converted me!”

“Genung”: A few years ago every Vic-
torian railway station was plastered with notices,
“Smoking strictly prohibited.” In Bendilarat lived
a white-whiskered old anti-tobacco crank,
who haunted the local railway station
looking for smokers. On finding one, he
would bear down upon him and demand
that he should put out his pipe at once
or he would be given in charge. This
went on for years. Then, one evening,
the nuisance saw a tubby, fashionably-
dressed man sauntering along, with a
cigar between his lips. Nothing pleased
old Whiskers more than tackling one of
the Upper Ten. He lined up to him.
“Don’t you know',” he said, “that smok-
ing is prohibited on railway stations.
Can’t you read! Put out that cigar at
once.” The smoker puffed and gazed ab-
stractedly ahead, as if he neither saw nor
heard. This infuriated old killjoy to such
an extent that he snatched the cigar from
the smoker’s lips, and threw it out on the
permanent way, right under the wheels of
an approaching train. Then he awaited
developments. But Shiny Hat smiled
blandly at him and turned away. Next
week every one of the offensive notice-
boards was taken down, and nobody
missed them except Old Nark, who went
home and died of a busted job. The
tubby man he had bearded was Tait, the
Commissioner of Railways. Now every-
body smokes who wants to. I guess old
Whiskers is smoking also.
“Vic”: Buchner, a scientist, is just back
from Barren Island in Bass Strait. Its people form
the last link with the Tasmanian aborigines. There
are only nine family groups, but they include 150
persons. The hard-living men who established them-
selves on this island went there for sealing, took Tas-
manian aboriginal women and settled down, Captain
Phil Thomas is nowadays the principal islander. He
is the son of another Captain Thomas and an abori-
ginal. Thomas, jun., married a half-caste, and one
of his sons has married a quadroon. A son of this
last union is very dark and a daughter very white.
As for living, Buchner mentions that one family of
five during the mutton-bird season regularly makes
about £l2O in six weeks. Some of the half-castes
are of a decidedly superior type, but others are in-
ferior, the result probably of descent from convicts of
a bad order. Tuberculosis is prevalent, and has
got worse of late, which is no doubt one reason why
the inhabitants show little attachment for the poor
place.

“Gouger”: It is a strange fact that the
mountain springs all give out more water in the
mornings than in the evenings. In tin sluicing, if
the only available water is from springs, the pressure
is always strong until about four o'clock in the after-
noon, when the water begins to slack out. Whether
the springs have a union, and refuse to work long
hours, I don't know. But anyhow, they “go easy”
from the early evening until daylight next shift.

“Aslew”: About the "Send it down,
Hughie” of the shearer and cocky (B. 14/11/’l2,
5/12/’l2). I heard the expression used first many
years ago by a blue nose A.B. on the ship Yarkand,
outward bound to Rangoon. We were in the dol-
drums, and everybody was waiting for a wash —
body and clothes —as was (and I suppose is) the
custom on the lime-juicer, with its “three quarts of
water, two for the cook.” I have since heard it in
different parts of Australia. Up Gunnedah way it
was, “Send it down, Jimmie.” Perhaps they referred
to James Pannikin, Esq.

“Yarrum”: If “Chump” can find that 1
made use of the words he attributes to me (B.
5/12/’l2), I’ll shout a trip (first-class) for him from
Sydney to Narrandera, and there I’ll show him the
wood ducks assisting their young ones back to the
nest in the way I described. On the other hand, if
he fails, I shall expect him to part up for me; and
I think that is a fair go. He speaks of me, too,
as a Clontarf ornithologist. In answer to that reck-
less charge, I must plead guilty to having been to
Clontarf only once in my life, and thereby hangs a
tale. I shall tell him all about it on the way to
Narrandera, when I hope to be his guest. His other
charges are about as cranky as the first I dealt with ;
so that I shall leave them out if he doesn’t mind. In
conclusion, I would like to ask “Chump” if he knows
what Carlyle once said about the man who mis-
quotes.

To city men motor cycles are not a necessity, to
the farmer they are. The day’s work o’er he can
bestride his machine and “hie away’’ to town for
amusement. Should “Mother’s” groceries become un-
expectedly depleted he mounts his trusty steed and
soon returns. During busy harvests a broken bolt
or casting is replaced from town most speedily. It
used to take hours to harness the family horse and
make the trip. Phillips Ormonde and Co.’s, Lom-
bard Buildings, 17 Queen Street, Melbourne.*

FIXING THEIR RELATIONS.

“He Toka”: The crabs at Yamba(N.S.W.)
know a thing or two. Saw one crawl up and wait
for an oyster to open. As soon as the lid was
raised the crab pushed in a small stone quickly.
Then he spat on his hands, and started to drag the
tenant from the premises. And I’m nearly sure he
didn’t do. it merely to give the tenant a pleasant
change of air.
“Stohr Ruddell”: The youthful country
police do their share towards blocking decentralisa-
tion. the swag is, to an extent, a necessary evil
with the man who makes to the bush for work;
but the raw young recruit, fresh from the barracks,
marks it as the insignia of the loafer and the beer-
chewer. He (with great emphasis) will put it
down. In the small river towns which the habitual
do-nothings, as well as the honest, swag-humping
work-seekers, make their retreat, the ignorance of
young law-an’-order is a menace. He is let loose
amongst 500 or 600 people with his new uniform,
and he has been told or led to believe that he's there
to do things. How can he show what he has done
unless he produces a lengthy charge-sheet? If, how-
ever, he dashes out in an indiscriminate pursuit of
lawbreakers, more especially as regards sobriety, he
is pulled up immediately he lays hand on ’ the
wavering miller, the shire secretary, or the butcher
who is also the Jay Pay. If he pinches the son’
brother, aged parent, or second-cousin of any of the
aforesaid, he is publicly decapitated—or moved. So
he falls back in haste on the man with the swag,
who is an easy win, a walk-over, and a long-odds
chance. The bagman comes from anywhere, and has
no idea where he will spend Eternity. He may
have been chopping hardwood timber for eight or
10 months, and now he wants relief. According to
his temperament and the disposition of Bung and
Bung’s sparrers, he grows joyful, voluble or fight-
able. In any case, he is hilarious. At the summit
of his magnificence he is rudely grabbed by a lean
young man in civilian dress. He cleaves the air
with his hands in protest, and shocks the ears of
people within hearing. Eventually he is overpow-
ered through the commendable assistance of the
local banker and the boy who peels spuds at the
pub, and next day he is sent along for three months
in default of an impossible fine. That town gets a
black mark in the Bauman's Gazette, and sends
others to blew their cneque in Sydney, “where a
man can bash the old woman cheaper.” The irony
of it all is that the “dinkum” tramp—the übiquit-
ous, useless beer-sparrer and borrower of threepenny-
bits—never becomes over-jovful, fightable or volu-
ble When he can’t get any more, he toddles off
to his “knot,” and he worries the Wowser only when
he places an exorbitant value on his labor.
Binghi’s census, taken in N. S. Wales
last year, discloses that he numbered 7329 all told.
Of these only 2076 were full-bloods (9.35 males, 564
females and 577 children). The total showed an
increase of 372 abos. over the figures of 1910,
although the difficulties of catching Binghi to count
him are so great that nothing is more than approxi-
mate. The first round-up took place in 1882, when
the full-bloods numbered 6540, or 4464 more than
last year. In the same 19-year period the half-
castes increased by 2874. Counting full-bloods and
half-castes, there has been a falling away of 1590.

“L.J.P.”; The line that separates “the in-
side” from the Never-Never retreats fast towards
the setting sun. Not so many years
ago it was commonly supposed to be
somewhere about the Bogan or
Bourke; but to-day, from an Outback
point of view, those places are well
within the suburban area, while the
same remark applies to the Warrego,
Barcoo and the Thompson. The line
has anchored in turn at all of those
centres; but, with the coming of rail-
roads and population—especially that
population which wears tan boots and
smokes cigarettes—-it invariably rolls
up its swag and shifts further out.
Even away back on the Diamantina,
with its traditions of rough-living and
hard-drinking, a distinct air of re-
spectability commences to pervade the
atmosphere. Telephones and motors
have annihilated distance, and it is
now quite the thing to see squatters
and managers viewing the scenery and
admiring their woollies from up-to-
date cars. Another thing that has in-
vaded the West —out as far, almost,
as there is any West—is the gramo-
phone. There is hardly a shanty, way-
side pub or accommodation house of
any pretension whatever but has one
of these; and Mulga Jim can now
knock down his cheque to the accom-
paniment of the most popular songs
of the day, sung by the world’s lead-
ing vocalists. Furthermore, he has
the additional privilege, should any
one of them offend his refined taste, of
saying blankv and turning on another.
So the old order giveth place to the
new, and it would be hard to say
where Outback really does begin now.
Personally, I have a hazy notion that it starts from
somewhere about Boulia, where snake-juice and other
liquid refreshments cost the weary wayfarer a bob
a time, and where (on my oath) a chap asked me
only a few months ago whether the Boer war was
over yet.
* * * *
“A.McI.”: Hugh McColl, the father of
irrigation in Victoria, had nothing to do with the
origin of the bush saying, “Send it down, Hughie!”
when rain is falling or is wanted. I heard it first
as a boy in the navy in ’B5, and about once a week
for 14 years after. “Old Hughie” would be “hav-
ing a go” when it rained a bit out of the ordinary ;
and when it meant “pipe down” for a day or two
(which it often did on the West Indies station)
we would sing “good o 1 e Hughie,” parodying
Mother Shipton. I have also heard “Elijah” spoken
of in the same manner, and I think it probable that
“Hughie” is a corruption of ’Lijali.
‘Hamer”: A new imported curse, or sus-
pected curse, has been discovered by the Wagga
(N.S.W.) Municipal Council. The silver poplar
(Populus Alba) is accused of causing hay fever, and
inciting the populace to consumption, and the coun-
cillors intend to have the suspected vegetables re-
moved. At certain times in the year the silver pop-
lar casts off showers of fluffy stuff, which float into
Wagga’s lungs, and are supposed to cause the ills
of which the river town says it has far too much.
Its guilt has not yet been definitely settled, but the
trees are to go on the off-chance.

“Drummer”: Re “6-tooth’s” par. (R.
5/12/’l2). As one who toils at the sheep-barbering
profession, I push this down the shoot. I have never
seen a sheep’s eye gouged out, and very rarely a
jugular cut. Anyway, a dissatisfied majority on a
wet-sheep question don’t organise a “flying cut” ; and
if they did, and “rose” a bit, they would be more
likely to miss eyes and jugulars, and would lift less
pelt. A deliberate eye-gouger or jugular-cutter would
be firmly told about it, and if the boss missed him,
the other barbers would say nasty things, not in news-
papers, but to his face. That’s my experience. If
“6-tooth” wants to air humanitarian views, let him
tell truths about those who do not crutch when neces-
sary, but prefer to wait for the shearer to do two
jobs for one job’s price. Strong men turn sick and
faint at their work in flyblown sheep. They growl
for their own sake. Now let “6-tooth” roar for the
agony-carrying sheep’s sake. They need a lot of
friends in their purgatory.

“Yam” : I can’t say what sort of soup the
tortoise makes, but I know he makes a first-class
fool. Last week I saw scores of him stuck against
wire-netting fences along the Mitta Mitta. During
recent flopds they left their haunts, and are now
making back to them over dry land. But when they
strike the netting they will not retreat, nor will
they turn to right or left. They just wait for the
fence to shift, and when it doesn’t shift, they
perish.

“Niuafo’ou” blows in from Tonga:—
How these tourists get their limbs pulled on the
mailboats in the Pacific, and what lovely things
Wwritei to cheer up the lonely traders in the
south Seas! Head a specially interesting article by
a lady in a Sydney paper recently. We, at Niuafo’ou,
were greatly touched to be called “the few solitary
ones for whom the eyes. grow misty and the throat
contracts, but we would have been better pleased
if the lady had mentioned the exact position of the
stone figures of the woman, with the child on her
back and the pig at her heels. We’ve raked right
across the five miles of island without finding them.
And the malu bird? The lady says it is “about
the size of a sparrow,” and, in arranging its nest
digs a perpendicular hole about eight to 10 feet ■
then it digs horizontally for the same distance: it
then lays its eggs, 100 in number.” in other words
a four-ounce bird lays 12-ounce eggs to the extent of
about a cwt! Shades of a Yankee poultry farm
advt. ! We had the impression that the bird was
the size of a large pigeon, and that its eggs were
nearly the same in body as a turkey’s. We have
found 30 or 40 eggs in the community-burrows in the
sand, but we had hitherto credited 30 or 40 hens
with the output. Perhaps we were wrong. Most of
us have been only 10 years or so on this dot, any-
way, and can’t expect to know as much as the glad
boys and girls on the mailboats.
“Crusoe” : There are about 800 people on
King Island in Bass Strait, nearly all of-them self-
important cockies from Britain or adjacent Vic-
toria. As. a type they are quite distinct from that
of the mainland. A stranger finds it practically im-
possible to make friends with them. In fact, unless
he pals up with the barman in the island’s solitary
pub he is likely to remain a stranger until he de-
parts. There 'is none of that open-banded hospi-
tality which is a feature of some parts of Aus-
tralia s agricultural and pastoral country—none of
the genuine, cheery welcome that characterises the
big Australian. Dunno the cause, unless it is
the isolation. Sometimes there is no news from the
outside world for a fortnight at a stretch, and the
oil sort of dries on the hinges of the intellect.
That’s the only explanation I can offer, and it is
not advanced so much by way of explanation as by
way of excuse.
THE HUS[?] AT PIKER'S PUB.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
A hush prevailed at Piker s pub. A strange religious
glow
Suffused the bar, and played across each brown
and bearded face,
And smoothed away the lines of care, the scars of
old disgrace ;
It only showed the excellence (or what was left to
show)
Of all that was commendable uncounted years ago.
Outside, the night was strewn with stars. A nmlga-
scented breeze
Awoke the sullen leaves from sleep on each scrub
silhouette,
And stirred the dust, as kisses stir a slumberous
coquette,
From where it lay throughout the calm of daylight's
languid ease.
Indoors, the Girl from Sydney-side no longer
strummed the keys.
The very bottles on the shelves became as holy things ;
The glasses on the counter like Byzantine sculp-
tures gleamed,
And from cathedral windows might their opal
tints have streamed ;
The bar-room lithographs, that showed how beggar
maids met kings,
Grew not unlike the Angelos the hook-nosed hawker
brings.
ihe painted girl from Sydney-side became demure
and shy ;
Her ample bosom heaved beneath her amulet of
paste—
But it were sacrilege to look, or her red kisses
taste.
Her perfumed presence was a thing the worst might
sanctify,
And when she passed it seemed as though Madonna
ventured by.
Had deep repentance liapt along to Piker’s lone
hotel ?
Had ev'ry sinner seen his faults, and turned a
brand-new leaf?
Had ancient Folly vanished like the Desert’s tented
thief?
At last had \\ isdom, long-deferred, vouchsafed its
lesson well?
Had Conscience, with the still small voice, invaded
each man’s hell ?
Would Piker close his shutters and incontinently
flee—
Explaining that contingencies of trade had forced
him to,
And adding that denial is a thing men over-do?
Alas! that indications with the truth should dis-
agree—
That holy calm, was but the hush that comes before
the spree !

F. J Thomas, general manager of the
Newcastle and Hunter River S. N Co has
been appointed a director. Thomas joined
the old Hunter River Co. in 1860, and
wRWv. chief” when it amalgamated
with the Newcastle Co. in 1892. Advancing
years has led him to exchange his old job
tor the more ornamental billet of director
, the co., whose solid position is verv
largely due to his skilful trick at the wheel.
William Durrant, a baronet, whose ances-
wui-are !?: ld X° have reached England with
William the Conqueror, has died at North
Harbor, Sydney, aged 73 years. He was an
jton boy, and had at one time a commission
as lieutenant in the 15th Foot. Coming to
Australia in 1862 as a consumptive, he got
lid of the affliction, dabbled in sugar and in
sheep, and at one time edited a country
newspaper. In 1875 his elder brother died
without issue, and he thus came in for the
title, in which he is succeeded by a son —a
clerk in a shipping firm’s service.

From the Second-Oldest Inhabitant:—
A wedding of some Australian interest was cele-
brated the other day in Melbourne. The bride was
a daughter of Mr. and Mrs. Edward Mitchell, of Fan-
court, East Melbourne, and granddaughter of William
Henry Fancourt Mitchell, a man of some note in the
early history of Victoria. This Mitchell was the
son of the vicar of St. Mary’s and All Saints’,
Leicester (Eng.), and chaplain to, the Duke of York.
He arrived in Van Diemen’s Land young, and for a
time • was _ Acting Colonial Secretary. Crossing to
Port Phillip, about the year 1840, he “squatted”
in the Kvneton district. When the gold rushes broke
out Governor Latrobe asked him to take on the job
of Chief Commissioner of Police, with almost un-
limited powers. He introduced the cadet system, by
promising young fellows commissions and handsome
uniforms and plenty of bushranger-hunting and escort
duty. Edmund Fosbery, ex-inspector General of
N. S. Wales police, was one of those he snared. Pre-
sently Mitchell went to England on leave, and Capt.
McMahon succeeded him. Returning to the colony,
Mitchell entered politics, became a member of the
Leg. Council, and sat there till his death; in later
days he was President. The mother of the bride
is a Geelong Morrison. The bridegroom is a young
Irishman, son of Captain Armit, of Castle Jordan,
King’s County.
Concerning an uncommon art collector:—
The art collection of the late George MacCulloch
is to be sold in London next March. The collection
is a notable instance of how Art can be made to pay
good dividends. MacCulloch was the bachelor man-
ager of Mount Gipps sheep station for his uncle,
James MacCulloch, a former Premier of Victoria.
Mount Gipps includes Broken Hill, and Geo«ge was
there when that uninteresting knoll was first found
to contain silver-lead. He had received a fair educa-
tion in Glasgow, having intended to be a chemist
and druggist. Being the station boss, he naturally
became the chairman of the Broken Hill syndicate,
and he added share to share. (There were only 16
altogether.) When his big fortune was assured, he
drifted to London, bought a freehold near the Im-
perial Institute, and built a house designed to hold
pictures. The dining-room was octagonal, the vista
through each of the eight arches ending in a master-
piece hanging on the wall of the adjacent room.
Wherever one sat at table an art treasure met the
eye. At dinner MacCulloch used to entertain mem-
bers of the Royal Academy, and from them learnt not
only the argot of Art, but the practical business
points of buying pictures, and, incidentally, selling
them. He had a fine sense of coming greatness in a
rising artist, and always laid in a stock of cheap
pictures by one whom he judged was on the point
of arriving. Also he watched the setting suns, and
often bought their best works as they were nearing
sunset. For instance, at Christie and Manson’s pic-
ture sale room, one afternoon, he bought five Vicat
Coles at what looked like high prices. But Cole was
dead within a week, and his works doubled in value.
MacCulloch had casually learned that the artist was
ill. During the last years of his life this shrewd
art-dealer made a lot of money buying and selling
pictures, and, naturally, since his “coming” men
didn t always arrive, his collection is streaky. Among
some poor things it includes the very best specimens
of modren art.
