Dated this fourteenth day of November, one thou-
sand nine hundred and twelve.
_A cheerful innovation was disclosed in the
will of Robert Wilson, late of Mystic Park,
near Kerang (Vic.), who lived lonely and
unmarried till he reached the age of 84,
and left £10,763, mostly real estate. His
only near relative was a nephew, “last
known of at Northumberland, England,” to
whom he bequeathed 762 acres of fairish
land. The remainder, some 2600 acres, he
parcelled out among his half-dozen long-time
tenants.
“B.” on an unsteady monarch :
Tim Coghlan’s hold on the N.S.W. Agent-General’s
office has nearly always been precarious. Indeed, since
he earned his first pound in N.S.W. Government em-
ploy, he has always been in dread of the Irishman’s
“rise,” and must often have wished he had stuck to
engineering. It was Dibbs, Jennings and Dailey who
encouraged the keen, clear-headed young outsider to
jump into the Civil Service; and simply because
these did it, Parkes vowed that he would fire him
out. So Tim put his head down and did the work
of three men, and Parkes, when he gained authority,
filed his vow with his lOU’s, and naturally forgot all
about it. But Tim was still a-tremble. When Reid
took the Premiership Tim started off to do the work
of four men, and, to his surprise, Yes-No loaded him
with favors. These culminated in the mission to
London, which afterwards led to the Agent-General-
ship. Tim, however, still rolled in the pickle of fear.
Constitutionally, he is a man who looks ahead, and
tries to secure a future, but the winds of political
change turned his sails inside out and his plans up-
side down. McGowen last week informed reporters
that he has been pestering the Government to learn
what is to become of him at the end of March ; but
McGowen’s Government is in such a stew that it
hardly knows where itself will be at the end of
March. Therefore, something temporary has again
been rigged up.

sea sickness,” which prevented the completion of his

syndicate—a powerful combination in the days when
Governor Arthur did pretty well as he liked. One
of Arthur’s nephews was a member of the syndicate,
and quite likely was a mere dummy for his uncle, the
Governor. That Fawkner could pay 20s. in the £
no one who _ knows his circumstances can doubt.

Cornwall Inn at Launceston, was a newspaper pro-
prietor, pleaded in the court known as “The Little-
(f, m0 H debts, etc.), and was constantly mixing
with the public. It was to the Cornwall that John
Batman went to make his boast of being the “largest
landowner in the world,” referring to his supposed
purchase of the Yarra territory from the natives. It
was just the sort of thing that Batman would do
it he knew (as no doubt he did) that Fawkner had
announced all along his intention to settle on the
Yarra. As a reading man (and that he was a reader
has never been doubted) Fawkner knew of Grimes’s
survey, and availed himself of the knowledge Fawk-
ner s services have been belittled by James Bonwick,
the early Vic. historian. But Bonwick never liked
1- awkner—probably, I think, because the latter
called public attention to the alleged lax performance
of some of Mr. Bonwick’s duties as a public school
inspector. Yet even Bonwick can't destroy Fawk-
ner s claim that his money and h'is men built the
first house m Melbourne, and that his vessel, the
Enterprise, was the first to enter the basin of the

GROVER,

“SUN.”
Marion Phillips, M.A., D.Sc., a Mel-
bourne University girl who is a sister of
lawyer Arthur Phillips, is making a bit of
history for herself. She is a Councillor for
Kensington, London, having been returned
in the Labor interest at the municipal elec-
tions last month.
Carlyle Valentine Ilosswood Johnston
the Labor candidate for Blaynev (N.S W )
in opposition to Beeby, is satisfied as a
general thing to travel light as Carl John-
ston, and under that style has become fairly
well known in the N.S.YV. Labor movement
fie is a young man (about 30, which is
young as politicians go) of apparent possi-
bilities lie was one of the Labor candi-
dates tor Belmore in the recent Sydney
municipal elections, and though unsuccessful
be did good work. He is well known in the
Llayney electorate, having lived there or
thereabouts most of his life. He has an
absorbent brain for political points and a
share of platform ability ; also a sufficientlv
taking way with him off stage to attract
the fancy of electors who bother about men
rather than matters. Until recently John-
ston was at Portland (N.S.W.) as secretary
ot the Cement Workers’ Union. He left
that job to come to Sydney with a view to
qualifying for the horsehaired profession.
Ben Rounsevell, who was Treas-
urer of S. Aus. as much as 32
yoais &go, and waved his long
beard in half a dozen subsequent
Ministries, was induced to come
out of a more or less Rip Van
Winkle retirement the other day
and become Mayor of Glenelg. The
vigorous old “sport” passed his 70th
milestone a few months ago: but
he lives in a State where the sep-
tuagenarian is liable to be a mere
youngster. Until the other day
there was an Adelaide man in the
quarry business who signed himself
(say) William Brown, junior
though he was 70-odd. Then his
father died and he had to drop the
tag.
Delegates to the recent International Art
Congress at Dresden—there were 2500 of
them wore colored badges to indicate what
language they spoke. White represented
German; red, French; and blue, English—
mese being the Congress languages. A. R
Coffey, the N.S.W. Education Department’s

THE HARD CASE OF J. JONES.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The question was asked in the House of Repre-
sentatives, yesterday, whether consideration had been
given to the compulsory and efficient training of
young women in first aid and hospital training to
enable them to render service to their country in
time of war? The reply was that such training" was
not considered necessary. —Daily paver.
Jones as a youthling very raw
Went out to earn his daily bread ;
Each hour Life hit him on the jaw
And drove some sense into his head.
Large men and stout all yelled at Jones;
He jumped some twenty times each day ;
He wrote and ran, he answered ’phones,
And drew his microscopic pay.
Black murder surged in his young heart;
He dreamed of bosses shot and hung;
But still he played his humble part,
And learnt his work, and held his tongue.
And so it came to pass, the lad,
Compelled by Want to train for Life,
One day a place of honor had
And took a pretty girl to wife.
Now Mrs. Jones had lived at home,
And never worked for strangers who
Could stamp and roar, and rage and foam,
If things were not attended to.
She “ordered” with a regal air,
But hated orders to receive ;
Advice she “simply couldn’t bear”;
She never murmured “By your leave 1”
Then she was ignorantly proud,
Intolerant and snobbish, too ;
She patronised the vulgar crowd
And worshipped blood she thought was blue.
She sewed “a bit” and cooked “a bit” ;
Not well enough to make a dress,
Or coax plain-living folk to sit
Around the joint Jones used to bless.
She was an amateurish wife,
And never wholly learnt her part;
He bore with her right through his life
Because she’d won his simple heart.
War shakes the Jonesian home each day,
Most standing armies age in peace;
But train her in a homely way,
And war upon the hearth will cease.
N.S.W.
THE RELIGIOUS EDITOR.

The erratic figures in the column devoted to profits
suggest that the co. plays a good game of poker in
the smoke-room, and occasionally has the luck to
strike a millionaire. It looks like that, but probably
there is another explanation. The co. has 69 ships,
and ships have a habit of wearing out. As soon,
therefore, as a ship is built and starts to work, the
management says: “Part of your earnings in excess
of actual working expenses isn’t profit. You will be
a practically valueless hulk in 25 years. Therefore,
besides earning us interest, you must earn us enough
in 25 years to pay us back what it cost to build you,
so that we may then build another ship to take your
place.” Some cos. show that amortization, or depre-
ciation, or repairs and replacement fund; this co.
doesn’t. Supposing last year the co. set aside 21
p.c. of the book value of its ships—that is, £33,923"
Then, instead of showing that sum first as profit and
then as an addition to reserves, the co. simply
deducts the sum from the net profits exhibited in
the P. and L. account, and either spends the money
on some new asset, which it doesn’t show in the
balance-sheet at all, or writes down an existing asset
by that amount. But, though the co. has put by
(say) 21 p.c. (or it may be 4 p.c. for all this paper
knows), it presently that, through extra care or
extra good luck or something, the ships have lasted
longer than was calculated. Then the depreciation
fund, of course, becomes bloated —in other words, a
greater provision has been made out of profits for
renewals and replacements than is necessary. When
that happens it is allowable to transfer some of the
unwanted surplus back to profits ; and that, no doubt,
is what has been done two or three times in the
co.’s history.
But let’s try to get some idea of the provision
which is being made now. For, plainly, the real
position depends a very great deal upon that. For
instance, if a co. didn’t lay by enough revenue to
make replacements, the time would inevitably come
when, having claimed and probably paid away more
profits than it had really made, it would have to go
to the shareholders for more capital to make neces-
sary replacements. On the other hand, if it socks
away too much it may be able to disgorge now and
then, just as this co. has done. The handiest way of
socking away profit is by putting it into ship pro-
perty which doesn’t figure in the balance-sheet. Now,
this is the position (stated only approximately for
some years in the middle of the table) :

In 1906 the fleet was 112,853 tons, and the value
set on it was about £8 9s. per ton. To-day the
figures have gone up to 197,119 tons and the value
to £1,356,944, which works out at only £6 17s. 6d.
per ton. Or put it this way. In the past six years
74,266 tons have been added to the fleet, while the
book value of the fleet has been increased by only
£403,458. Assuming that the new shipping has cost
only £lO a ton (and you can’t buy a 5000-ton ship
for £50,000), then £339,202 has been provided to-
wards the cost out of profits. That is to say, that
is the amount of profit put by into a depreciation
fund, and thence diverted to this purpose of replac-
ing ships, in the past six years. The figures must
not, however, be taken as absolute; for there is room
for some error on account of the variable tonnage
of new ships building, and not all paid for perhaps,
though included in the total tonnage. But this
should, not throw the figures far out.
From the foregoing it looks as though the co. not
only makes very ample provision against wear and
tear, but something besides. Further evidences of its
wonderfully sound position can be got from this :

as shown above, a vast amount has been spent which

But things can’t go on improving in looks like that.
In the past 10 years nearly £25,000 of profit on sales
has been added to reserves. This money is, however,
leally not profit at all, but is merely capital previ-
ously written off as lost. The society didn’t get into
quite as bad a mess as a good manv others; never-
theless, after several years spent mainly in the under-
business, it found in 1898 that things stood
somewhat as follows: Assets (mostly foreclosures and
advances) £IBO 8 76 ; estimated real value of the
lot, £.118,151 ; shrinkage, £62,725. To make good
shrinkage, which represented the hot-air
boom, the co. decided to write off two-
hlths of the capital. This was more than the valua-
tion called for, and allowed £10,532 to go to a
reserve fund, to provide for anv further diminution
ot values. But there wasn’t any further shrinkage.
On the contrary, Victoria steadily got over the sick-
ness caused by the explosion of its borrowing bubble
and good seasons and brisk trade went on adding
I’tt! o by little to property values. As the society
sold the old assets it made a profit on them—that is
made less loss than it had provided for in
iB9B.
But the old written-down properties are almost all
gone now. This next table shows, amonest other
things, how the foreclosures have been realised:—
„ . 1900. 1909. 1910. 1911. 1912.

Thus only £1772 of properties now remain in
possession. But that doesn’t mean that the profits
on sales which have so helped the reserves will now
suddenly peter out. Profits have only been taken
into account as the actual cash has been handled ; and
as most of the sales are no doubt on time payment,
money will continue to come in for a while. But
if the revenue from this source is doomed, the new
business should continue to bring in something
pleasant. Not, of course, that the advances or the
profit from advances can be expected to go on moving
ahead at the recent rate, because the sale of Dm old
assets on terms has bloated those figures. Still, the
society has clearly held on to a nice, profitable con-
nection ; and as long as it has made its advances on a
nrudent basis, the fine size of its reserves in propor-
tion to capital ought: to insure for it an exceptionally
good rate of dividend—exceptionally' good from the
building-society point of view, that is.

This co. has been a disappointment. Most that cater
for the public’s entertainment are. There is no last-
ing quality in them. The public goes mad over
ping-pong to-day, and to-morrow forgets all about the
insanity. Wonderland booms this month, and tramk
burst with the crowds that want to reach its bulging
gates; to-morrow it is an abandoned desert. Of
course, the showman invariably says to the backer
he’s angling for: “Oh, yes, that’s true; but ours is
the exception. Ours isn't a craze or a fad. Ours has
come to stay, like cricket and football and
the theatre.” _ This was told concerning ice-
skating. Hadn’t people been skating in “the
Old Country” beyond the memory of man?
Unfortunately for investors in the scheme, what the
Old Country could do without charge in the open the
new country wasn’t prepared to pay big money to
do within walls. For a few weeks it was a modified
craze; after that it was left pretty strictly to Alger-
non and Ermyntrude. And since Alg. and Erm. don’t
occur in very large numbers, the co. presently de-
cided that it would have to, so to speak, join them
in partnership with cold mutton and frozen . lamb.
This paper can’t swear to it, but it suspects that the
net revenue from the skaters doesn’t now compare
with that from freezing, cold storage and the sale of
ice. The enlarged works are capable of handling
15,000 carcases a week for export. In fact, the co.
puts the skating part of the business in this offhand
way: “A satisfactory lease of the Glaciarium Hall
has been entered into for a fixed term of three years,
with the right to the co. to resume the same for its
skating season in each year.”
. The paid capital is £29,500. This includes £SOOO
in 7| p.c. pref. shares, issued when the co. found
that it would have to find some other ways of making
money besides ice-skating. The directors would have
been glad of still more capital, but were presently
content to raise £12,500 on mortgage at 5 p.c.
Still, so much money has had to go out, in conse-
quence of the co. having changed its character, that
the finances are not even now very easy ; apart from
the mortgage, liabilities (including overdraft) run to
£4537, an increase of £9ll during the year, not-
withstanding the current profit. Plainly the co
having gone into other lines, the past is no guide to
the future. The business it is now in, though bound
to be irregular, is one which is likely to give very
good profits at times. That, anyhow, has been the
experience of the few already in it.

The apparent slump in the last half-year’s profit is
due to the payment of a bonus of £4ll to the staff
lhe co. is gradually calling up its capital. Last
half-year when there was a bonus of Is. per share
a call of the same amount was made. This took
the shares up to 12s. paid, leaving them liable for
another £1 18s. The shareholders’ risks are not,
however, appalling. Certainly the management is
liable to misread the. instructions of a client and
thus fall disastrously into the soup; but such cases
oc ?’i 1 ’ Z rare ly- To add to its comparative
safety it hasn t a penny of other people’s money at
risk, . while its own funds are invested in Perth
premises (£12,400), public securities (£4050),
and . mortgages and advances (£11,227). Interest
earnings last half-year produced £1241. which was
enough to pay 20 p.c. p.a. on the capital. Of course
the revenue from rent includes the charge made
a s amst f-he co. for the use of its own premises;
still probably somebody else would pay as much if
this co. went out. The net value of' the trust es-
tates m hand five years ago was £501,355; now
it is £1,528,517. And the continued growth of
the business done by trust offices seems just about
as certain as anything can be. They bring a special
knowledge to a business mostly transacted hitherto
by amateurs, and they give a guarantee that they
won’t disappear with the widow’s mite. Also, a
very important point, they don’t cease to exist
just because someone or two persons die; and that
is the trouble one is always bumping up against
in connection with the personal trustee, involving
an alteration of the will or other document if the
person who made it happens to be alive, or an appli-
cation to the court if he happens to have set sail for
other kingdoms.

The profit for the year was really a bit more than
that, but the S. Aus. Taxation Commissioner insisted
on the co. paying income (ax on the premiums col-
lected on the new issue of shares made in 1911.
That raises a nice point. Should those premiums be
liable for income tax any more than the new capital
itself? It is, for one reason, certainly undesirable
that premiums should be taxed; reserves are such
good things that no excuse should be given a co.
for neglecting to ask a premium for shares when it
can be got. Certainly, this money is generally liable
to be later on distributed among shareholders. It
shouldn’t be, but in most cases it is, though, hap-
pily, instances where divs. are paid out of a pre-
mium reserve are exceedingly rare. And, anyhow,
when it is done it simply amounts, in most cases, to
taking money out of one pocket and putting it back
into the same man’s other pocket. In other words,
even in such a case the “dividend” so far as it is
ever paid out of a premium reserve isn’t income ; it
is only return of capital. On the whole, then, there
seems to be much more to be said against regarding
premium capital as income than can be said in its
favor. Indeed, one object of the good accountant is
to prevent it being regarded as income; he wants
it recognised as an insurance fund, provided not out
ot profits, but out of the shareholders’ own pockets
and designed to protect (1) the co.’s creditors; (2)
the shareholders’ capital proper. Before to-day the
b. Aus. Taxation Department has demanded a tax
(in the Wallaroo and Moonta case) which was un-
doubtedly calculated to encourage improvidence and
bad accountancy; and it looks to have done much
the same in the present case.
This co. had the S. Australian field to itself for
many years, until a couple of the big pastoral-agency
cos. decided to float separate small concerns to look
after the dead man’s money. Such rivals will not
however, do much harm. There is plenty of trustee
work for them all to do. What is needed is a
wider recognition of their special value, and it is a
pretty _ tough job for one small co. to do all the
preaching necessary. Still, one can’t complain of the
progress. This shows the amount of trust funds in
the co. s control at various dates:—
1895.. ..£482,987|1910.. ..£2,309,316
1005" 1911 •• •• 2,388,695
1906 -’ ..1,413,953 ! 1912.. .. 2,550,825
In handling the trust estate, the private trustee puts
up no guarantee that he won’t swindle the estate
or be guilty of a disastrous misreading of his in-
structions. This co. puts up a guarantee to the
extent of its capital (£22,850) and its reserves;
and if they arc not sufficient, shareholders—a very
solid lot—are liable. for £52,500 in calls. This
guarantee fund (capital and reserves) is in cash
public securities, advances to estates and real pro-
perty, and the interest earnings alone ( £2043 last
year) are enough to pay a 9 p.c. div.

That table starts from the time when the Federal’s
face was washed, and it was sent out from the
Inebriates’ Retreat, apparently a cured and converted
institution. It had got into dreadful habits in the old
days, but after 11 years of painful treatment, it was
judged fit to go into ordinary business again. Nomin-
ally, therefore, the old concern was wound up and
the undertaking sold to the present new one. The
remaining assets were valued at £122,839, so when
anew co. was formed, with a capital of £96,351,
there remained £26,488 over, and that amount was
used as the nucleus of a reserve fund. Since the con-
verted co. started, what looks like very satisfactory
progress has been made. Here you are:—
19U4. 1909. 1910. 1911. 1912.

Foreclosed properties, valued at £54,071 in 1904, '
have been steadily got rid of, until now there is
only £8927 worth on the books. At the outset the
co. used to show a profit on sales; lately the accounts
haven’t disclosed either a profit or a loss. That is
curious, and suggests that there is probably an
undisclosed contingency reserve to come and go on.
As properties have been sold new advances have been
made; also a moderate amount has been borrowed
and then lent out to clients. There has been very
fair profit in this business so far ; whether there will
be ultimate profit in it depends, of course, upon
whether the co. gets paid 20s. in the £. It is a
fine thing to see foreclosed properties moving off
briskly, and leaving, in most cases, an actual profit
to the mortgagee.; but there is this to remember:
if property values are so inflated as that,
it is highly probable that new advances are
being made on too high a basis, with the result
that losses Will be shown when the slump comes.
Whether this or any other mortgage co. in particular
is making this mistake cannot, of course, be told
from the accounts ; and that is the risk the investor
has to take.
W. J. Miles has failed in his appeal to
the High Court in the Sydney Meat Preserving case.
The S. M. P. Cos. was formed many years ago by
stockowners to keep up the price of fat stock. The
thing has ever since been done by the simple process
of bidding up when the market showed signs of
slackness. Moreover, most of the owners allow the
co, a rebate of per lb., so that when it bids
(say) £lO, it usually has to pay only about £9
10s. Anybody else who wants that beast must, how-
ever, bid higher than £lO, though the £lO is really
a fictitious bid. Obviously the higher the co. forces
the market, and the less profit it makes, the better
for the stockowning shareholders. Hence the co
has never paid a div. This was all right until Miles
who doesn’t own any fat stock, inherited a very
large number of shares—more, in fact, than anybody
else owns. He naturally wanted a dividend, and
tried to compel the directors to work the thing as
an ordinary business concern and pay one. The High
Court has, however, decided (Justice Isaacs dissent-
ing-) that they needn’t do anything of the sort. Said
Chief Justice Griffith, with whom Justice Barton con-
curred: “The injunction sought in this case is, in
substance, a mandatory injunction to command the
directors and the company to set their affections on
other objects than those which have hitherto at-
tracted them. Although such an injunction would be
an entire novelty, I should not hesitate to grant it
if it were consistent with any known principle of law
But, m my opinion, the law allows the members of
a company to adopt what policy they please to
guide them in carrying on its operations. If they
think fit to carry them on with the collateral object
of enabling another enterprise to be carried on with
greater success than would otherwise be possible I
think that they are entitled to do so without any
interference from the Court, provided they do not
expend the funds of the company upon any object
not authorised by. its constitution.” This must be
accepted as good law; but it none the less leaves
things in a horribly unpleasant state for W. J. Miles
and the least the other shareholders can do is to buv
him out at a. fair valuation. *

Without anv intention of putting it
through this session, the Commonwealth Government
has introduced the long-pronnsed Insurance BiUj em
the recommendations of the Lommissiu
wWch reported some time ago. The measure deals
with all sorts of insurance concerns and generally
sneaking deals with them very well. I erhaps tne
two molt striking provisions so far as hie offices OT
enneerned refer to deposits and policy reserves.
Existing offices are to put up £20,000 with the Com-
monwealth Treasury, in cash or approved securities
' i must add £IO,OOO a year till £loo,uuu is
reached. Other insurance cos. (including foreigners,
who must have a registered Australian representa-
tive! must put up £20,000. In the case of anew
insurance co. (any sort) formed in the Com,non-
wealth the first deposit is to be £2OOO. that win
stop any more purely mutual offices —unless, as in the
case of the National Mutual, some people put up
a: 2000 for the mere sake of getting anew concern
going. After the first deposit has been made, _o p.e.
of the premium income—or £IO,OOO, whichever is
tb f , smaller—must be added to the £2OOO every year.
As to policy reserves, if a premium remains un-
naid for 30 davs —the usual period of grace the
companv may charge compound interest upon it.
The policy is not to lapse until the premiums due
and unpaid, together with the interest charged, are
enual to the reserve value. During the second year
after the cessation of payments, the company may
~j ve notice in writing to convert the reserve value
into a paid-up policy. “Reserve value and sur-
render value” are, of course, very different cattle,
the former is a matter of actuarial science ; the
latter is anything that the office likes to make it.
It is not provided that anybody may demand cash
for a policy which he may want to throw up. Any
such transaction, it seems, must still be a matter tor
negotiation between the co. and the policyholder
with the trump cards held by the co.
There is to be an insurance commissioner, who is
to have extensive powers of inquiry, and is to make
an annual report on the working of the Act. He is
to see that cos. prepare their accounts and their pro-
posals” in a prescribed form. Government audit is
not, however, provided for. And finally, the fact
that you have been hanged is not to prevent your
widow getting your insurance money. #
Another reason why Australian life offices
should be content with the good business they can
do in Australia arrives from S’Africa. When tne
sorely-harassed Colonial Mutual decided that it
wasn’t going fast enough in the Commonwealth, it
moved on to S’Africa, and set up local Boards and
agencies there. A while ago the head-office rubbed
its eyes and read this: —
“No doubt by this time you have heard about ine
from your Cape office ; if not, permit me to give you
a few particulars. (1) I have taken several hundred
pounds in premiums. (2) I have drawn a loan on
policy ‘A. Marsh’ in vour favor. (3) I have drawn
a claim—M. C. Friedman —for £IOOO in my own
favor (this policy matures about five years hence).
(4) I have written a letter to your London office, and
had same signed by your secretary at Capetown, ask-
ing them to pay me the sum of £3157 10s., and
tins has been done.”
After setting out these and a few other things, the
self-confessed culprit asked that no proceedings
should be taken, on the ground that he had practi-
cally unlimited power at the Capetown office on a
salary of £ 216 a year. Says he: —
“I recorded all the correspondence in the morning,
and passed such letters as I wished over to the other
clerks. I took premiums at the counter, and did the
pay-in daily. I had charge of the petty cash. I
drew cheques when I wished, and had them signed
without question. I, had full charge of the whole
renewal department. I checked all notices and re-
ceipts goinc out of the office. I did all surrender
value, loan, - paid-up policy, and many other calcula-
tions required in the office. I had charge of all
claims for payment, and, if my initials appeared on
a claim envelope, no questions were asked. I did all
the abeyance loan advances from the abeyance lists,
and no one else ever touched them. I did all the
correspondence for renewal department.”
Assuming that the man’s confession, is true, it shows
what sort of supervision an Australian office is liable
to exercise over a small business scattered through
S’Afriea and other remote places. #
B. H. Junction North is busy on its new
slime-treating campaign, having laid in a big stock
of that tough material. During a recent week 1180
tons of the stuff were put through, out of a total of
3569 tons of all sorts (ore, etc.) fed to the plant.
The return was 594 tons of lead concentrates, value
56 p.c. lead and 330 z. silver, and 410 tons zinc con-
centrates, value 42 p.c. zinc and 15oz. silver. The
recoveries show an improvement. But recoveries
don’t tell the whole tale; you need to know some-
thing about costs —including depreciation. The
latter is most important. Junction North, moreover,
paid a very high price for some of its purchased
slimes; but, fortunately, the lead market continues
very firm.
* * * *
The Commonwealth Oil Corporation
loaded a few tons of oil-fuel into the Commonwealth
destroyers at Sydney the other day, in fulfilment of
a contract. But the corporation is an exceedingly
sick giant these days, and operations are being car-
ried on only on a very small scale at Hartley Yale.
The works at Wolgan have been shut down for
months, and nothing is being done to develop the
very big deposits of rich shale known to exist between
the Capertee and Wolgan valleys. The co. is still in
existence, of course, but everything is in the hands of
the receivers, who are carrying on the business, and
can hardly be making a profit. The co.’s money has
all gone, and there is still a lot of development, etc.,
to do. What will be the end of it all nobody knows,
though it is certain somebody is going to make a
lot of money out of those shale deposits some day.
Westralia’s gold yield goes on shrinking.
For the 11 months ended November it totalled
1,166,7140 z. ; the year’s shortage, compared with
the 1911 figures will be about 100,0000 z., and (say)
200,0000 z. less than the output for 1910. The Gov-
ernment has just introduced a Bill to abolish piece-
work and the night shift, and to reduce the under-
ground weekly working hours to 44. These things,
whatever else they do, won’t assist in instantly check-
ing the drop in production.
Mines Minister Collier (W.A.) has agreed
to assist copper seekers in the distant Roeboume
field, by advancing cash against ore bagged ready
for shipment. The stone will be assayed by a Gov-
ernment man, and after deducting cost of sampling,
transport, realisation, etc., an advance up to two-
thirds of the estimated net value will be made. The
scheme will enable prospectors in remote parts to get
the, often-indispensable quick returns from their
labor. # * *

F A. Govett, on Lake View and Star, at
the Lake View and Oroya Exploration meeting:
“Earnings now amount to £40,000, or about-Opc.
n a and are likely to continue so. For some
years we have not had to make any reductionsi in
the grade. Development continues to reveal fresh
bodies of ore, and after an interval of about _ seven
years we have resumed sinking. The Star mine is
opening up well, and reserves do not include any-
thing below the fifth level, while we know of good
reserves as deep as 1100 ft. The co. is at present
distributing £30,000, or 15 p.c. p.a., but as soon
as some modest reserve is reached we shall not Want
to pile up funds, and the full earnings of £40,000
or 20 p.c. will be available for dividends. 1
think an increase to 18 p.c. will almost certainly be
made in December. You cannot well value this
share at less than 35., seeing that at 18 p.c. the
dividend value works out at four years purchase.
Our holding of 626,999 shares would at 3s. per
share be worth £94,000, while our share of the
profits with a dividend of 18 p.c. would be
£22,500.” # *

The derelict Greenmount, Southern Cross
(W.A.), has got a further respite from labor con-
ditions. It has been worked off and on since 1905,
and although 67,000 tons have been extracted and
crushed for an average yield of 18s. 2d., the co. is
without funds and scrip is unsaleable. Present exemp-
t.ion was obtained to realise on assets and try to let
the mine on tribute.

Obit: Birthday Gift Cos. # #
“Bar None”: Re Great Boulder, “Bar
One” (B. 28/11/’l2) said the mine had good healthy
values to shove it along the road to dividends, cham-
pagne and press notices. It had, and it also had a
management that worked honestly, and not for mar-
ket purposes like some of the mines he blows about.
As regards costs, about which “Bar One” seems to
know little or nothing: in these days of fake and
fraud, monthly accounts can be, and are, doctored so
frequently by giving some costs and suppressing-
others, that no one (except “Bar One” and perhaps
a few other credulous persons like him) dreams of
taking them seriously. F’rinstance, a co. may treat
(sav) 3000 tons a month for £SOOO, and spend
£3OOO or £1 per ton to get it. But in order to
hoodwink shareholders and impress the guileless
“Bar One” and his cobbers, the tonnage is given
as 3500, costs are stated as a shade over 175., and
the profitable working of “low-grade ore” is dis-
honestly whooped about. This sort of thing has been
done over and over again at Kalgoorlie, and people
who ought to know say it is being done still. Even
where costs are not faked, a comparison _is
difficult in the case of a field like the Golden Mile,
where the size and character of the ore bodies, etc.,
differ so much. “Bar One” makes another “bloomer,”
when he says that Boulder’s three main hauling
shafts save trucking. Two of them certainly lend
a helping hand in that direction, but the third (Ham-
ilton’s) is nearly a quarter of a mile away from the
mill, and all the ore from that section has to be
trucked every foot of the way. His contention that
the management is dawdling in the work of deep
prospecting is his, and no one else’s, and the fact
that in June last four directors were on the register
for barely 13,000 shares, proves and means nothing.
In the case of some of the big Westralian mining
cos., directors are on the register for many thousands
of shares, and are “short” of them all and many
thousands besides at prices much higher than the
present market quotes.
“The treatment of the Transvaal (W.A.)
ore presents no insuperable difficulty,” says director
T. J. Hoover in his report on the different properties
in which the Lake View and Oroya Exploration is
interested. If so, why has the mine, with its 48,509
tons of 445. reserves ready for stopfng, been so ener-
getically hawked for sale during the last two years?
The fact is, no one is anxious to take on the job of
treating the Transvaal ore after the costly fail-
ures at the very similar Lancefield.
William Rain, chairman of North Nug-
getty, Daylesford, recently wrote divs. in sight down
to the 15s. mark. The Queen-street plungers consoled
themselves by saying the expert wasn’t born who
could estimate a Victorian mine ; so when a develop-
ment came in the mine and in the Central next door,
the Rain stock went down as prices went up. Still
William turned up at the half-yearly meeting with
his usual pleasant smile, and in a nice way declined!
to write values up above the “visible” point and a
reasonable “probable” expectation. Another sweet
William, in Bill Gullock, the manager, supplied the
sweetening to this sauce, otherwise one or two of the
disappointed crowd would have tumbled off their
seats from heat apoplexy. Gullock’s idea is to tear
out nine months’ divs., and he thinks he can develop
another nine months’ dirt in the meantime. A
statement like that gives the lie to those who said
that Gullock was a plant about to adorn the Nug-
getty scrap heap. Still, putting nine and nine to-
gether doesn’t make 305., any more than it tots up
to the famous 90s. mark, where Queen-street got left
when the crash came.
* * * *
New Jubilee, Scarsdale (Vic.), lives up to
its income. Last half-year it trundled 128,888 tons
to the battery. This stuff graded 4|dwt. per ton,
and left the co. with only about £IOSO after a thou-
sand had been spent on plant. Yet the Board de-
clared a 6d. div., leaving only £SOO to start the
new year with. Looking at its past, the mine hasn’t
done badly, 91,743 tons having given £110,567
worth of gold, from which £24,750 has been spun
out as divs. But most of the profits have come from
the upper levels; so if the junction of the main lode
and the western formation at 1000 ft. doesn’t bring
a rich patch, another Scarsdale hero will have proven
that he hasn’t the vitality to exist below the 1000 ft.
zone. This poverty at depth is the odd and un-
pleasant feature of the district. As usual, the costs
sheet of the New Jubilee looks nice—mining and
filling 6s. lid. per ton, hauling Is. 4d. per ton, de-
velopment Is. lid., crushing is. Old. ; total 11s. 3d.
* * * *
South New Moon, Bendigo, on a called-
up capital of 17s. 7d. (32,000 shares), has shelled
out £ls 6s. 6d. per share, and has still a great deal
more than a bob left. Costs with it now reach 225.
2d. per ton ; but much of that arises- from heavy de-
velopment work, partly in the old mine and partly in
sinking on the Paddy’s Gully line. Surveyor W. H.
Cundy, who does the mothering- for a lot of Bendigo
shows, wants South Moon to rattle up the old bones by
trying to pick up the extension of the spurs worked by
the New Moon, next door, at 416 ft., and by opening
up the big body of spurry country at 820 ft. To-day
the co. relies mostly on the big block of spurry
ground above 980 ft., as the stone opened up at
1140 ft. isn’t too extensive, though some of it has
crushed fairly well. The co. may shine yet by mak-
ing a good hit on the Paddy’s Gully line. The belt
of country in which the Moon group lives has been
so remunerative that the very name of the co. would
bring in buyers if once a good report was forth-
coming. #
The giant at Ararat (Vic.) is still Catli-
cart Central, which, without drawing on the acknow-
ledged rich belt of wash opened up, can give rattling
good dirt from the development drives now being
pushed out; 16380 z. from 380 fathoms, the record
for the past six months, is better than any alluvial
show has done since Cathcart alighted on its rich
patch in the early Bryant days. Work off No. 4 rise
to the south-west didn’t prove too cheery, and there
seems to be some difference of opinion as to whether
driving was not done too far in this direction.
Anyway, to the nor’-west 60ft. of good wash was got,
though values shaded off. Water is heavy, but there
needn’t be much loss of sleep wondering whether the
pumps can beat it, as the pressure is being steadily re-
duced. Still, there is a deal of nasty talk about the
water and the lower-grade material developed lately,
just as if such things were unusual. Alluvial shows
have had both troubles, and yet have stood far higher
on the share list than Central Cathcarts have ever
done.

Charters Towers for 11 months of this
year belted out £351,677, or £172,054 less than
in the corresponding period of 1911. November gave
only £29,140, as compared with £48,658 12 months
ago. For 11 months the dividends were only
£31,824, while the calls were £35,200; but the
figures will be improved before the end of the month,
and the outlook is than * the figures
The last crushing of the Papuan Brilliant
and Victoria, Charters Towers, was worth £2977
from 284 tons of stone—the best yield since the
Moonstone reef was re-opened. Still, the market is
languid, for the reef at the bottom of the underlay
shaft is low-grade. And with poverty in the bottom,
the reserves and “probable” reserves of good stone are
limited. The co. is in 96,000 shares. The new
Queen Cross, next door, has 200,000.
Hampden Cloncurry (Q.) got hold of a
prize when it bought the Duchess, for the mine has
good values at the deepest level yet touched —48ft.
below the 550 ft. The bottom of the winze shows
57in. of ore, assaying 23 p.c. This winze is riffbt in
the heart of the richest zone in the mine, some 220 ft.
north of the shaft. Helped by this patch, the
Duchess has been supplying the smelters with ore
averaging 18 p.c. In connection with the returns
from the works, it should be noted that those for
September (721 tons), October (725 tons), and
November (650 tons) included the product from
accumulated copper matte, 2125 tons of which were
treated in three months, representing, roughly, 700
tons copper. This, of course, should be credited to
the period when the ore was reduced to matte. As
a regular thing, the directors expect an output of
from 600 to 650 tons of copper per month, but the
scarcity of fuel or fluxes may cause variations.

Waihi, Maoriland’s once-big mine, may-
have a kick in it yet as .a low-grade proposition.
The directors, thinking so, continue to push on with
the electric power scheme, and are not over-anxious
at present to purchase another property, although
various proposals are under consideration. The co.
is well off for cash.

The amalgamation of the Tasmanian
Smelting Cos. with the Hercules and Primrose mining
cos. is under consideration, and a report by G. H.
Blakemore on the project will soon be available. The
conditions favor consolidation —of some interests,
anyway—for a good deal of capital will be required
if the zinc problem is to be tackled in earnest.
Hitherto Australia has had no mines worked ex-
clusively, or almost exclusively, for zinc.
Tongkah Harbor’s return of 37 tons of
tin oxide for a week’s work is more cheerful than
late reports have been. The dredge-building still
continues; the next machine will be able to dredge
the tin-wash from a depth of 60ft. The plan of the
harbor shows a narrow streak of worked ground,
evidently following the course of an old river bed.
The decline in the yield from a whole fleet of dredges
suggests that values have gradually tailed off, as
dredging has been pushed further out. Concentrates,
dressed to 70 p.c. metallic tin, have been recently
selling at £l3O at Tongkah. On 76 tons produced
in November, the return would be £9BB0 —hardly
enough to pay all expenses and leave a net profit of
£SOOO, which is required if the quarterly £15,000
is to be forthcoming.

“R. Sayers”; Re the contribution of “An
Old Insurance Man” (B. 19/12/’l2). The recom-
mendations made by the Commission on Insurance do
not embrace the payment of a cash surrender value at
all. In fact, the commissioners specifically advise
against it, and state their reasons for so doing (see
pages 21 and 22 of the Report). Where, therefore,
does the question of “harpies” arise, and where
the value or necessity of the suggested short bill?
What the commissioners do recommend is that in the
event of the payments under a policy being discon-
tinued, the actuarial reserve should be anplied pro
tanto to maintain the policy in force ; but, as above
stated, thev go so far as to recommend that there
be no guaranteed cash surrender value at all. Evi-
dently “An Old Insurance Man” does not know so
much of his subject as he thinks he does. #
“Vagabond”: Here’s another lot of Aus-
tralians and Maorilanders who have wandered from
their homes to take a lead in tearing the golden in-
nards from the earth. The bunch I’ve just been with
is in Paracale, in the top of the Philippines. The
compa-ny is dredging 1 the stuff out of the little rivei
there, and from one or two other small streams
round the corner. Only about a score of white men
are on the job, all told, and most of them are from
the best country. Some of the exiles have been
marooned there for about five years and others are
new-chums of a bare year. Paracale itself is a one-
horse sort of show —though, by the way, the local
ox-waggon and hansom is “horsed” with a carabo,
which is a splay-footed, sleep-walking, hump-backed
caricature of a cross between a bullock, a gnu, _ a
tired snail and a cake of mud with whiskers on its
nose. The other animals in sight are gaunt pigs, a
derelict goat or two, the weirdest breed of mongrel
native dog, a few chickens, and plenty mosquitoes.
The mosquitoes are about the same size as the chick-
ens. The whites live chiefly on quinine, tinned meats,
hens, and hopes of seeing home some day. The
female population (white) numbers two (2). One
hundred p.c. is married ; 50 p.c. is American and 50
p.c. Maoriland. The principal edifices are one old
Spanish church. Its bell has the sound of a soul in
torment, and it rings on an average 10 times a day
oftener than anyone wants it to. The chief industries
are (native) building dredges and working them, and
(white) tearing hair over the way the nigs, do these
things, or, oftener, don’t do them. The dredges are
the bucket type, and the river has to have its bed
taken up and shaken to a depth of about 40ft. The
metal is mostly line, with occasional patches of
coarse, and evidently it grew right where it is
found, as it has sharp, uncut edges without a sign
of being waterworn. Lumps of quartz are also
scooped up, and these are crushed on the spot. Alto-
gether there are four dredges at work, and another
two are being rigged as quick as the machinery can
be fetched along. Paracale is a small place, but it
holds enough all-white hospitality to the stranger
within its sliprails for a township 20 times Jts size.
“Hail Columbia” Hoover has something
interesting to say on “Mine Valuation and Mine
Finance.” in the October London Mining Magazine.
As a means of overcoming some of the difficulties
inherent in the valuation of so tricky an asset as
a mine, Hoover suggests the issue of debentures in
lieu of shares to cover the assured profit in a mine,
leaving the prospective value to be expressed in
shares. Any statement of profit assured ought, ac-
cording to Hoover, to give (1) the tonnage of
ore, (2) the maximum distance in feet that the
ore has been assumed to continue beyond a sampled
face, (3) the average value, (4) the percentage
of extraction assumed, (5) the working cost, (6) if
other than a gold mine the price of metals assumed,
(7) the cost of new equipment necessary to recover
the profit, (8) the loss of interest involved during
♦he period necessary to recover this profit. Profit
assured, subject to these conditions, is, he claims,
security worthy of a debenture or a bond redeemable
out of this profit.


Peter F. Heering reports that the
last year has been an advance on the pre-
vious one, and his well-known Cherry
Brandy liqueur, with the Blue and Choco-
late Label, is well in evidence throughout
all Australasia; this being only a small
portion of his all-over-the-world output. He
with his two sons have the command of this
liqueur trade, their brand being well in
advance of all competitors, and, for an after-
dinner liqueur, no one who has dined well,
feels that their repast is well finished if a
glass of Heering’s Cherry Whisky or Brandy
is not the final bonne louche.*

WOMAN'S
LETTER
Sydney, December 23, 1912.
My Dear Arini. —
The State is rejoicing in a much better
season than was expected a few months
back. So, in spite of the vast amount of
capital which is being driven away, the city
shops are bulging with country visitors, and
the sheep-farmer can still say baa to the
Christmas goose. Indeed, N.S.W is
apparently bursting its sides with laugh-
ter and joy, though still the Raven
croaks ‘Bad times.” The Government
might appoint a Commission of de-
tectives, armed with search warrants, to
look for the address of that Lost Capital.
The departure of the Islingtons from Maori-
land s Government House lifts a load from
local Society, which has felt ever since the
haughty lady Excellency arrived that it has
been living on sufferance. Islington him-
self was well liked, but his bitter half ill-
concealed her disappointment at being
dragged or coaxed from Civilisation to Pov-
erty Bay and thereabouts. It is credited to
her that she said of the two leading cities:
In Wellington everybody is in Societv • in
Auckland nobody is in Society.” So she
goes back to London where only the intel-
lectual and .the brewers are in Society.
Governor and Lady Chelmsford enter-
tained members of the Teachers’ Confer-
ence at a garden party the other afternoon.
A broiling morning tamed down into a cool
and dusty day, watered by a few dozen rain-
drops.
Before you run out of Christmas good
wishes, wireless a few to the nurses in the
public institutions. I don’t mean the regu-
lar hospitals, but such places as the Rook-
wood Asylum, the Waterfall Sanatorium
and the infirmaries.” Life is not choco-
lates and motor-cars for the attendants at
these places. The Rookwood nurses had-
their salary put up, I’m told, to £llO a
year. But when they got that rise, the
Government also increased the deduction
made for maintenance. It used to be £25 a
year;_ now it is £4O. You’ll say that £4O a
year isn t much to charge for the board and
at gm ? of a heart .Y young woman, and that
Mrs. Apartments Vacant wouldn’t do it for
a “ one y- Quite true; but, then, Mrs
Ap. Vacant hasn’t the right to call on her
young women to lend a hand whenever they
chance to be on the premises.
Besid s, Mrs. Apartments Vacant expects
to have to supply her young women with
every meal they eat, whereas the Rookwood
" ars , e gets away whenever she can. If she
didn t she would probably remain at Rook-
wood for good, merely shifting to the ceme-
tery next door. The food is very poor stuff
and there isn’t too much of it. There is a
dietary scale ; the nurse is allowed so many
ounces of this and so many gills of that—
I remember it’s three gills of milk, which is
something over half-a-pint. Such a thing
as cake or afternoon-tea is, I’m told, un-
known, unless the nurses pay for it them-
selves. And, remember, these women, who
have the unappetising stuff pushed before
them, have probably just come from the
harrowing work of attending to as
many as 100 patients. Think of even 50
patients, old mostly, helpless many of them
•?? 1 ? d J nearly all of them >' numbers of them
with bedsores ; a few of them with worse than
that. Picture yourself in such surround-
mgs, with the work of attending to such
sufferers, and with the knowledge that you
will very likely have to attend a death-bed
before the week is out. Then reflect on the
deduction of £4O a year for the nurse’s
maintenance, leaving well under 30s. a week
tor the nurse herself, and—well, I’ll let you
say what you like about the parsimony of
the administration.
However, they are not careful merely over
tne nurses’ rations and their pay. Many
of the inmates at Rookwood are dying of
tuberculous lungs and throats. If they are
not far gone they are sent to Waterfall; if
they are doomed they remain at Rookwood—
its handy to Sydney’s Great City of the
Dead. Many pigs are kept at the institu-
tion. 4he fork plays to and fro between the
consumptive’s mouth and. the plate; and the
leavings on the plate go—where? Two or
three weeks ago the cows at the institution
were found to be. tuberculous. I wish some-
body would examine the pigs—not grown, let
me add to reassure you, for outside consump-
tion.

A. hankering to attend public or semi-
public funerals is becoming a vice with a
section of lady-voters in these parts. A man
who had to almost fight his way to the open
grave of George Rignold, tells me it was
an amazing spectacle to see women with in-
fants in their arms scrambling to get the
best places, with the cheerful chatter that
attends the daily gossip over the back fence,
lhe heat and dust, the tawdry remarks of
parson -North Ash (who told the assembly
hacl buried both Mr. Rignold’s wives, on
different dates, of course), and the fierce de-
sire of women and .young children to get
near the coffin, contributed to the gloom of
the occasion The open grave is not gen-
erally considered a fitting spot for a long
dissertation on Shakspeare, mixed with a
-ick of religion, and a dash of the musical
glasses and an allusion to Socrates and
Greek sculpture. Our mutual friend Blicks
saj s it does him harm to go to big spectacu-
lai funerals he swears all the way home.
, Kindergarten Training College (Ros-
lyn Gardens) took a hand in the general
breaking-up riot, and you could hardly see
the pupils heads above the deluge of Good
Advice. Miss Harriet A. Dumolo, the

pected outbreak of appreciative language,
but the teaching profession may have fanned
itself violently and sniffed sal volatile to
keep its mental balance.

Indulgent parents, fond aunts and other
hard-working relatives are toiling to keep
up with the annual rush of children’s parties.
Mrs. T. Holdsworth’s infant revel, the other
afternoon, raged round a Christmas tree that
nearly foundered through its tub being over-
loaded beyond, the Plimsoll line. Seventy
young things, in curls and miscroscopic mus-
lin skirts, relieved the situation by getting
away with a present apiece. Santa Claus
was master of ceremonies.
The Twilight House League had another
meeting last week in the Town Hall. The
committee has resolved to stick to the
original name, “Twilight House,” which is
a great deal more expressive than some of
the banal substitutes suggested by Suburbia,
which is accustomed to reside in miscalled
villas, with pepper-pot towers, in streets that
perpetuate the worst crimes in nomenclature.
During the coming holidays, committee
dames, will ransack town and country for
subscriptions, chairs, tables, broom handles,
ideas, and anything else they can pick up.
ihe results will be aired at a public meeting
in January.
Miss Jenkins, the Murkan lady who used
to train kindergarten students "at Roslyn
Gardens College, has swallowed a hurried
snack of the Montessori method, in Italy,
since her departure from this city. Now
another disciple. Miss Simpson, of the Pub-
lic Instruction Department, will set forth on
the same quest. The Montessori method ap-
pears to be another artful scheme for de-
luding the young child into an unnatural
love of acquired knowledge. The pill of
learning is wrapped up in the jam of play.
Miss Simpson, already an authority on in-
fant education, will bottle up the results of
her pilgrimage for home consumption.
“Milestones,” at the Criterion, sees Julius
Knight make love in Act I.—a knightly
specialty, which, goads even the front stalls
to softly pat their gloved palms. He does it
on one knee in the baggy garments of 1860.
In the next act (1885 period) Julius is less
decorative. He grows mutton-chop whiskers
and scowls upon romance from the height
ot an impending baronetcy. Then he be-
stows his weeping daughter’s affections on a
mere iord. In the third act, as grandpa
Rliead, Julius is so unlike the Superbus of
yore that he finishes up the scene in an
arm-chair. This sacrifice will yet cause a
revolution in the gallery.
Were all the dressing-rooms of 1860 so
lull ot white antimacassars and green up-
holstery as the Rheads’ room in “Milestones” ?
Were the forget-me-nots of that era as hard
and spiky-looking as the cold-blue wreath
which sits like a domestic halo on the flaxen
wig of Rose Sibley? Julius Knight-Rhead
only avoids getting a mouthful of the spikes
by carefully saluting the meek brow beneath
the halo Grace Noble, the charming
Gertrude Rhead of 1860, gets more fun out
or flounces than the rest of the party As
this is all the joy the authors five her, she
has a fairly dismal time in 1885. And in
l')12 she is still waiting—with some im-
patience, one imagines—for the undertaker.
Mrs. Ka.te Helen Weston, secretary of the
Elder Conservatorium (Adelaide), strolled
into Sydney one morning last week. In the
spare moments of a generous Christmas
holiday, Kate Helen, a small, alert woman,
with a capable manner, will industriously
tread the Inky Way. She’s on the trail of
copy in the Northern Territory, which
she 11 meet in the wet season a week or two
hence. Mrs. Weston takes a daughter with
her as far as Queensland.
A slim booklet comes this way. It bears
the unhandy title, “The Crossing of the
Blue Mountains Centenary,” and makes a
remark you have probably heard before—
‘Money very urgently required.” In May
the committee, which has Mr. Frank Walker
as president, will try to serve out a generous
helping of brass bands, bunting, demon-
strating school children, and official ban-
quets garnished with speeches. The first
successful efforts, in 1818, to penetrate the
then unknown land beyond the indigo-blue
hills that shoulder the skyline 3000-odd feet
above the Nepean River, were of priceless
value to Australian pioneers. The explorers,
Blaxland, Wentworth and Lawson, are
memorialised in the names of Blue Moun-
tain townships.
The centenary committee intends to spend
half the funds subscribed by the public on “a
lasting memento” of the three explorers
The obelisk on the bald patch of Mount
York already does something in stone to keep
the first dash across the hills before the
tourists eye. The centenary enihusiasts
propose to make a demonstration on this
breezy summit in May. The Mayor of Svd-
ney; Mr. H. G. Remits, Mount Victoria; and
hon. sec. Venning Thomas will receive cash
to help the affair.
Mrs. Percy Hunter and her two boys
leave London in the Seharnhorst on December
23 for this port. Mr. Percy, who has been
turning the feet of the immigrant this way,
has concluded his mission,” and comes home
too.
Miss Dolly O’Donnell, who was married
the other week to Mr. F. B. Shaw, is a
younger daughter of the late and wealthy
P. J. O’Donnell, of our squattocracy. The
bride met the man of her heart during a trip
to the other side. He’s something in Siam,
and the newly-wed have bustled off to that
coffee-colored strand for two years. Later
they’re going to live in England. The girl’s
trousseau built up another record in the mat-
ter of those superfluous luxuries, mostly in
cobweb lace and cr6pe-de-chine, which the
modern gilt-edged bride packs away in a ton
or two of luggage.
Mrs. C. B. Fairfax, who used to be the
pretty Miss Fraser of this township, is com-
ing back to see her native land,'after a
long residence on the other side. Dress is
said to be her favorite recreation.
Actor Ford, who winds up as a 1912
Labor member in “Milestones,” has a ten-
dency to scribble in his spare moments.
When he snatches time for ink, he contrib-
utes to the Harmsworth (London) combina-
tion.
G. L. Goodman, who has kept an eye on
the business end of the J.C.W. firm at Her
Majesty’s for many years, has been seri-
ously ill, but is celebrating the festive sea-
son by leaping along the road to recovery.
Mrs. C. R. Blaxland, who died in Armi-
dale on December 17, was a daughter of the
late Charles Marsh, of Salisbury Court, near
Armidale. Her husband belongs to the nu-
merous clan which proudly traces its descent
from Grandpa Gregory Blaxland, who led
the 1813 expedition over the Blue Moun-
tains.
Glen Innes district broke out in wedding
favors a week or two ago, for the orange-
blossoming of the handsome Hope Lamb.
She is a daughter of Mr. Harry Lamb, who
has a cosy sheep-run in those woolly parts.
The groom is Robert Connell.
The. Rose Bay Golf House has been re-
sounding with a louder tinkle of tea-cups
than usual. First, Miss Patience Raymond’s
friends gave her the farewell bun prior to her
departure from the circle of gay young spin-
sters. A bag and bangle were added to the
good wishes for the day that should change
Miss Raymond into Mrs. Giblin. Then the
bride-elect and her mamma returned the bun,
also at the “Goff” House. Now she’s Mrs.
Giblin, and will probably live mostly in
Perth. He is an engineer or something of
the kind at Coolgardie.
Artist Frances Hodgkins blew in here the
other morning on her way to Maoriland.
Bhe is a small, bright-eyed woman, with a
vivacious _ air, which hasn’t suffered from
residence in Paris. Europe claims her again
later on.
H.M.B. Drake, with the Admirable King-
Hall aboard, has sauntered into our water-
front, after shaking its fin off the Maoriland
coast. The Drake is officially booked to
start for the Channel fog on January 1.
The N.S.W. Gould Bird League had its
second _ annual chirrup the other morning.
The Girls’ High School, where young maid-
ens have recently broken up under vice-regal
patronage, served as the aviary. About
1900 bird-lovers in this State have taken
the pledge to abstain from bird’s-nesting
and similar crimes.

On Friday Fanny Powers, who is
to , mari 'y Mr. S. Foote of
Adelaide, had a farewell matinee ’from
m, e vaudeville profession, at the Tivoli,
rue miant who sang precocious songs 12 or
14 years ago. turned up her back hair and

, bright and unspoilt youngster, she has

The brilliant scholar, Dr. Findlay, who
came over to Sydney the other day to meet
his home-coining wife, and take her back to
Maoriland, has a highly-developed artistic
nnnd. One of his beliefs is that people’s
pleasures have more influence in fixing their
moral level than their business pursuits
have. The theatre is to-day, as it always
has been, he says, a reflex of the moral tone
and tendency of its age. To ensure a satis-
factory theatre, he suggested, when Attorney-
General in the Ward Administration, (1)
a social discipline mainly enforced by law
t- u a • stricter regulation of the stage’,
which, in addition to sumiressing the pal-
pably evil, would foster and cultivate the
better class of play, (3) national or muni-
cipal theatres, producing the great tragedies
and dramas _ and cleverest comedies. Find-
lay once boiled his theories into this sen-
tence . . Art in one or other of its many
shapes is the most profitable friend of a
nation’s leisure ; hence the diffusion of true
art is no unworthy aim of government.”
In some respects Maoriland’s Government
was weakened by the loss of Findlay. He
would have made a good Minister for Uni-
versal Culture. And Maoriland could do
with one.
We live in an age that isn’t much afraid
of spooks. Back in a N.S.W. country town
a man, the other day. hanged himself in his
own hall. In the old days his house would
have been shunned, and perhaps in the end
razed to the ground. In these days the house
was promptly taken by a man who knew all
about the tragedy. But, stranger still, the
housekeeper of the self-murdered man, after
going some hundreds of miles away, saw an
advertisement asking for applicants for a
similar job. in her own town, answered it,
and found it was to take the same position
in the same house with the new tenant. She
was offered the billet, at once accented it,
and is now at work within sight of the hang-
ing ghost. Who talks of decadent woman ?
It often happens that when an Aus-
tralian Conservative returns to his native
land, and is interviewed, like Queensland’s
ex-Premier Philp, he almost persuades us
that, owing to Laoor legislation we are on
the rim of a Great Bankruptcy. Then, as
a . curious coincidence, the same press men-
tions that probate has been granted to a few
fat wills. Ewen Cameron, a Wagga pastor-
alist, managed to leave £67,000-odd in spite of
rabbits and land taxes. George Bardwell,
another old identity in the Wagga district,
passed out at the age of 90, about three
months ago. His will has been proved at
£105,441. “The land” still seems to be a
tairly reliable means of keeping the wolf a
few hundred miles from the door.
The W. F. Brennans contemplate a flight
a few thousand feet nearer Pleaven. The
Mountains will be their camping ground
ew Year has climbed the horizon
of 1913.
Mrs. Steer Bowker and a daughter or two
will part the summer down the middle. One
half will be spent in Bowral and the rest
with the Jim Gordons up in the Braidwood
district.
Yours affectionately,
VANDORIAN.
N. qr M.”: I don’t think there is a
weirder, epitaph, tombstone or such-like in
Australia than a carved tree-trunk near
Frogmore (N.S.W.). The affair has been
roughly hewn to the shape of an unstable
obelisk, and purports to mark the tomb of
John Baker,, though, as a matter of fact,
John takes his rest elsewhere. The old man
spent the leisure hours of years over the
carvings, and achieved a turbaned head, a
horse, a large-featured individual in a top-
(locally supposed to represent the Duke
of Wellington),, and a clock-face with the
hour-hand pointing at 11. Goodness knows
what it all means. But the carver left the
district before he made his last move, so the
highly-decorated monument marks a whim
instead of a grave.

Cobra,” 15he Boot PolisK.
Chunder Loo,
Of Akim Foo,
Goes to sleep,
As others do.
Dreams he sees
The Melbourne Cup-
“ COBRA” winning—
Chunder up.
Shining like
A Victor’s shield,
“COBRA” leads
The thund’ring field
Past the packed
And cheering host,
Up the straight
And past the post.
Chunder with
A beaming face,
Wakes and cries,
“ I’ve won the race !
“ Of all dreams
That was a gem !
Get my boots
And ‘ COBRA’ them !
