MELBOURNE CHATTER.
Melbourne, December 23, 1912.
My Dear Yarrie,—
G.-G. Denman has departed to Adelaide to
stay with the State cocked hat, and the
Falters have made a bolt for the cool Mace-
don cottage. Melbourne, for the time being,
is bereft of vice-regalities; but the sun still
persists in rising and setting, and Collins-
street seems as cheerful as ever.
Mrs. Alfred Deakin takes a lively interest
in poor babies. She already has a large col-
lection of free kindergartens and creches on
her charitable list, but is now gladly adding
another, whose address is Bouverie-street.
(Didn’t one of the historic Mel-
bourne pushes” hail from Bouverie-street?
I have some disreputable recollection of the
warlike “Bouveroos”—mighty blue-metallur-
gists in their day.) Bouverie-street lives
largely by charing and washing; and Mrs.
Sugden (of Queen’s), Mrs. Norman Brookes,
Mrs. McCracken and Mayoress Hennessy are
all helping to fix up a comfortable place
where baby can play harmlessly until moth-
er s work is over, and she is ready to take
up the other end of her burden once more.
• last Melbourne flutter of “Night-
birds was flower-pelted and generally hil-
arious. Oscar Asche sat behind an acre or
so of shirtfront and comfortably filled half a
box, some of the other half was occupied
by I My Bray ton, robed beautifully in Ori-
ental silks and decorated with water lilies
Various stars and planets of the JCW'
firmament dotted the front of the house!
Hilda Spong, looking about 2st. lighter, was a
gracious sight in black chiffon. With her
was a party of doctors and their womenfolk.
Galeb 1 orter was penned in a south box A
noticeable were Peter Trafalgar Mit-
chell and his wife. Since her last foreign
trip this ? lady seems to parade a new Paris
creation ’ at every public appearance. Sybil
Arundale was off duty for the co.’s last
night, and Australian Dorothy Brunton
tripped in her small shoes.
The _Kew Canoe Carnival, which paddles
more vigorously every year, has again spread
itself over the tree-fringed Yarra. All the
river-siders came and squatted on the
bracken-clad banks, and opened hampers and
thermos bottles of tea under the gums.
Motor boats had racing events further down
stream and shied at snags that the canoes
easily slide over. Every second or third
river-maiden paddles her own shell, and has
a fair idea where the worst snags are
located. Motor-boat enthusiasts at intervals
make a concerted attack on the more or less
responsible councils, and pray them to ex-
tract tlie stumps; but the problem is always
passed along from one to the other, and each
boating party that uses the stream watches
the surface and tries to remember the valu-
able snag-hints in Pilot Mark Twain’s “Old
Times on the Mississippi.” At this latest
canoeing Knight Best, who belongs to one
of the river suburbs, was there with his dame
and pleasing train of little, fair-haired Bests
h red Kcrouse and Mrs. Fred (Essie Perrin
of old)were at the fo’c’sle head of the
function. Kcrouse paid out prizes, held a
race flag and performed other important
duties. A contingent of rowing men drifted
up from the lower reaches to encourage the
milder water-sport and admire the
girls.
David York Syme, jun., was married this
month to Jessie LaycoCk, who lived with her
parents, the Burdett Laycocks, near the
a stronghold in the Balwyn hills.
All the Melbourne Steamship Co.’s boats in
port were flapping with bunting, and the
co. s offices waved more haberdashery in the
city air. Mrs. York Syme, who is a sister
ot our lie Commissioner, was dressed in
ivory brocade with pale pink chiffon roses
horn mg the drapery of the pannier skirts.
Mr ?\- rdett haycock, in grey, received the
wedding guests at Frognall. There were
heaps of burglar-baiting presents,
and a string_band to play accompaniments to
tne rood. The bride endured the ceremony
prettily in Limerick lace and other things,
and, later on, went off to Sydney in a smart
grey dress. Trinity Church and the Laycock
house were obscured with beautiful vege-
tables, and grandmamma Cornelius looked
magnificent in a rich snreading gown of
duchess satin and Chantilly lace.
The Very Best People are feverishly mak-
mg readv for Thursday, January 9, on which
day Marjorie Jowett and Clive Fairbairn
are to put on the holy shackles of matri-
mony in the ivy-covered Scotchbyterian kirk
at loorak, and, later, listen to toasts at
Manmngham. A section of Fairbairn rela-
tives depart for England immediately after
the confetti has been swept up.
. e t a Brandt, a dainty little Hawthorn
girl, was married to barrister Ted Lewis on
December 15. Dr. Strong did the job.
after which a family party petrolled round
to the Grand Hotel to champagne the occa-
sion. Sister Mrs. Forbes fluttered about
Wl . th P ink roses in her hat. and looked like
a fresh young bridesmaid. Mrs. Brandt was
stately in black and ostrich feathers. Uncle
Crown-Prosecutor Sam Leon was on hand
with his wife and daughter Ethel, the girl
who acts in an excellent amateur way with
the Repertory Theatre folk. Andrew McClel-
land, an American friend of Elwood Mead,
was substitute for _ that absent man at the
party. Mayor Wilks Malvern) and his
Mayoress were present in their store clothes.
All hands pelted the bride with rose leaves
as she sprinted for the motor in a grev suit
with a hat full of chiffon roses. Then the
remaining members of the party sang “For
felie s a Jolly Good Female,” and the bell
boy started to tidv up.
atr i ce Holl °way, of the Get-Rich-Quick
Wallingford co., played lead in a matri-
monial performance, at St. Peter’s, Eastern
Hill, on Friday last. Bob Greig, of the
same co., was the other principal party.
It is rumored that a member of a local
wealthy clan, at present in foreign parts, is
liable to become engaged at any moment to
a daughter of Pierpont Morgan.
Melbourne is “simply dying” to see the
bride of Sam McCulloch. The lady, who
was an American widow before he claimed
her hand, accompanies him home in the New
Year, when the pair and Sam’s two school-
girl daughters will repair to Woodlands, the
station homestead so beloved of Governor
Ilopetoun during his earliest Victorian days.
A heap of nice Victorian mothers yearned
to get the handsome, silver-haired one'as son-
in-law ; but he always seemed oblivious of the
smiles of the local young women, and was
generally in the wake of some big musical
star.
The new Mrs. Sam McCulloch and the brace
of young stepdaughters are said to have be-
come delightfully chummy. Before she became
one of the family, the diplomatic lady skil-
fully set out to win the affection of the two
girls. When the win had been brilliantly
accomplished, she cried, “Behold your pros-
pective mamma !” And they, beholding, fell
upon her fair neck and said, “Why, cer-
tainly !” •
Francis Syme, the farmer among the num-
erous sons of David, has dropped the hoe
tor a while, and is climbing aboard an out-
ward-bound ark, followed by his wife, four
sons, three daughters and two nieces. The
sons are to be left at Edinburgh for educa-
tional purposes, their late grandfather hav-
ing acquired some useful knowledge there.
Mrs. Syme will hover around for a year or
two, designing to pick them up at holiday
time and take them for motor runs about
Europe.
. The Andrew Chirnsides have bought Mel-
ville Park, the Mackays’ Berwick place
mostly inhabited by racehorses and fowls.
Mackay and Mrs. Fan Dango Mackay have
quite settled down to suburban existence in
(if you please) Chapel-street.
The two splendid act-ors were enjoying their
own conversation. A had received several
letters lately from adoring females, “all
ladies, too, by George.” It then appeared
that B also was enormously admiAd by the
fair sex. I-Ie produced the week’s post from
his bosom pocket, and recollected the time
when he received as many missives per diem
from adoring dames and maidens. It was
A s practice, he confessed, to meet foolish
maidens who offered him the devotion of a
lifetime, and gently reprimand them for their
foolish conduct, give them a homily from
the nearest melodrema, and despatch them
to their parents’ arms. “Of course, you do
likewise, B?” said he. “That is my invari-
able custom,” B replied, “my invariable cus-
tom —if they are plain.”
Mrs. Gavan Duffy, daughter of the late
Allan Maclean, has been wandering in foreign
places since her father’s death. She is now
getting back within cooee of Australia.
Major Griffiths, who is our exchange
officer from India, has settled down in a
Melbourne suburb with his wife, some fowls,
a cow, and four polo ponies. The cottage is
handy to the vice-regally blessed Kooyong
Ground, and the old game of horse shinty,
honorably mentioned by the late Omar in his
rhymes, is practised early and late with
fanatical fury.
Two young she-squatters, who will shortly
come of age and be burdened with stations,
have been enlivening Melbourne. They are
orphans, hail from the Back of Beyond, have
just put up their hair, and possess fine coun-
try complexions. An aunt is chaperoning
the jolly and fortunate pair.
The leopard is the latest unfortunate
wild thing to attract the roving eye of de-
stroyer Fashion. An English duchess was
recently sighted snuggling inside a leopard-
skin motor-coat. Now the husband of the
Australian wife who made the discovery
turns pale every time the subject of next
winter’s motor-coat is mentioned. He
doesn’t know what the remarkable garment
would cost and is afraid to ask.
An English Parliamentary committee,
which is inquiring into the sale of proprie-
tary medicines, has discovered a new use
for our old nobility. A peer is a partner
in one of the shadier of these- peddling con-
cerns, and, when he was cautioned about
allowing his name to be advertised in such
a connection, he frankly replied: “What
was I to do for a living?” But the old
nobility’s prices have fallen pretty low nowa-
days. An extra-special Duchess gets only
10s. a week for the use of her name as.
patron of an “Electric Institute.”
Dolly Castles is making headway in
America. She was at latest playing in New
York with the Woods co. An American visi-
tor, commenting on her success, says Aus-
tralians mostly do take in his motherland
(Please read that sentence quietly and re-
frain from stressing the “in.”) Miss Dolly
is the hardworking member of the melodious
family. Hers was originally the smallest
of the Castles voices; but sound teaching
and tireless practice worked the usual
wonders.
A Melbourne man, who is possessed of
much wealth, is now engaged trying to ex-
plain an awkward situation to mamma.
There is a lady with whom mamma does not
get on, and whom Sonny promised to Re-
nounce. The matter was regarded as settled,
and all was well—or would have been, had
not a bothersome “itis” laid the young man
low while he was paying a friendly visit to
the renounced one. The consequent fuss and
flurry disclosed the fact that he hadn’t done
any renouncing worth mentioning. A teacup
cyclone is now understood to be brewing, and
warnings to shipping have been issued by
the weather bureau.
Merrie Boyd, an extremely young member
of the artistic Boyd family, had a glad show
last week of jugs, mugs and bowls fashioned
and fired by himself. He had the bad luck
to also fire the room in which the show was
set out on the very first day. When Society
(about a quarter of the Very Best People
are related to the Boyds) rolled up to the
Centreway lift, a flaring placard of post-
ponement met its eye. The doubly-fired stuff
is, however, now on view. Gum-leaves,
acacia, native bears and parrots are mixed
up _in the designs that enrich the weirdly
artistic vesels. The energetic young potter
has also evolved a new milk jug, which he has
patented. He discovered the special clay
whereof it is made in one of his native gul-
lies, and invented a glazing that makes the
vessel liquid-proof inside, while the outside
is capable of absorbing half its own bulk in
cooling water. He has already been rushed
with orders for this artistic and useful ad-
vance in the domestic milk mug.
Esam, a strikingly original artist of the
Backblocks and its bullocks, has hung some
200 water-colors in Centreway for a fort-
night's gazing. Mallee scrub-rolling gives
him a chance to chase six straining horses
into a frame. “Heading Cattle” is full of
movement, and the dust is so real that the
visitor who looks too long sneezes violently.
Esam loves the nearly extinct coaches that
still creak across the railless solitudes, and
has some fine old specimens for sale. Ex-
cellencies past and present have possessed
themselves at different times of this bush-
artist’s work, and Victor Hood also trea-
sures an example or so.
Early in the New Year the Charles Fair-
bairn family set out for the Continent, where
the nations rage together. The heads of the
family will escape the tedium of the long,
familiar voyage by travelling via America,
whilst daughter Esther apd the youngest of
the late Dr. Robertson’s five daughters will
mother the Fairbairn babies through the
heat of the Red Sea.
The Bowes Kelly matron having become
invalided, the family’s annual flight to its
acres in the island speck has been postponed.
Meanwhile, the hundred-odd trunks yawn
emptily in the attics, until such time as the
doctor’s motor ceases to stand at the door.
The George Tallis couple and J. C. Wil-
liamson have started on the return journey
to Australia, rt® Canada. Mrs. Williamson
is remaining in Rome to look after daughter
Marjorie, who is to spend another year or
so acquiring a high Continental polish.
From my Adelaide correspondent:—
Henley-on-Torrens eventuated in blithering heat.
Still, the large section which doesn’t mind taking
its pleasures swelteringiy—provided cool drinks are
handy to act the part of antidotes —rolled up, and
both banks were crowded. Of the racing—but who
ever watches the racing at a regatta? The Adelaide
Club and the Adelaide Bowing Club had marquees
wherein they entertained their friends, and “Sas-
siety” rolled up in force. Mrs. Mayoress Parsons,
one of the judges, wore a smart costume—blue
striped ninon, veiling white, and a hat which hinted
of Paris. In the competition for decorated water
vehicles the Kookaburra Ladies’ Rowing Club ap-
propriated the first prize with an apricot and black
■design, and Miss Linda Champion’s Crescent came
second.
The Blind, Deaf and Dumb Asylum has benefited to
the extent of £2OOO from the money left by the
late ex-Mavoress E. T. Smith.
The advance guard of the Denman polo team- —15
ponies—arrived on Tuesday. The programme ran this
way: Polo, Thursday; an amateur variety show,
Thursday night; a dinner, Friday night; more
polo, Saturday. After which our visitors hurry off
to lead the strenuous life somewhere else. The Gov-
ernor-General and the faithful Lord Dicky stay at
Marble Hill; the rest of the party endure the rigors
<of the Adelaide Club.
The incoming mail has on board Mrs. Girdlestone,
the George Milnes and their daughters and Miss Jean
Stirling. The Woodheads, from Gilberton, leave by
the Orama, for England, next. week.
The Deutscher Verein celebrated Christmas at the
May Club on Mondhy evening. It was a stifling
night—a night for repose, and peace, and good
fellowship and beer —especially beer.
Judge Gordon’s leave of absence has been further
•extended by two months —which carries him to the
■end of the vacation. Meanwhile, he continues to con-
valesce at Kangaroo Island.
Mr. G. W. Purvis died at Woodville on Thursday,
aged 91. He was a colonist since ’52, and was con-
■cerned with the building of the old wooden light-
houses on the Semaphore and Glenelg ietties, and
also with the tide-gauge at the former place. His
grandfather, Brigadier-General Walker, fought at
Waterloo.
Another of the 'G. H. Dean sons is married—“ Ted,”
the second one. The bride was Miss Grieve, of Glen
Osmond. The Dean-Gordon wedding is to be early
in the New Year.

“Hassan’’; In a recent case at Dayles-
ford (vie.) a man named Herod was fined
a *? d ° to pay £1 4s. 6d. damages
and ±5 ss. costs, for throwing bottles about
a bar. The massacre instinct is evidently
still strong in the Herods. Compared with
the original and historic crime in Judaea the
recent outburst was, no doubt, more humane,
but it was also more unjustifiable. Bottles do
not make a noise and wake the household up
at nights. They stand, cool and reserved and
silent, on the sideboard or in the cellar, and
the only sound they ever make is the cheer-
ful cry they give when the corks are drawn.
It was a dastardly thing, therefore, to slay
a bottle; and the fine was really very light
upon this descendant of the deceased
tetrarch. ,

POLITICAL
POINTS
The other day McCutcheon, of St. Ivilda,
the most hopeless reactionary in the Cab-
bage Garden’s politics, upended himself to
animadvert on the “excessive expenditure in
the Education Department”—the most
starved branch of the Victorian public ser-
vice. He holds a quaint notion that “per-
sons who could well afford to pay are get-
ting their children taught free,” regardless
of the fact that the State pays and there-
fore every citizen pays. Thus McCutcheon’s
bachelor nephew and his maiden aunt pro-
vide some portion of the education money,
and so far have made no great song about
it. A _ lot of other people also contribute
something towards the University education
of McCutch’s children, but the foolish person
probably forgot that. His idea is that if
“steps were taken to compel those who could
afford it to pay, £750,000 a year could easily
be raised.” Quite easily ! Or £7,000,000 for
that matter. And the children of those who
couldn’t afford to pay—a 545.-a-week boot-
maker working half-time, for instance —-
would be just as easily branded as paupers.
Millard, M.L.A., at Blayney (N.S.W.),
the other night, as follows: —
I have been a consistent member of the Liberal
party for the past 19 years, and during that time I have
been as free as the wind to exercise my right of judg-
ment with reference to any and every political ques-
tion affecting the interests of my constituency and
the country at large.
What would happen to the Liberal who pub-
licly favored an affirmative vote on the Re-
ferenda questions, or a Federal Land Tax,
or the “eviction” of Lord Denman, or any
other measure that had been duly damned
by his party caucus and press? The grim
fate of Speaker Willis, and of the man who
was chased for 19 years by mad jackals,
would be his for a certainty.
The sharebroker, being a sort of non-pro-
ductive parasite on the Fatman, naturally
barracks for what will make the Fatman
fatter and his own commissions more corpu-
lent. Hence J. Palmer and Sons (Sydney) :
Commonwealth politicians are trying to work up
an anti-trust mania.... The combines in Australia
have been beneficial to the public. .. .The fact
is, it is essential to the existence of a combine that
it be beneficial to the community. Directly it
misuses its power it exposes itself to competition and
is bound to come to grief, and there is no need for
legislation or prosecution to accomplish or hasten
its inevitable fate.
Quite easy, of course. Says a butcher:
“Hullo, here’s a meat ring, which has
pushed up the price of chops too high. I
can sell cheaper. I’ll start business.” He
starts, sells cheaper, and the ring, which has
the bloated profits of the past behind it,
doesn’t do a thing, of course, to the little
man.
The extraordinary demands made by
Cameron, Tasmania’s one Independent, as
the price of his support of Labor on a want-
of-confidence motion, indicate very clearly
the pass to which things may readily be
brought under our beautiful system of party
government. Among other things, Cam-
eron had the enormous assurance to ask that
all legislation of every kind should be hung
up until after the first week in September,
1913, and that no wages of any State em-
ployees should be raised. What kind of re-
presentative is this Cameron person who
can advocate that the Assembly shall do
nothing at all to earn its screw for nine
months, and at the same time insist that no
higher pay shall be given to those %vho are
actually doing the State’s work?
MERELY DESIRED A FUNERAL.
However much we may disagree with the trend of
Labor policy, it is to the. disadvantage of everyone to
attempt to make political capital out of the financial
trouble in which the present administration has in-
volved itself. —S. M. Herald.
GRANNY: “ It’s not political capital we want to
make out of it. A mere writ of habeas
corpus will do, won't it, dear?”
In these days of furious talk about the
“disloyalty” of a Government which desires
to make some use of N.S.W. Government
House grounds, it is worth recalling that
Governor Bourke, as far back as November,
1832, sent a despatch to Yiscount Goderich
urging the necessity of appropriating a large
part of the land —for wharves. He also in-
dicated the rapid growth of the city in the
direction of Farm Cove, and urged that
portion of what was called the Governor’s
domain should be thrown open for occupation.
Bourke’s own idea was to make that a resi-
dential area, with a convenient and ample
six or seven acres reserved for Government
House in the centre. He thought it prepos-
terous to hold for merely nominal use so
many acres attached to what would in time
be a town house. In these circumstances,
William McLinen and his tag-rag party,
instead of throwing all the mud at
McGowen, might take an afternoon off and
aim a bit at the statue of the dreadful dis-
loyalist Governor Bourke, standing in front
of the Mitchell Library.
THE ETCHERS.
We believe in making more comprehensive the im-
mediate aims of Liberal policy, and in passing laws
for the betterment of wage-earners as well as of other
kinds of workers. What is there to prevent any
disinterested patriot supporting a party of that kind?
And when these find their class bondage more than
they can bear, and determine, like Mr. Beeby, to
break loose, there is no need, etc., etc.

The Commonwealth’s old-age pensions still
worry those who have to administer the
business. A new Amending Bill straightens
out some crooked matters, but leaves many
problems still to be faced.
Thus, old John Smith can
own his house where he
permanently resides, and
lose no pension on that
account; but if he should
let the house and use the
rent to pay for another,
his income goes down at
once. This, may be a
serious hardship, for John
might want to move to
some place where his son
or his daughter lives, or
to be handy to the surf or
a picture-show, or make
some other move grateful
to his old age; but even
under the Amending Bill
he can only do it by for-
feiting the little screw
the Commonwealth allows
him for wearing grey
whiskers. And it doesn’t
look as if such troubles
will be avoided until the
annuities are made uni-
versal, and paid without
argument once the citizen
reaches the pension age.
The cranks who say that Australia needn’t
worry about Defence because war doesn’t
pay are up against a contradiction in the
case of the Italian grab at Tripoli. The
gore-sprinkled business cost Italy £40,000
per day for 12 months ; and as a result the
Italians get their new African estate for
less than £18,000,000. And that even in-
cludes the capital necessary to produce the
£BO,OOO a year which is to be paid to Tur-
key—poor, old, unprepared Turkey.
THE TAG .
As was to be expected, Vic-
toria’s House of “Review” tossed
out Willie Watt’s timid little Bill
providing that municipal councils
might levy their rates on the un-
improved value of land, if thev un-
accountably felt so disposed, or if
a majority of ratepayers ordered
them to do so by referendum. The
man or institution which to-dav
holds large areas idle for specula-
tive purposes pays a mere trifle in
rates, while the “small” man along-
side who dwells on his block and
has improved it to the uttermost
and thus shown himself a desir-
able citizen and an advantage to
the country, is taxed and super-
taxed for every improvement he
adds. The obvious honesty and
advantage of the new proposal so
struck the House of Land Monopo-
lists that it wouldn’t even allow
the ratepayers to say whether thev
approved of it or not.
In addition to the many oil paint-
ings of the fathers, step-fathers,
foster-fathers and grandfathers of
Federation to be painted by Aus-
tralian artists for the National
Memorial Ilall, a big picture of the
Yassberra capital site has been
ordered from a Sydney brush. The
gentlemen entrusted with the busi-
ness of handing out the jobs were
at first much inclined to give the
commission to a well-known water-colorist,
but changed their minds at the last moment,
influenced, no doubt, by the suspicion that
there might not be enough water at Yass-
Can to allow the artist to moisten his colors.
“ OUTRAGE .”
It has apparently proceeded upon the principle of
cutting off the highest heads. Let a man have put
money into a pastoral or agricultural enterprise, let
him have risked a possibly hazardous investment in
land of any sort, and he is forthwith penalised. The
Federal land tax has fallen alike on the just and the
unjust.—S.M.H., vaguely, on Labor taxation,
An art exhibit from the S. M. HERALD'S
Chamber of Political Eforrors.
The farmer who is told that he has no-
thing to gain by Protection has the doubtful
joy of seeing freights up to a higher point
than they have soared to for the past 15
years. The cry of the foreign trader is that
Australia should just grow wheat and wool
and not worry about manufactures. So, on
account of an inadequate tariff, millions go
every year to pay for the goods that are
made in other countries ; and the Australian
farmer’s wheat has to be
dragged across the globe to
feed the foreign workman
and help to pay for the for-
eign goods. And the drag-
ging—which is quite. un-
necessary because practically
all the manufactured _ goods
might be made here —is this
year costing the farmer 50s.
a ton, or (say) Is. 3d. per
bushel. In other words, for all
his year’s labor and outlay,
the Australian farmer gets
3s. 3d. per bushel, while for
the mere carting of the stuff
to Europe, the shipowner gets
Is. 3d. Certainly Is. 3d. per
bushel is a most inflated
charge, in any case ; but the
inflation is due very largely
to the shipping ring. Rings,
trusts and combines are kind
things which, the farmer may
remember, his josses of the
“Liberal” party are begging
him not to empower the Na-
tional Government to con-
trol. He may be an ass and
listen to those josses; hut, if he thinks of
the Is. 3d. per bushel he will probably whack
them instead.
“ADVANCE AUSTRALIA!”
[For The Bulletin.]
The national motto, camerados!
Presumably our guiding idea....
You, my good sir, what are you doing to advance
Australia ?
Do you eat three large meals for your country?
Do you imbibe nine, or nineteen, assorted drinks for
her?
Do you sell saucepans, silk or sausages to advance
Australia ?
Do you smoke coarse-cut tobacco or shilling cigars
to advance Australia?
Do you take Aspasia to the picture show from pat-
riotic motives, or conduct Corima to the matinfie
to advance Australia?
Do you visit Flemington, Onkaparinga, or Randwick,
in order to assist the pushing ahead of this fine
and large country?
Do you attend church, chapel or other brain-soften-
ing function to advance Australia?
Do you, upon the following morning, advance, or help
to advance, the price of saucepans, silk or
sausages, in order to advance Australia ?
My good sir, Australia is not advancing when you eat
four large meals a day.
The camerados deny that you are helping Australia
to advance just because you have increased the
price of your saucepans, or silk or sausages.
Four winners in one afternoon, and an expensive
supper at night, do not advance Australia one
inch.
Because now you wear seven-guinea suits and live
in style, do you think you help to advance
Australia ?
I do not consider, nor do the camerados consider,
that Australia is advancing just because the
Cost of Living keeps on advancing!
I do not consider, nor do the camerados consider,
that Australia is advancing, just because an
accommodating Uncle keeps on advancing !
You inquire, llow can I advance Australia?
Think, my good sir, think!
Faith may move mountains, but Thought will shift
a continent —this continent!. .. .
And you, my dear madam?
In what way are you helping to advance Australia?
Do you wear all those gaudy importations to advance
Australia?
Do you 1011, and chat, and laugh, and grow fat, and
monopolise the telephone in order to advance
Australia ?
Do you motor to the Block, or tram it to the sales,, to
advance Australia?
Do you spend a week’s housekeeping money on a
flimsy gew-gaw to advance Australia?
Do you storm the drug shops, the drapers and the
dining-rooms to advance Australia?
Do you powder and paint, and mince and giggle, and
constrict your diaphragms, your hands and your
feet in order to advance Australia?
Do you lavish time, money and affection on curious
canines to advance Australia?
I do not consider, nor do the camerados consider, that
Australia is advancing because the number of
drapers’ shops is advancing.
A riot of drapery sales, and a furious orgy of fluff
and feathers, paper flowers and hatpins cannot
advance Australia.
First-nights, chocolates, silk scarves, late suppers, and
expensive perfumes will never advance Aus-
tralia !
You do not inquire, my dear madam, “How I can
advance Australia?”
Why should you?
But you might advance. us a few more good Aus-
tralians.
You might exhibit less powder and a little more
patriotism.
Remember, that you can best help to advance Aus-
tralia by inspiriting the man who loves you to
do something to advance Australia!
And I salute you 1
N.S.W.
O. C. CABOT.

SPORTING
NOTIONS
James Neil Campbell, of Britain, is a
brave man. First, he earns his crust as an aeronaut,
which is a game in which quitters do not make big
incomes. Then, as if not satisfied with, the perils of
his ordinary avocation, he married three ladies —a
performance which stamps him as one of the bravest
of the brave. The Judge who tried the case re-
marked hopelessly: “Apparently no one knows how
you are married, or to how many” ! Campbell re-
plied, cheerfully: “I’m only married to three, sir!”
He seemed to be almost ashamed of his modesty.
Yet he was a kindly soul. He said he would do his
best to produce the death certificate of his first wife,
a Sydney woman, but added, feelingly: “I hope she
is not dead, for I spent some very happy years with
her.” Finally, by some weird stroke of tne law, he
got out of the charge of bigamy, and merely fell in
for a month for making a false declaration in the
marriage certificate of his third bride.
* * * *
Melbourne Argus discovered a new and
rising cricketer the other day. Giving the names of
Ma’s team for the Boxing Day inter-State cricket
match, it mentioned Truinper, Wadd.y, Minnett, etc.,
and then, to make up the eleven, A. N. Other. But
there are really 12 in that team, the last man being
S. Undries.
* * * *
“Vagabond” : I’d like to recommend to the
water-sports of Australia in search of an exciting
craft the native Philippine “banea” or canoe. It
is simply a log of wood shaped to sharp ends and
dug out to a shell. When the crew is aboard the
gunT is about seven-eighths of a hairsbreadth from
the water’s edge. It is propelled by paddles, some-
times fixed in a makeshift sort of rowlock made of a
bit of twisted cane, and pulled oar fashion. These
“bancas” give points to a racing eight in the bal-
ancing they require. Their nigger crews take them
out into the open sea and they ride over the waves
like a swimming sea-gull. Certainly they slop some
water aboard in choppy waves, but, being one piece
of wood, it doesn’t matter if they do fill. Not know-
ing this peculiarity, I was sitting in considerable
perturbation and a pair of soaked trousers waiting
for the craft to founder the first time I made a trip
in one. Nothing happened, however, and we reached
the end of a three-mile paddle in safetv, though the
water was up to the waist line. Taking it all round,
I think the banca the nearest to the poetry of
motion in water-craft that I’ve floated in, and when
I get hack to Sydney Harbor I’ll see if I can’t im-
port a specimen to take the girls picnicking in.
But they’ll have to parade for it in Canadian cos-
tumes, and the lunch basket and billy will have to
wear a waterproof diving suit.
* * * *
A good semi-final struggle is always a
glorious thing, and the one for the President’s
Trophy of the Victorian Bowling Association,
last week, would be hard to beat. The
outed clubs were Camberwell, which fell to
North Fitzrov, the holder of the trophy, and
Carlton, which went down before Essendon. The
interesting feature of the struggle was the equality
of the showing of the two victors, North Fitzroy
scoring 48 to Camberwell’s 31, and Essendon rolled
up 49 to Carlton’s 36. If the two left in don’t have
some excitement in the final, then there is nothing in
form.
* * * *
There are lurid stories floating around re-
garding the goings-on of a section of the Australian
XI. The team was divided into two lots, and between
them was a great gulf, that was never bridged even
when they were travelling together. According to
report, one section consisted of the men who knew
how to conduct themselves ; the other of hogs, who
put both feet in the trough—hoodlums who were
thrown out of hotels and cafSs for using bullocky
language to waitresses ; who requested total strangers
to pass the crimson bread; and who spoke of the
other portion of the team as the vermilion *****
elite. Whether this dissension had any bad effect on
the playing is hard to say, but it is a moral cer-
tainty that it did it no good. The scandal is to be
investigated by a committee appointed by the Board
of Control, and there is considerable agitation to have
the inquiry held with open doors, so that it mav be
reported by the press so far as the press dare report
it. If only half of what is told is true, the time has
come to make an example of the gentrv who have
disgraced both the country and the Board of Control.
Provided he doesn’t burst his hatband,
Melbourne University cricketer Hartkopf is a slogger
with a gay future before him. When ’Varsity went
up against St. Kilda, Hartkopf smote 138 of the
Students’ 232 ; then, feeling still hungry, he came
against Sydney Uni., and smacked the top scores, 58
and 78 respectively. There should be quite a number of
vacancies in the Australian XI shortly, and it is
pleasant to have bright colts of the Hartkopf stand-
ard to fill the gaps.
S. Aus. cricket is faced with a not un-
common difficulty, a lack of young bowlers. Murray,
Moyes, Steele and Gooden all look capable of any-
thing with the bat, but there is nobody promising
to help Crawford, Whitty and Rees with the ball,
unless it is Len Chamberlain —and he is no novice.
The S.A. Cricket Association has, therefore, pro-
moted a competition for young bowlers, each of whom
had to send down a few overs to Clem Hill, Gehrs
and Claxton, with Crawford, Campbell and Travers
.judging'. There were lots of entries, and the winner
was Riley, son of the association’s secretary, and a
schoolboy at St. Peter’s College. After which he
went into the field against Prince Alfred College,
and on a bowler’s wicket took 0 for 30 and three
for 31, while unconsidered school mates took six for
56 and six for 48. Such is cricket. In this match,
Hon Steele, who .was given an inter-State game a
year ago, made in the P.A.C. first innings a beautiful
120, while his 10 companions together amassed 37.
It is the seventh year he has been chosen for his
school, and opposing barraekers asked scornfully
whether he has not yet qualified for his old age
pension.
The cricket umpire got badly ruled out at
the recent meeting of the N.S.W. Cricket Association
when W. It. Jones, representing the North Sydney
club, moved, by his deputy, that the screw of um-
pires officiating in the State be £1 per day
in inter-State matches and in matches against Eng-
land or S’Africa, the total sum of such payment not
to exceed £lO. It was bad enough to reduce the
screw from £2 per day to £l, but some of the
delegates rose to remark that a few of the umpires
were not worth even £1 per day. That hurt, especi-
ally (and extraordinarily) as the motion was carried.
* * * *
N.S.W. Cricket Association’s treasurer
Green rose with a frown at the last meeting and
warned members that there were breakers ahead.
They had started the year with £4OBO to credit, but
in six months they had galloped through £2564, and
of the £1516 still left a lot was ear-marked to make
good deficits that were already hovering on the
horizon. They were paying out £lOO there, and
£2OO here, and there was mighty little coming in;
so the time was dead-ripe to slow down and go
cautiously, and look at every bob twice before paying
out. There would be no English team coming out
for some time, and before then a team had to be sent
to S’Africa, and that would cost a bean or two.
Distinctly, it is time to slow down.
* * * *
That picturesque actor, Allen Doone, set
out to establish a motor-car record betwkeen Ade-
laide and Sydney last week. But Doone’s luck was
out. Everything went well till they entered N. S.
Wales, and then the 40-h.p. machine began to bump
the shire councillor’s idea of a main road. Eight
miles on the Sydney side of Albury a wheel smashed,
and Doone threw up his idea of making a record.
But, wishing to see the country, he paddled along in
the car as far as Picton, where a tyre blew out, and
the idea of a record-manufacture was definitely
dropped. It was hard luck for one so beautiful.
Of course plenty of people have made the Adelaide-
Sydney run, but without thought of a record. Two
months ago Dr. McKay, of Broken Hill, and his wife
set out from the big naming town in a Ford run-
about. They went to Adelaide, 350 miles or there-
abouts ; thence to MeUiourne; thence on to Sydney;
then back via the South Coast to Melbourne, calling
in at Kosciusko on the way; and, after having a
look at the Cup, they cut north through Bendigo and
Wentworth, and got home with no worse mishaps than
a couple of punctures. The total distance covered
was about 3000 miles, much of it over bush tracks.
* * * *
The back-light problem has cropped up
again in a Melbourne motoring case. The defendant
was a doctor, and made no attempt to get awav when
hailed by the constable, who asked him why he had
no tail-light. The doctor seemed surprised. He
had lit the thing before starting, and presumed that
a heavy bump over a deep rut a little way back had
put it out. As the doctor said, when driving
through crowded streets it is very difficult to keep
a watch on the tail-light; and if the Act is to be
rigidly enforced, it means that improved lamps will
have to be put on, lit bv either acetylene or elec-
tricity which runs to an outlay of something between
£4O and £5O. The Bench expressed sympathy with
the doctor, but fined him 10s., and endorsed his
license. The Law had to be upheld. Here, then, is
a job for some brilliant young inventor—-to produce
a tail-light which won’t cost £4O or £5O, and yet
will be reliable. And if the driver can get a warning
in the event of anything going wrong with it, so
much the better.
* * * *
The United States automobile builders
have swamped the Olymp’a Exhibition (London)
with cheap cars ranging from £95 to £220. The
average is £l6O to £lBO, for which the Yankee
supplies a car that looks well and runs well.
Whether it will stand well is another matter. Most
Yankee machinery is not built to last longer than
the development and marketing of the first im-
provement. The English maker is now announcing
that he, too, in the near future, will supply low-
priced cars. But the Trust is at work, and as the
price of cars goes down the cost of petrol rushes
up, and a further rise to 2s. is threatened as a
New Year gift. There’s a fortune for the man
who can devise a cheap substitute for petrol. One
is being tried, a mixture of benzol and alcohol.
Benzol is a distillate of crude naphtha, a coal-tar
by-product. Alcohol can be made by wood and
vegetable distillation at Id. per gallon; but there
seems to be trouble with Excise people all over the
world, which makes it impossible to put this cheap
and efficient substitute for petrol on the market in
huge supplies. Presumably the Trust, a huge, inter-
national and shameless octopus, uses its influence
to maintain vexatious regulations which, under exist-
ing circs., are an ironclad wall of defence to its
flagrant monopoly.
More than a chance that Australia may
be extensively represented at the big cycling road
races of Europe next season, as Don Kirkham, J.
Wilson (both Vic.) and F. Keefe (Tas.) are said to
contemplate a raid on the French events. Road
racing in France and Germany is quite a business,
from which the rider makes a decent income apart
from prizes he may win. The best riders are all
under engagement to manufacturing houses, who not
only pay them well, but make elaborate arrangements
for their training, mounting and feeding. In fact,
it is almost as good to be a racing cyclist in France
or Germany as a racehorse in Australia.
* * * *
They seem to have got Australian sculling
champion Pearce in a cleft stick. The rules gov-
erning the championship lay it down that any holder
must defend his title when called upon to do so
within two months of the date of the challenge.
Pearce is booked to row Barry for the championship
of the planet on the Thames. Immediately he
arranged to go he was called on to defend his Aus-
tralian title. He declined, as he had other fish to
fry, and could not attend to two fryingpans at once.
The challengers persist, and it is now said that they
will, in the event of Pearce refusing to row, demand
that he forfeit and let them row among themselves
for the vacant place. Pearce says he will neither row
nor forfeit, though how he can avoid one or the other
it is hard to see. It is understood that if he declines
to row, the championship will be declared vacant,
and Charles Towns and Felton will race for the
empty place and £2OO aside. Under the rule the
championship will be vacant shortly after Christmas ;
and as Pearce does not leave Australia till February,
he may attack the winner and pick him clean before
he goes* to measure himself with Barry.
Let us rejoice! The Australian eight-oar
crew to Stockholm returned with £4O still intact in
the team purse. The good management must have
been absolutely monumental, as the purse was not
over-full wiien the crowd went away. The Henley
Cup, won by defeating the Leander on the Thames, is
due to arrive in January.
J. C. Parke, the top-notcher of the British
tennis team, put up a tall record in Australia by losing
only one set of II played. And consider the quality
of his scalps—Brookes and Heath (Vic), A. B. Jones
(N.S.W.), and St. John (Q.). The match between
Britain and Australia, at Sydney, on January 17,
will see him again matched with Brookes, and the
contest will be worth going far to see.

The Davis Cup contests in Melbourne
gathered from the crowd close upon £3OOO in gate
fees. Ihe exes., including £9OO for the erection of
a temporary stand, do not nearly mop up the whole
amount, and it is believed there will be a profit of
(say) £l3OO. Of this, about £6OO goes to England.
England is on a good wicket, as Australia’s share
of the profits of the games held there in 1907 was
a miserable £l3l Bs. 4d. This year the English
body gets it all back, about five times over.
Jack Johnson is not cursed with an imag-
ination. When he got out of the charge of having
abducted Lucille Cameron, on the ground that the
youftg lady was sufficiently old to decide whether
she would go to the devil or not, he married the
girl—in the same room in which his just-buried first
wife had committed suicide. Lucille, apparently,
has no overplus of imagination either. The public
was very wroth, and talked loudly of lynching the
cynical black with the gold-lined mouth. But, as
usual in Chicago, nothing happened; and Johnson
started to take his wife round openly. When they
entered a caf6 the other evening, the manager hur-
ried forward and informed them they would not be
allowed to stay. Johnson withdrew, leaving his wife
to enjoy the advantages of her color. But the man-
agement was obdurate. Mrs. Johnson must leave,
too. As she did so, the manager is said to have
remarked: “A few more incidents of this sort and
you will understand why the first Mrs. Johnson
suicided!” It was pretty brutal; but the girl de-
served it.
The bi-colored marriage is a horror which hits out
in some unexpected ways. Some time ago a wealthy
New Yorker fell in love with one of his type-ladies,
and proposed marriage. She accepted him, and in-
formed him that, although under 30, she had been
already married, borne two children, buried them
both and divorced her husband, Felix P. Jackson, or
some " such thing. This tragic history endeared the
lady more than ever to her new admirer, and with
the idea of blotting sad memories away he never
asked her anything about Felix P. Jackson—not for
a long time; not till a baby came home: a baby
with white skin but negro features. There was no
negro blood in the arteries of that infant, but he
bore the brand of his mother’s early shame.
At the Sydney Stadium last week O’Grady
supplied another excellent illustration for the old
couplet—“He who fights and runs away, lives to
fight another day.” Leon Bernstein was the pursuer
on this occasion, and also the victim; for O’Grady
hit him often and hard, but invariably bolted before
he could get anything in return. Indeed, his back
was scarred from slithering along the ropes in his
wild crab-rush out of danger. The exasperated
Frenchman at the opening of the ball was quite fast
enough to keep up with the Donaldson of the Ring,
and he did it; but he received some vicious upper-
cuts that caused him to slow down for breath,
and stand right off for a while, after which he made
further advances only with the utmost caution. In
the 14th round he caught O’Grady. He was running
when he got hit, and before the Hibernian knew
what had happened he was on his back. It
was a bad floor bump, but at nine O’Grady
struggled up, and took another smasher which
sent him down for four more. Hardly was
he up again before he once more went down for
three, and when he came up still another time, Ber-
stein stood in to kill. Instead, however, of doing
anything of the sort, he stopped a vicious uppercut
which rocked him, steadied him down, and gave the
Australian a respite till the sound of the gong.
Bernstein had missed his chance. Nothing else
of consequence happened till the last round,
after which a majority vote gave O’Grady the
verdict, and the G°’d retired, remarking that the
times were out of joint.

Maoriland Treasury last year drew
£75,611 from the tax on Totalisator investments.
It was a goodly sum with which the Government
could well do and which the punter never missed.
The chief contributor was the Auckland province,
£9502. Canterbury came next with £8046, Well-
ington £5280, little Wanganui £3599, and the
Metropolitan Trotting Club £5039. The rest was
made up by the other 13 clubs on a descending scale.
* * * *
N.S.'W. football for the coming season
will be of the stay-at-home order. The N.S.W. Rugby
League asked the Northern Union to send out a team,
but the English, for some reason, declined the invita-
tion. The Rugby Union will neither receive visitors nor
send them, and the talk about wild activity in foreign
parts by the Soccer crowd turns out to have been
mere flaim. The Soccer chiefs will wisely devote their
energies to developing the inter-State side of their
attractive game. The chief excitement will be the
wild-cattle rush for the playing grounds.
Another swimming marvel bobbed up at
Manly (Sydney) the other day, in Churchill Cracken-
thorp, a water-sprite of 13 summers. He fulfilled the
Royal Life Saving Society’s conditions by swimming
over or through 600 yards, fully dressed, in 14min.
54sec.—3min. 6sec. under the society’s standard.

5 per cent, of stakes won, which may come to

When Ethe \bert Ambrook Southee, the
Ma State s new Rhodes scholar, leaves for Oxford
N.S.W. will see the last for a time of one of its
best all-round athletes. In one day he recently
bagged the State and Australasian championships for
a broad jump, for a 220-yard sprint and a few othe-
tnfles. Besides that, he has the Sydney High School
Cup tor best all-round athlete in his year. At the
University he is president or sec. of just about
everything that has a president or sec., and a
in football; so when he sets out for the Fog the
Uuiversit.y will be busy with by-elections for a
month. Southee was born in Cootamundra ‘ ? 2 years
ago.
* * * *
Frank K. Kerr, the Victorian Rhodes
Scholar, goes away after having taken as bio- a bite
as he can chew out of Victorian athletic champion-
ships. He has won the University mile champion-
ship four times, the inter-Uni. mile and half-mile
championships, the five-mile eross-countrv champion-
ship in record time, the inter-collegiate mile four
times. He has a '‘blue” for cricket, football and
athletics, and captained Queen’s College cricket and
football for four years. In the brains line he has
an almost equal collection of scholarships and prizes,
and has done it all in 23 years.
* * * *
Cabled dead, the racehorse Common, in
his 25th year. He was one of the few that won the
sporting triple crown—the Two Thousand Guineas,
Derby and St. Leger. More extraordinary, he was de-
feated only once. When he retired from the track
he was purchased by sportsman Blundell Maple for
£15,000 for the stud. It was a bad spec, as Com-
mon was, comparatively speaking, a failure in that
department.
Bill Sikes lias been taking more than a
passing interest in cricket trophies lately. Re-
cently he relieved C. G. Macartney of the best part
of his English tour mementoes ; now he has made a
considerable hole in Clem Hill’s large pile.
Australian runner A. B. Postle some time
ago made for S’Africa, where they take more interest
in running than we do in Australia. At latest he was
in Jo’burg, and had met something like disaster,
having been outrun by R. E. Walker, the African
champion, in a match over 100 yards. Walker won
by two yards, in 9 3-ssec. The time is equal to that
once put up by that busted champion, Arthur Duffey,
the Yankee flier who was disqualified for admitting
that he did what everybody knew he did. It is a
record for the world. Probably some cave-man fled
faster when the dinosaur was close behind, but the
timekeeper left also, and the event was not chronicled.
If the punter ever had any doubts as to
his being an ass, the question should be settled by
an analysis of what the bookmaker hands the Sydney
metropolitan racing clubs. Of the £137,000' paid
out by the clubs in the shape of added money, no
less than £59,100 was contributed by Patrick
O’Scariot in ring fees. And if O’Scariot pays that
huge sum merely to be allowed to get his teeth into
the punter, what fat bacon those punter boobs must
be! Isn’t it time the State took a hand in the game,
and insisted that if these punting boobs will lose
their cash it shall be lost to the State and used for
public purposes?
* * * *
Carter, Adelaide’s best-known football
umpire, has had the courage to claim legal damages
from a particularly dogged barracker, who stupidly
went into details: “You are a cheat,” “This man
bets,” and so on. But somehow hard words lose
their sting with the lapse of time, and when the law-
suit came on, full three months after the insult, it
was “settled on satisfactory terms” (not made pub-
lic), “all imputations of unfairness withdrawn.”
The chief item of interest was Carter’s statement
that he “had orders to keep away from people, and
was forbidden to be drawn into conversation with
anybody at all concerning football.” “Were you
under orders from the League to shun the society of
your fellow-men?” counsel asked; and the boss
whistler replied mournfully: “Yes. An umpire has
no friends at all.”
UNDUE INTERFERENCE.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
In a recent fight With S. Burns in Londbn,
Johnnie Summers prayed earnestly in the ring during
the interval. — Cable.
The hoxer dumped him down to pray,
His seconds flapped the tow’l.
The blokes who went to see the play
Of gory mits had words to say—
They said ’em with a howl.
For by the rules ’tis clearly shown
A pug should battle on his own.
The holy lad put up his dukes,
And waded in like Ilell.
He landed with some frightful flukes.
And hooked most punishing rebukes
Whenever it befell
That Burns in his defence was weak,
Or if he turned the other cheek.
Good Johnnie prayed for help to shift
His rival’s ear awry.
His voice in thankfulness he’d lift
Each time the other’s nose was biffed,
Or when lie bunged an eye,
And when with uppercuts he scored
Most piously he praised the Lord.
The scrap was o’er; with eyes a-glare
Poor Burns was down and dumb.
The referee then said that where
Two lads were matched it wasn’t square
To ring in Kingdom Come.
Amid a great, applauding howl
Said he: “It’s Burns’s, on a foul!”
VICTORIA.
BILLY T.
A CHOKING CHILD.
CONVULSIONS AND BLACK IN THE
FACE FROM STOMACH WORMS.
FIVE EXPELLED BY ONE DOSE.
The terrible sufferings which a child
afflicted with stomach worms has endured is
told in this statement made by Mrs, C. A.
Madden, of Kurrajong, N.S.W. Her little
boy, at the age of 16 months, became very
irritable and cross, and the doctor attri-
buted the cause to stomach worms. The
child became so bad that on three or four
occasions it; went into convulsions and almost
choked, owing to the worms passing up to
the throat. Various treatments were tried,
with practically no success, until Comstock’s
“Dead Shot” Worm Pellets were used with
the following marvellous results. These are
Mrs. Madden’s own words: —“For some six
months,” writes Mrs. C. A. Madden, of Kur-
rajong, N.S.W., “my little boy, now aged
one year and ten months, has suffered from
Stomach Worms, which the doctor attri-
buted to the use of impure water. Until he
was 16 months old he enjoyed very good
health, but subsequently he became very
irritable and cross, and, later on, he was ex-
amined by a doctor, who stated that the
cause was Stomach Worms. Various treat-
ments were tried, and on one occasion a
Stomach Worm was passed from the
bowels. The evacuations from the bowels
were always stained with blood, and the
motions were quite unnatural. On three or
four occasions the child was almost choked,
and went into convulsions, and almost black
in the face, through the worm passing up
the throat, and I had to force my finger down
the throat to remove the worm. Only last
week I was strongly advised to try Com-
stock’s “Dead Shot” Worm Pellets, and hav-
ing obtained a box, I gave the child a dose
in accordance with the instructions, and the
same evening five worms, varying in length,
the longest 15 inches, were expelled.”
Comstock’s “Dead Shot” Worm Pellets
are a purely vegetable preparation for the
eradication of Worms in children and adults.
For sale by all chemists and storekeepers,
price 2s. 9d. per packet, or will be sent post
paid at the same price by the W. H. Com-
stock Co., Ltd., 23 Lang Street, Church Hill,
Sydney.

“New Rival”
Shotgun Cartridges
An even spread of shot with good penetration is
what you need for bird shooting, especially for fast
flyers. It is these qualities that have made Win-
chester “New Rival” cartridges so popular among
bird shooters. The Winchester system of loading
and wadding, which is the result of vast experi-
ence in manufacturing shotgun cartridges, seems
to be rather more successful than others. Besides,
the corrugation around the head of Winchester
cartridges, which is patented, is a great feature.
Winchester “ New Rival” cartridges are made extra
strong to permit reloading and are always satis-
factory. They are sold by all dealers.

fiIVAL”
“Way Verley Penn’’: Hindmarsh (S.A.) identity
Janies Chittleborough, one of the Buffalo consign-
ment of pioneers who reached Glenelg (S.A.) on Dec.
28, 1836, turned 80 the other day. He can remi-
nisce by the hour concerning the Holy City's earliest
playhouses. The first was a wooden structure on
North Terrace, a long hop, step and jump from the
site of the present Adelaide Royal. Next one Lazaar,
the progenitor of Sam and Abraham Lazaar, got a
theatre going in Gilles Arcade, Currie-street, with
which enterprise George Coppin and Harry Lambert
were associated for many years. The place is now a
livery stable. At the same time as the Lazaar show
was running a Mr. Deering ran a little theatre at
the back of the Aerated Bread Co.’s old premises in
Waymouth-street.
“Melb.” : Asche’s Christmas attraction at the Royal
(Melb.) will be “Antony and. Cleopatra.” It is singu-
lar how little this play was acted until almost our
own time. The boy performers of Shakspeare’s day
would hardly be convincing as Cleopatra; and the
succeeding actresses, such as Oldfield, Bracegirdle,
Woffington, Cibber and Pritchard, stood off. They
were not over-particular ladies, mostly, but yet ap-
pear to have been of the opinion, later expressed by
Mrs. Siddons, that it was degrading to act the
Enchantress. How magnificent Mrs. Siddons would
have been in the part! Yet there is no record of her
attempting it. Nor would Miss O’Neill act it, though
she would have shone, too. I cannot find that Mrs.
Warner, Helen Faucit, Ellen Terry, or Fanny Kemble
risked it. The first to make a speciality of it was
Miss Gl.vn, who was short and almost ugly, yet
fixed herself up grandly. Phelps brought out the play
at Sadlers’ Wells; but C. Kean’s ..superb London pro-
ductions did not include it. In later time it has been
a good deal acted by Miss Wallis, Mrs. Potter, and
others. I have a dim idea that Montgomery and Miss
Cleveland gave it in Melbourne. The last production
there was by Miln, for whom Hennings painted
scenery—his last work. Beerbobm Tree gave a sump-
tuous London production—which he repeated in Ber-
lin—and Asche has the props, got together on that
occasion.
“The Glad Eye” has been celebrating its first anni-
versary in London Strand Theatre. It is still winking
hilariously to crowded audiences, and looks like last-
ing for a second year. Et'hel Dane, an Australian,
has made a big hit as the blithesome maiden whose
merry optic produced so much mischief. After the
anniversary performance on Nov. 4 there was a big
congregation on the stage, where Miss Dane cut the
anniversary cake and drank a bead or two of the
wine of gratitude.
Joseph Blasheek, with Mildred Wrighton, is getting
good notice in London as an Australian Humorist. As
former president of the Dagonet Club in Melbourne,
he has got on the right side of George R. Sims, and
that experienced old journalist can make things very
nice with the Press in London for anyone who is
“solid” with him.
In Melbourne a benefit is being arranged for Flora
Graupner, who has been putting up a long, hard fight
against the grimmest sort of illness. The little
woman has earned the help. She made her stage ap-
pearance in the early 80’s as a juvenile Josephine
in “Pinafore.” That was under Mrs. G. B. Lewis.
In 1890 she was Gianetta in the first Australian pro-
duction of “The Gondoliers.” Billy Elton, Howard
Vernon, Maggie Moore and Flo Young figured in the
cast. She played Cicely in the first production of
“Marjorie,” Michaela in “Carmen” (Marie Halton
was the gypsy charmer, Walshe was Jose and Charles
ltyley the toreador), Phyllis in “lolanthe” (Ida
Osborne was in the name part), and “The Old
Guard,” in which the Eltons made their farewell
appearance in 1892.
“Harrison 0.”: One of the things about Shaw
which provokes my admiration is that he does not
have to provide his interiors with doors on all sides.
The average dramatist —Pinero, the much-boomed
craftsman, is a bad offender in this respect—appears
unable to get along unless the boudoir of the affluent
Mrs. Lais, or the garret of the starving heroine, is
studded with doors like a pub corridor. Shaw's in-
terior in the first act of “Man and Superman,” which
has just been produced by the Melbourne Repertory
Theatre, has only one door, yet he manages to get
the eight characters on and off without mishap.
According to the London Daily Chronicle the
tongue-twisting song of the approaching pantomime
season there is to be “Woolloomooloo” —a name
which has been popularised in London halls of late
by Herbert Rule.
From London: The Palace Theatre, which has been
specialising for some time in undraped female, has
nearly had its teeth jerked out of its head by the
bit of the Lord Chamberlain, who has refused a
license to “A Venetian Night”—a new wordless
piece in which a giddy reproduction of Eve before
the Fall was to have been frequent and free. The
play was to have been produced on a Monday, and
was extensively boomed in advance. On the Sunday
night there was a final rehearsal, at which an official
of the Lord Chamberlain’s Department was present.
It was so much of an undress function that the
official fled with the news that the thing was alto-
gether too raw to be licensed. The manager scurried
round and borrowed fill-up turns; but, having spent
£4OOO on the “Venetian” preparations, he is a very
enraged Batt. Yet he has only himself to blame. 1-Ie
had so profitably exploited the large, undraped
female that at length he took her for granted, and
thought he could do what he liked with her. He
started the craze with Maud Allan, a Canadian dam-
sel who wore a handful of beads, and his latest speci-
men was Gaby Deslys, whose going-to-bed scene was
just about the most daring ever-shown in London. A
few steps more, and Batt would have taken the license
of the Palace over the precipice, so the Chamberlain
has done him a good turn really.
“Norbell”: The dramatic “Faust,” which is to be
one of J.C.W.’s Christmas attractions, was last pre-
sented in Sydney in 1895 at Her Majesty’s under
George Rignold’s notable management. It was a
revelation in stage craft, from the uncanny effects
in the prologue to the weird and wonderful steam
curtain just before the apotheosis of Marguerite.
Marie Lohr made her first appearance in that pro-
duction. She was one of the tiny kiddies who
fought for a hot cross bun on the steps of the Nur-
emburg Cathedral at the instigation of Handsome
George Nick. Marie’s talented ma, Kate Bishop,
was in the cast as Martha. George Rignold was
Meph., the brilliant Scott Inglis was the rejuvenated
Faust, and the late Stirling Whyte was old-man
Faust. Cyril Keightley, who is now holding up his
end at Drury Lane and adjacent parts, played Siebel,
and Harry Plimmer was Valentine. Miss Agnes
Knights was Marguerite; and James Hazlitt, Harry
Hodson, D’Orsay Ogden, Frank Harcourt and George
Marlow helped the show along. In fact, there were
quite a lot of clever people in the cast, including
me.
MELBA’S HATE.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
“I hate dress.” — Melba, to a London interviewer.
Hate pretty clothes! Excuse us, please,
If -till incredulous we seem.
To womankind such words as these
Are blasphemous in the extreme,
For woman’s hell of blank despair
Is having nothing fit to wear.
With dress forgotten, shops would be
Deserted by the female crew,
And dreary, grey monotony
Would surely brood in Heaven, too.
Is woman’s Eden in the sky
Not dubbed in hymns, “Sweet buy and buy” ?
If true it is, as you confess,
You find those splendid gowns you wear
A fearful trial, a weariness
Of flesh and spirit, why still bear
The pangs? A million sisters pray
You will your burdens heavy lay
Upon their shoulders right away.
VICTORIA.
SILAS SNELL.

SEE THE BEST TROTTERS at
VICTORIA PARK., Dec. 28
THE last Trotting Meeting for 1912, to be held at Victoria Park, next Saturday, will prove of
unique interest to country visitors in town for the A.J.C. Meeting. Australia’s finest Trotters
will be seen at work upon the most up-to-date track South of the Eine. The Trotting Track
cost ovei /10,000 to instal, being 1-g- miles in circumference. The Distance Starting, adopted by
the leading Victorian Trotting Clubs, is used at Victoria Park.
Many Country Clubs have already adopted Distance Starting, and, in a short time, this system
will be in vogue throughout N.S.W.
The Victoria Park Grounds are artistically laid out and dotted with flower beds—the whole
presenting a pleasing picture. Commodious Retiring and Sitting Rooms in the Paddock Stand and a
well furnished Dining Room, to seat 500 guests, occupies half the ground floor. De Groen’s Vice-
Regal Band will render selections during the afternoon’s sport.
If you wish to spend a really pleasant afternoon, come to Victoria Park next Saturday.
Full particulars from
The Secretary V.P.R.C., 20-21 Imperial Arcade, Pitt Street, Sydney.

ANSWERS to CORRESPONDENTS.
No notice (except of a hostile, character) will be
taken of communications insufficiently stamped. Name
and address must appear on MSS. No liability is
undertaken re contributions voluntarily submitted,
whether sent by post or handed in; and ’in MS. will
be returned unless stamps accompany.

“Altair”: “Ivuscobin” (B. 5/12/’l2) re-
lates a thrice-removed story of an “in-
vasion” of Australia in 1813. It is all a
fairy tale. As Ernest Scott (“Terre Napo-
leon”) and other authorities show, Aus-
tralia was much more secure from foreign
aggression at that date than it is at the
present moment. The British navy was
then supreme on all the seas, and the few
French sloops left were mostly looking for
aiding places. There were always one or
two British warships about Sydney, not to
mention the forts on South Head and else-
where, and the few hundred soldiers of the
N. S. Wales corps. The alleged destruction
of the IT whalers is even more incredible
than the contemplated capture of Sydney.
Those old-time whalers were not toothless
craft like the cetaceans they hunted. They
carried guns and letters of marque, and
filled in the off-whale season with a bit
of privateering. Two of them arrived in
Sydney as early as 1799 with a rich, Spanish
prize all the way from Peru, and the other
whalers promptly left in search of a few
more of the valuable fish—which may ex-
plain the scarcity of oil at a later date.
Scott certainly states that Napoleon “or-
dered” the taking of Port Jackson in .1810,
but, for the very sufficient reasons above-
cited, the order was never carried out.
From the Sugar Commission’s report: —
It might safely be said that the opinion generally
held by medical witnesses is that the white man can
lead a healthy life and rear a vigorous family in tro-
pical Australia, provided he adapts himself to his sur-
roundings as regards diet and clothing, and' avoids
alcoholic excesses, which, while injurious in any-cli-
mate, are particularly debilitating in the tropics,
and a fruitful source of sickness amongst the workers
in the sugar industry. In addition to the medical
evidence a large amount of testimony was given by
other witnesses to the effect that the adoption of
white labor had had a beneficial effect generally, and
the virtual unanimity of opinion against any proposed
return to black labor conditions is remarkable. In
this connection it is interesting to observe that one
witness stated as his experience that the lads reared
in tfie district ipade the best farm hands.


LooK at the Photographs.
They Tell a Graphic Story
Fig. 1 merely illustrates the relation of the iron-frame and the wrest-
plank (that is, the tuning-block) in an ordinary imported piano. The tuning-
pins and wires have been removed.
Fig. 2 is a nearer view of the wrest-plank of the same piano.
Fig 3 is the Beale Patent Piano Frame, removed from the piano, and
the end of the wrest-plank cut off to show the wrest-pin in the solid iron frame.
Fig. 4 is a nearer view of the Beale Patent frame shown in Fig. 3.
Now—
The wrest-plank of an imported piano is a block of wood about sft. Din.
long, and one and a half inches (l|in.) thick, into which no less than two
hundred and sixteen steel spikes are driven. Each spike is about 2£in. long
and |in. in diameter; each one is hammered into a hole previously drilled in
the wrest-plank. The holes are all slightly smaller than the spikes, so that
when the latter are driven home the wood will pinch tightly round them.
Consequently each spike acts like a wedge in the wooden plank, tending to
burst it asunder.
But that is not all.
To each spike a steel music-wire is attached, and the spike is wrested
round with a key, thus coiling up the wire and tightening it. It is to resist
the tendency of the wire to turn the spike (or “wrest-pin”) back again that the
wood must pinch tightly upon the spikes.
When all the wires are at full tension—that is to say, when the piano is
in tune at concert pitch—the total strain upon the two hundred and sixteen
steel spikes is nearly sixty-seven thousand pounds (67,0001 b.) not quite
thirty tons.
No block of wood can withstand such a strain for long, especially in the
trying Austialian climate. Remember that a dual strain is exercised upon it
- theie aie first of all two hundred and sixteen wedges bursting the plank
asunder, and then there is the levering strain of 67,0001 b.
Of couise, the wood must sooner or later give way. The pins become
loose and the piano will no longer remain in tune.
fsd
ALL THIS IS OBVIATED IN THE BEALE PIANO by
our wonderful patent METAL wrest-plank, forming part of
the usual iron frame. (See Fig. 3.)
The pins are merely placed in the holes, not hammered
in. The thirty-ton strain—or even double or treble that
strain—will not have much effect, will it?
And, seeing that the pins are case-hardened steel, working
in smooth, lubricated metal bearings, no amount of turning
will materially loosen them.
In addition to this advantage of permanence in tune
which is attained by our patent steel tuning system, a
GREATLY ENHANCED TONAL QUALITY TS ALSO
OBTAINED.
We cannot illustrate the tone of a piano—you must hear
that for yourself. Call and let us demonstrate their tonal
quality.

ENTHUSIASTS —!
This is the camera for those who detest carrying anything in the
hands, and have a dislike of everything bulky. It can be carried —as your
watch —in a vest pocket without any inconvenience, and will tell the
story of your holidays and other days better than a notebook. Being
free of projections and complications and entirely self-contained, weigh-
ing only B£oz., you will appreciate what a wonderful example it is of
mechanical ingenuity in the saving of bulk and weight.
The Y.P.K. is always ready for immediate use; can be loaded and
unloaded in daylight; and as the developing and printing of films can
also be carried out in daylight, no darkroom is necessary at all. Alto-
gether a desirable instrument to possess, both for the beginner and the
more advanced worker who wants a handy pocket camera for photo-
graphic note-taking—notes which may subsequently form the basis of
large pictures.
The Vest Pocket Kodak Camera is fitted with a carefully selected
Lens, Kodak Ball-bearing Shutter, Brilliant Reversible Finder and
Auto-time Scale. Loads in daylight with Kodak Film for eight
exposures.

“Billy B.”: I didn’t really know what
dreadful enemies the Peace Societies and
Labor red-raggers were to Australia until I
read the Times (London) the other day.
When the said Peace Societies and red-
raggers urge the freedom of the great White
Commonwealth to the Asiatic they
sureiy know what the morals of the
colored races are, otherwise, I take it,
they would not dare to face argu-
ment. And here are some of the morals of
the inhabitants of India as set out in the
Times :
Among the Hindus, forming two-thirds of the
population, the service of the temples is held to
demand large numbers of girls dedicated in infancy
to religious prostitution.... When in 1891, under the
persevering influence of the late Mr. B. M. Malabari,
the age of consent for married as well as unmarried
girls was raised from 10 to 12 years, there was a
storm of opposition from orthodox Hindus.
Three private Bills were introduced re-
cently in the Viceroy’s Chamber to improve
matters, and Sir Reginald Craddock, while
welcoming the Bills on behalf of the Gov-
ernment, had this to say :
It was proposed to raise the age of consent, the
relation of husband and wife being excepted, from
under 12 to under 13. . . .It will be penal for any per-
son having the custody of a minor to cause or en-
courage her seduction ; and power is to be given, as
in the English law, to take away minor girls from
the custody not only of strangers but of guardians,
even of parents, who are bringing them up to a life
of immorality.
And Craddock was careful to explain that
the British Government “was not actuated
by any desire to assail the religion or to
cast a slur upon the social usages of the
people of this ancient land.” Now, I want
to know what is actuating the Peace So-
cieties and the red-rag mongrel-race advo-
cates and others in battling to foist on this
clean young nation the awful horde that
has, as part of its religious practices, the
violation of tender children. If I don’t re-
ceive a prompt and satisfactory answer, I’ll
be compelled to believe that my horribly-
unpleasant suspicions are correct.
“Milmeridien,” on a Northern scandal: —
There is a cry, in Adelaide particularly, against
the Northern Territory Aborigines Protection Depart-
ment. Yet there is no public department which, by
speaking out, could so easily smash up its detractors.
Why doesn’t the Minister relax the rule of silence
and permit the heads to protect themselves in print?
If he did that, the officers could paralyse these mean
crawlers whose one desire is to withdraw all protec-
tion from the blacks, so that there might be a return
to the old tariff of nothing a day and find themselves.
One Darwin Wowser is up against the Department
because Beckett, the Chief Inspector, refused to sup-
ply him with 20 strong natives to do his tin-sampling
at ss. a week —work which Malays had thrown up
at 7s. a day and white men wouldn’t tackle at 10s. a
day. Another cheap-labor lover is interested in the
despatch of news to Adelaide newspapers, so that
distortions of aborigines protection facts, as of
mining and other facts, plead louder against the
muzzling of the officials. Add to this a leakage of
telegraph messages, which amounts to a censorship,
and there is every inducement to the best-meaning
official to “ca’ canny” as a prelude to returning to
the rotten ways of the old regime. The administra-
tion is tending that way with great rapidity.
“Trotter”: The Australian National Gov-
ernment decides to make a start in the
Northern Territory by offering 26 farms,
averaging about 500 acres. These are re-
cently surveyed lands on the navigable Daly
River, and are recommended by the Land
Classification Board (Ryland, Clarke and
Day). The 26 blocks will be handed over
free to approved persons; but as immense
tracts of good surveyed land are in the
vicinity, it is expected that the balance will
be taken up at a satisfactory rental. When
a start has been made, smaller areas will be
offered for intensive cultivation. The Board
will soon have plans of land now under sur-
vey in the Waterhouse district, close to the
railway, 70 miles from Darwin. The blocks
now offered free are 100 miles from Darwin,
but only accessible by sea and river in about
150 miles. Or it is possible to go by rail
via Brock’s Creek, and thence by road.
By rail it is 104 miles to Brock’s
Creek, and by road 70 miles. Trans-
portation will be provided by Govern-
ment vessel, trading regularly between Dar-
win and the settlement; and the settlers will
also get help from a Government demon-
stration farm near the blocks. The district
has been proved eminently suitable for grow-
ing maize (two crops a year), tobacco,
sugar, cotton, sweet potatoes, citrus fruits,
bananas, etc. The estimated rainfall is the
same as at Darwin, about 60in. Ad-
vances of material will be made to settlers
at cost price, and they will also get the
offer of any Government odd jobs to keep
the pot full. Applications will be received
up to the end of February, and possession
can be had about May. The date when
there will be a local market for the pro-
ducts of the farms isn’t mentioned.
The Sydney importer has been wailing
bitterly again. So much repulsive rotten-
ness is traded off on the Australian public
as “figs in boxes” that Tudor, of the Cus-
toms, issued a regulation to the effect
that, being put into circulation, every
box of this imported fruit should be
labelled “Sound, fresh and clean.” The
importers know that the prime Smyrna
goods are often chiefly maggots ; hence they
howled at Tudor for daring to suggest that
they should give any sort of guarantee with
the food. And the S. M. Herald, which
can’t make up its alleged mind whether to
cry Stinking Fish or be a decent Australian,
got down on the wrong side of the fence
again and declared that the pure food regu-
lation was a mean subterfuge to sneak in
Protection, although “this particular 'fruit
cannot be produced in Australia.” If the
Herald tried for 24 hours it couldn’t tell a
more brazen lie. As a matter of fact, figs
can be grown almost anywhere in the Com-
monwealth, and in S. Aus. they are about
as cheap as thistles —you can get a barrow-
ful of them for a shilling.
“The Oldest”: In connection with the
municipal jubilee of Newtown (N.S.W.),
all the Sydney dailies have raked up the
discredited legend about the semi-historical
Margaret Catchpole being identical with
Mary Reibey. As a matter of fact, the
latter lady- came to Australia eight years
before Margaret Catchpole, namely in 1792,
in the Royal Admiral. She lived in Reibey
House, Newtown, from the early ’4o’s until
her death in 1855, whereas Margaret Catch-
pole died in 1819, according to the entry in
the register kept by Parson Fulton, at Rich-
mond (N.S.W.).

Fashions come and fashions go, but the
appreciation of Scrubb’s Cloudy Ammonia,
like the brook, goes on for ever. It is admir-
able in the bath, possessing wonderfully in-
vigorating properties, and is an ideal water-
softener, while in hot climates it is exten-
sively used for allaying irritation caused by
mosquito bites. Stains and grease spots on
clothing, garments and furniture can be also
removed by its use. The good “hausfrau” is
ever enthusiastic regarding its many merits
in the kitchen and pantry.*
