BANANA LAND
“Flinderino”: It is pleasing to note that
many of the stopping-places on Bananaland’s
new coastal railway will carry aboriginal
names. Between Rockhampton and Yaamba,
a distance of 22 miles, there will be I'andon
(mountain wallaby), Panpananco (plurry
good-pfella camp or bosker place), and Ka-
wanafwild flowers or some other vegetable).
Another of the stations is to be named Jar-
dine, after John Jardine, the first police
magistrate of Rockhampton, and boss of the
Somerset settlement, formed at the top of
Cape York in 1563-by the British Govern-
ment.
“Politicus”: How is it that when Queens-
land Cabinets are in process of repair and
renewal the claims of James Forsyth,
Philp’s brother-in-law, are so persistently
overlooked in favor of the pretensions of
men not half as able? It looks as though
he may have to wait until Philp comes in
again—a contingency not very far away if
the ex-Premier only cares to give the signal.
But the genial Robert is not anxious to take
on the butcher’s job of retrenchment that
must inevitably follow the present extrava-
gance.
After hunting down the milk sophisticator
for nearly half a dozen years, the Banana-
land Health. Department still contrives to
collect £B4. in fines rom milkmen in one
week. It is high time the more-than-onee-
convicted adulterator was obliged to carry
on his cart a conspicuous sign running this
fashion: —■
MALAOHI MJXK 011.
Milk (and Water) Vendor.
Fined three times for defrauding his customers and
poisoning little babies with a blend of poor
milk and dirty water.
BEWARE !
“Platman’s” yarn :—■
When the Brisbane strike was a couple of days old,
Home See. Appel obtained a permit from the Trades
Hall to deliver commodities to a Government chari-
table institution, rather than run the risk of having
the place put on short commons. That caused a row
in the Ministerial room. The two men who most
easily allow political differences to develop into per-
sonal bitterness are Barnes and Macartney, and
neither of them has ever forgiven Appel. The former,
however, has a keener eye to his own advantage, and
has concealed the hatchet, whereas Macartney con-
tinually flourished it. Denham, Grant and
Tolmie did not, like the other two, think that Appel
had committed .the unforgivable sin; besides, they
probably recognised that, as far as popularity was
concerned, Appel was easily the strongest man in the
Cabinet, So Appel remained a Minister and a red
rag, The trouble ended in a burst-up when chief-
policeman’s Cahill’s job came up for review, and it
was. Dreposed to fix him in it for 10 years. Macartney
was Cahill’s chief backer. Appel rebelled. Macart-
ney saw which way the Cabinet cat would jump—•
and get out,
Winton is the prize lucky district of
Queensland. During the last 20 months
£23,000 went into that woolly region from
Tattersall’s. The faith of the West in
Tatt.’s is beautiful to behold.
Captain G. A. Richard, formerly general-
manager of Mount Morgan, just back from
Asia, gives a few facts about wages and
cost of living in Japan:—
Weaver, male, lid. per day; ditto, female, 6jd. ;
tailor, 124 d. ; carpenter, 20d. ; plasterer, 20}d. ;
sawyer, 181 d. ; bricklayer, 24d. ; shipbuilder, 20d. ;
cabinetmaker, 18d. ; blacksmith, 16d. ; silk-spinner
(female), GJd. ; farm laborer, 9jd. ; day laborer.
12d. ; maid servant, ss. ltd. per month. The Mining
Bureau gives the wages of men employed in mining
for metal as 16d. to 81 d. per day. Men employed in
coal mines get 13d. to 2s. 6d. ; timber men, 11 Id.
to 2s. Bd. Female labor is largely employed in
mines, but the women’s wages are much lower than
the rates for men. In collieries miners work nine
hours ; other workers, 11 hours. In Government fac-
tories the minimum working day is 91 hours, the
maximum 12. In private factories (in 1909) the
hours ran: Silk filatures, 13 hours a day: spinning,
12 hours; weaving, 12 hours; matchmaking, 10;
paper-making, 111 hours, each with one hour’s rest.
Cost of living for husband, wife, two children and an
old mother of 60 years(monthly earnings 100 sen, or
605.) would be: Rent, 6s. per month; rice, 165.;
fuel anti oil, 6s. 4d. ; fish and vegetables, 7s. ; sugar,
soy, salt and tea, 3s. 9d. ; liquor and tobacco, 6s.
7d. ; school expenses, 2s. 6d. ; children’s allowance,
2s. sd. ; baths and water rates, 9d. ; hairdresser,
61d. ; clogs, Is. ; newspaper, 6d. ; paper and matches,
sd. ; clothing, ss. 6d.
Incidentally, Captain Richard found that the
Japanese have “a greater tail of indifferent
workers than Europe, America or Aus-
tralia.” It may interest the Australian
woman who buys cheap Jap. draperies to
know that she is patronising factories where
women work 12 and 13 hours a day for a
wage of 6|d. per diem, and it may interest
Andrew Fisher and co. to learn that goods
made under such conditions are daily brought
into Australia to compete with “white” pro-
ducts owing to the Labor Government’s in-
efficient tariff.

PROFESSOR (grimly) : stones? Ah, yes. They will be found in the appendix .”
Mt. Morgan’s patriotic Town Council lias
a curious idea of assisting the great Defence
movement. Recently the Department asked
it for the use of a piece of ground which had
been a goat-run for years. The council said
“Yes”- —for £4O per annum. At the most,
the goat-run is worth £875 outright; and
£4O per annum for 21 years is £B4O. If Mt.
Morgan Council would only value its tax-
payers’ land on the same scale, it wouldn’t
always be stony broke, though some of the
taxpayers might be.
The Burial.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
A white-throat sang —a white-throat sang
As though he kept the joy we lost
Imprisoned in bis little throat
And loosed in sweet, defiant note
To challenge all the singing host.
And so our ears with music rang
As slow and sorrowful we twain
Went out to bury love !
Your face was hidden ’neatli the frown
Of your too-lieavy hair;
Your shoulders drooped beneath the load,
Your slim feet faltered on the road
We once had found so very fair
To dance and dally down;
And drear, dead leaves of old desire
Were thick—to bury love !
The silence fell....the silence fell.
Ah God ! If You would only speak,
In thunder or in lightning flash,
Some memory-drowning awful crash
To blind the eyes and blanch the cheek,
It might have helped the Hell,
Upon whose edge we stood and smiled
And smiled, to bury love!
And then there came the pulsing kiss
That was the very first we had,
And then the little broken word
Only the dark and star-hush heard,
And all that was so dear and Dad....
And then.... the full completed bliss.
You swayed a little on the edge,
Yet were the first to cast the earth
Across the breast of love!
And all the while the white-throat told
Of trust that has no earthly end,
Of wedding rings and bridal blooms
And baby-cries in darkened rooms.
But when in tones so calm, so cold.
You spoke my name and called me
“friend” —
Ah, then I knew the time had come
To hide the face of love.
QUEENSLAND.
M. FORREST.
QUEENSLAND GOSSIP.
Brisbane, December 21, 1912.
My Dear Thargomindah, —
The squeak of the Christmas trumpet is
about the only thing I have heard since
last I wrote to you. The trams are filled
with small pagan boys who industriously
wipe the jam from their fingers and the dust
from their shoes upon their unhappy neigh-
bors, and at every street corner wor 1 ied
mothers count their broods and split the air
with shrieks of "Bill!” or “Jim!” The sea-
son of alleged peace and goodwill is thus
clearly demonstrated.
Governor Macgregor, with a perspiring
aide, a fully equipped geologist and a cara-
van of some dozen others, clomb to the cool
top of Tambourine Mountain early in the
week. The respite from the atmosphere of
the city was brief, for next day the party
returned. After a night’s rest, his over-
worked Excellency got back to hard graft
in the morning, when he presented, in the
presence of a bunch of femininity, a weighty
purse to Miss Wilkinson, late headmistress
of the Grammar School. The purse was
wrapped up in a bundle of speeches.
The two Qualtrough sisters left on Mon-
day. bound for Sydney. Brisbane' flitters are
many at present, and include nearly all the
male portion of the University staff and their
partners. I must tell you, incidentally, my
dear, that pretty little Miss Ulrich will not
be one of the staff next year.
Bishop Frodsham, bound for England and
the cold'weather that is so trying to the cul-
tivated Australian, passed through Brisbane
this week. Mrs. Frodsham and the kiddies
sailed by the Osterley a fortnight ago, and
so will have more intolerable cold to put up
with than the Bishop. However, they are
younger and can stand it better.
Ex-Minister Macartney lias flown South,
presumably to meet “Bob” Philp and
tell him how dreadfully the party he
used to lead (or mislead) has been
behaving itself. Or it may be, lie wants to
have a word or two with Robert concerning
the Meat Trust, which has started to spend
£300,000 here. Both of them are understood
to be very friendly towards the visitor, and
to be more or less interested in its success.
Which reminds me that the report of an
interview with the ex-Premier has been sent
along from the South. As usual, Mr. Philp
cries for capital, capital, and is much dis-
tressed that Labor legislation is keeping it
away. Perhaps. And perhaps not. The present
rising generation’s father tells of the time
when capital simply rolled in, and, though
there wasn’t any Labor party at the time,
such dreadful things were done with the
money that Queensland’s biggest bank hasn’t
sent out a single dividend notice since, hav-
ing been too busy paying up by slow degrees
the ancient creditor. There’s a lot still to
pay, as Mr. Philp may know, being a bank
director himself; and I’m wondering whether
that fact hasn’t had just a little to do
with John Bull’s and Sandy Mcßull’s pre-
sent shyness.
Yours affectionately,
AUSTRALIA JANE.
Home-Secretary Appel is just about to
wipe out the toll on Bundaberg’s Burnetr
bridge, abolishing thereby the only bridge
toll now existing in Australia.
ALDEKMAM
DIDDUMS,
ex-Mayor (and
many other things)
on Brisbane.
Nine of the principal bakeries of Bris-
bane, doing about three-fourths of the trade,
have formed themselves into the Automatic
Bakeries, Ltd. As usual, it is carefully
pointed out that the new concern isn’t a
trust or a combine, but just an amalgama-
tion to prevent overlapping and waste, and
to secure better bread, made with up-to-date
sanitary appliances, at the old rate. So far,
of course, the idea is excellent. But the
thing will need watching. The means to do
ill deeds pretty often make ill deeds done;
and unless the Commonwealth is given the
powers for which the Government is now
asking, Brisbane may yet have to pay 6d.
a loaf for its principal fodder. The means
to stop ill deeds being done pretty often pre-
vent ill deeds being done.
Judge Chubb gave wlmt may prove to be
a momentous decision recently. A certain
John Smith, engineer in the Brisbane
Tramway Co.’s power house, refused
during the late strike to obey the
order to down tools and come out.
For this he was excluded from the
benefits of his union. Smith brought an
action to compel re-instatement. Justice
Chubb held that the Court had no power to do
what he asked, as the Amalgamated Society
of Engineers was acting in restraint of trade
when it joined the strike, and was therefore
an illegal combination. He held that on the
evidence the principal object of the union
was to get control of the member’s right to
work for whom he pleased, and for that and
other reasons the union had lost its legal
status, and could not sue or be sued. If this
extraordinary judgment is maintained as
sound, an industrial union will be about as
potent for fight as a hamstrung mule. It
would, however, be interesting to know what
Chubb would do in such a case as this: Sup-
posing a mining co. illegally bought some
beer (not being authorised by its articles to
buy beer), and supposing it then refused to
pay a solicitor his bill, would his Honor
decide that the solicitor couldn’t recover,
the co. having lost its legal status? And if
a shareholder had had his shares wrongly
forfeited, would his Honor tell him that he
had no redress, the co. being no longer cap-
able of being sued?

ARE YOU
DEAF?
If you are deaf, or going deaf, you will be glad to
hear of a simple and scientific device which restores
hearing, no matter how old you are. No mystery or
secrecy about it; we describe it fully, and explain
just how it acts. You use spectacles as sight magni-
fiers, and in the same way you use this sound magni-
fier to hear better. Unlike spectacles, the Wilson
Common-sense Ear-Drums are invisible and comfort-
al "f- No one can tell you are wearing them. They
rest the ear nerves by taking the strain off them —the
strain of trying to hear indistinct sounds. What a
relief to have done with that tiring strain. Being
made of the softest Para rubber they cannot be felt,
even when the head rests on a pillow. For the same
reason, a blow on the side of the head will not hurt
the ear. They protect the tender inner parts of the
ear, that are so sensitive, from cold winds, dust, sud-
den and piercing sounds.
So that you may clearly understand how the Sound
Magnifiers restore hearing, we give an illustration of
one as it lies in the ear. You will notice that the
Sound Magnifier is shaped like a trumpet, and so con-
veys even the faintest sounds right to the natural drum.
Being of the softest rubber, it cannot hurt the ear.
It fits the orifice of the ear
with precision and comfort.
The division that runs
across the mouth is to al-
low the removal and inser-
tion of the Ear-Drum, and
to brace it.
The shape of the Wilson
Ear-Drum has been chosen
after years of experience as
ed to carry the
faintest sound.
Of course, if
you have lost
the h e a r i nn-
nerve c o in-
pletely, or were
born deaf, we
cannot pretend
to euro you. Only a miracle could
do that, in spite of some of the
absurd claims made to-day. But
of you can hear the faintest sound,
do not despair. You will never
reftret trying the Wilson Ear-
Drums. Wo have letters to show
you from deaf people in Australia
and New Zealand, who have been
IQ
cured, and you can be cured, too. You inav wear
the Ear-Drums day and night, and take them out
whenever you wish. Children can wear them (we
have special sizes for children from six to twelve).
Even a child can insert and remove them without the
least danger.
The price is £1 (the fee for a doctor’s single
v, sit); This includes a pair of drums (they arc only
sold in pairs), a fine nickel remover, and also an
inserter. After the first pair is bought you may pur-
chase a single drum at any time for Bs., hut the
pair you get at first will last quite two years. Do
not ask for a trial pair. We do not send them out
on trial, and so they are never secondhand. Use the
Order forrn below, and the Outfit will he sent you
at once, with the fullest instructions. Don’t deiay,
but have the comfort of good hearing as soon as you
can.

Those who are troubled with falling hair, dandruff,
baldness, itching scalp, etc., know full well the
humiliation that they cause. There is no need, how-
ever, of such conditions. These troubles can be
quickly overcome by my True Hair Grower.
I am not an advertising quack, but am a business
man. My hair began to grow thin about the time
I reached the age of thirty. When I had reached the
age of thirty-five the top of my head was quite bald.
I had considerable pride, and sought for something
to cause renewed growth. I invested in every pre-
paration that I could see or hear of, and here iet me
say that not only did I try all the English so-called
restorers, but those of Continental Europe. I also
visited specialists. My experimenting cost me many
sovereigns and considerable time —yet the hairs came
not. I had given up the idea of ever growing hair
again. One afternoon, while enjoying a brief busi-
ness respite by an Alpine trip, I fell in with an old
collegiate chemist, whose hair was particularly plen-
tiful. I jokingly remarked that I wished he could
spare some of his hair for my benefit. This lead him
to tell me that he understood the human hair as well
as a good dentist understands teeth. He wrote out a
formula, and suggested that I have a quantity of the
compound prepared. I did this a few days later. I
was astonished to see the hair coming forth after
several applications, and within six weeks my head
was covered by a firm, yet soft, black growth of hair.
Since then the hair on my head has been as luxuriant
as when in my school days. Having arranged for
the legal right to do so, I have for some time been
privately selling this marvellous hair-growing com-
pound. I hold over one thousand letters from people
who have actually grown new hair through the
medium of my true hair grower. I do not pretend
to say it is the only hair grower in the world —I am
not interested in decrying others—but I do assert
that it is the most reliable, and in the end by far the
cheapest.

Business, Robbery, etc.
“Melb.”: A test has been made of the safes in
use in the Commonwealth’s strongrooms, where the
four-million gold reserve is lodged. The scientific
burglar nowadays has a device for generating oxy-
acetylene gas, which, directed in a blower, reduces
the hardest steel to butter. The Australian Gov-
ernment keeps secret its device for baffling the ener-
getic chemist, though this much is known: the outer
casing of the safes is a thick band of steel, with
copper sheets and concrete. Strange to add, the
steel is essentially weaker than the copper and
concrete. The harder the steel, the more vulnerable
to the burglar’s new ally. The Melbourne tests have
been satisfactory, so Fisher sleeps soundly.
. “Kuscobin”: Napierite, mistakenly referred to as
Powerite in recent cables, is an Australian-invented
explosive,, manufactured in two types—No. 1, the
“high explosive” used in shells for bursting on
impact (just as lyddite does), and No. 2 (corres-
ponding to cordite), for firing from the small-arm or
cannon mouth. It is the latter, if it has the
virtues reported of it, that promises to revolutionise
gunfire in modern warfare. At Gov. Chelmsford’s
initiative it was tested for eight months by the
British Admiralty officials connected with the Pacific
Squadron, and was proved by them to have nearly
twice the penetration of the explosive in service use.
And there were other advantages. Cannon from which
most “high explosives” are fired, have to be thor-
oughly cleaned within a few hours of use. If left
for a couple of days, the acids generated in the firing
will corrode the barrels. The Commonwealth authori-
ties proved Napierite generated no acids what-
ever. Add to this that Napierite is smokeless, fume-
less and odorless, and gives a very slight recoil. It
is generally known that the French have a secret
mechanism which enables them to work their quick-
firing guns 25 p.c. faster than any other nation.
Where Napierite is used ordnance experts believe they
will be able to equal this French record, owing to the
slight back-kick. As far as securing any proprietor-
ship or first call on Napierite, the Australian
Government seems to be nowhere. But in a state of war
civil rights would go by the board, and the Defence
authorities- would commandeer -all local supplies, and
no doubt would also insist on continued manufacture
by the Brisbane co. for war requirements, if the
enemy was kind enough not to rush things.
* * * *
“Larrakeeyah”: The Government refrigerator at
Darwin has given a knock to a Private Enterprise
monopoly. The meat trade of the town has been for
years in the hands of one man, who holds so much
country round Darwin, that he has so far had no
need to fear opposition, and has been able to supply
the community with beef of whatever quality he
likes to sell, at his own price. Even the hotels,
with their monthly bills of £4O and £5O, were
compelled to pay the same price (6d. per lb.) as
the small householder with a daily half-pound o’
steak. * The refrigerator opened a way out. An
hotel proprietor asked for discount on a month’s
bill of nearly £5O, was refused, paid his bill and
ordered a carcase or two from Brisbane, storing it
in the Government cool chamber, and taking out as
required. Others followed suit with such enthusiasm
that Private Enterprise brought down his prices all
round and became unusually and surprisingly polite.
Now, what about that State steam laundry? Is
Thomas going to allow Joe Cook to laugh him off
it? In a country like this, where everyone must
wear washable clothes, or become a public offence at
the end of a week, the laundry business is a serious
item. At present it is in the hands of the Chinese,
who assist their countryman, the Chinese tailor, by
washing the clothes with the help of a stiff bamboo
brush. And has Jocook ever seen a Chinese laundry-
man damping the clothes preparatory to ironing?
Serviettes and pocket handkerchiefs are treated in
the same way. Sing Soon takes a mouthful of water
and gently sprays the table linen and clothes and
handkerchiefs through his unclean dentures. A
glimpse into any one of the dozen alien laundries
in Darwin would make Joseph clamor for a State
laundry. He would feel the “dobi itch” at once.
* * * *
“Tiaki”: Some few years ago The Bulletin
printed an article showing how the reduction of duty
on white lead had closed a white-lead factory near
Sydney, and displaced a number of hands. At that
time white lead was selling at about £4 per ton less
than it was quoted in Maoriland. After the closing
of the Australian works, the article was bumped up
to about £2 10s. more than the M.L. selling price.
The foreigner had first of all killed the Australian
industry by selling at a loss, and then made good
the loss by charging a monopoly price. Some light
is probably thrown on the matter by a notice sent
out by a large firm of English color grinders: “The
lead corroders of Great Britain have formed a com-
bine (or trust, we prefer to call it) regulating the
price of dry white lead to the grinders, and have
compelled us to raise the price of ground white lead
to what we call a ridiculous price.” The sinking
and subsequent rising of white lead prices in Aus-
tralia is thus easy to understand. In M.L. there
are numbers of industries which have died the unpro-
tected death. One, a glass-blowing works, was
boycotted by the big importers, and went the way
of the weak. That mighty industry, the production
of population, is also languishing, and Bull’s vessels
are bolting back and forth across the deep in a- vain
endeavor to keep the supply of imported ‘incum-
brance” up to the demand. In fact, about the only
things which seem to be increasing in M.L. are
mutton, motor-cars, the National debt, strikes and
sly-groggers.
A THEME FOR JUBILATION.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The cemetery, which has been the centre of a great
deal of argument lately over the question of obtain-
ing a park for Newtown (population 29,000), con-
tains about 18,000 bodies. Here lie the victims of
the Dunbar wreck. An old resident, Mr. Sampson,
well remembers their burying. “It was about eight
o’clock in the evening,” he said. “Rev. C. C. Kemp,
the rector, buried them. A full moon was shining,
and it was sad, sad, to see the hearses driving up in
that bright light to the cemetery. —Extract from full-
page daily paper article, re jubilee of a Sydney
suburb.
Observe our dead ! They rest
In straight and decent rows.
(O Corpses doubly blest!
In Newtown you repose!)
The People of the Past,
Here in their earth-cells pent,
Still serve the purpose vast
Of our advertisement.
Though high above each head
The waving grass you see,
We don’t shut out the dead
From our live ju l llee.
There is a reason good ;
Deep in this crowded clay,
In rotting shells of wood,
Old Grief is packed away.
The Dunbar bodies here
Are treasured. One drear night
In moonlight coldly clear
They stowed them out of sight.
The undertaker’s men
Strange hats and whiskers wore;
Their like will not again
Cheer up the Styx’s shore.
Some Eighteen Thousand dead
This splendid graveyard line;
Outside the living tread —
(In thousands) Twenty-nine.
Don’t live and die afar!
In Deadtown you still leaven
The festivals ! We are
(In thousands) Forty-seven!
N.S.W.

A Bush-Bard Sonneteers His
Girl.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
She may not he treated classically
OH ! could I sit in calm appraisement
here,
A minstrel of true academic mien,
And sing the beauty of my bushland
queen,
Full many a symbol of the sonneteer
Would tell how like a rose she does appear ;
Full many show how clearly can be seen
The hazel glamor of her laughing een,
The dainty beauty of each shell-like ear.
Then would you hear her voice, the night-
, ingale, , . ,
And see her blushes in the early dawn.
Her gold hair as the sun makes haste to
rise; .
And, maybe, from the academic tale,
As jewels from old caskets, could be
drawn
A picture such as virtuosi prize.
But as a sweet hush-woman.
Blit classic thought and classic, simile,
The image elegant of Attic time,
The rounded beauty of the perfect rhyme
Such as our critics crave are not for me;
And even if they were I know that she,.
Fair daughter of a still unfettered clime,
Would take bush-freedom with the verse
sublime,
And ridicule the ancient imag’ry.
So, in the open spaces of the South,
For one great passion of the bush I bring
Rough words that may not be misunder-
stood,
And for herself no homage of the mouth,
But love that sees her throned in every-
thing— , ,
Austral in all except her womanhood.
She is lihe a creek-side.
She is so like a creek-side in the spring,.
Her voice the song of the harmonious
thrush,
That there is hope of seeing wild bees,
crush , .
Into her golden hair on eager wing.
Thinking the wattles are reblossoming;
Or of the fern-fays, with cool waters
flush,
Taking her presence for the noonday hush.
Pulling stray strands to make the heath-
bells ring.
She has the grace of August hickories;
The brown of fern-boles slumbers in her
e-yes;
And ;her breath comes and goes boronia
sweet
From lips like blooms of poinciana trees.
Oh, she is rarer than our edelweis,
Yet homely as a field of ripening wheat.
And a harbinger of Spring.
Her footstep is as musical as waterfalls,
And light as hakea seed winged to defer
To the warm breeze which acts inter-
preter
When Autumn to the scented Summer calls.
Yea ! and her native beauty so enthralls.
The bush-larks and the bluecaps look at
her
As if she were the Spring-time’s har-
binger.
And straightway sing their mating madri-
gals.
The lissomness of white gums is her own,
And she moves softly as the wild flowers
climb
Through velvet grasses up the mountain
height,
Her presence fragrant as a rose full-blown.
And sweet as southwinds in the summer-
time
When earth lies aching ’neath the hot-
browed night.
And all-provocative of Love.
Yet. O Faireen! I looked but in your
And love came to me like the driven fire
When the wild north-wind with consum-
ing ire
Swept from the rocky ranges to the rise
W'mre cornfields ripened for the sacrifice :
Nor did the flames of passion there ex-
pire.
But, forest-deep, against the night’s attir-'
Built blazing beacons that have scorched
the skies.
My being is that forest; night and day
r i' l he burning eucalypts are fiercely fed

But. though all hope in incense waste awav,

E. S. EMERSON.
Elder Man’s Lane
I.—JOHNSON’S JAG.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
WITHIN a sling-shot of The Bul-
letin office, in a northerly direc-
tion, lies a waterside alley which
I call Elder Man’s Lane, because the one
flagged footpath is so narrow that it is im-
possible for two ordinary men to pass each
other on it, though two “slabs” might sidle
past face to face (they might do it back to
hack, but that wouldn’t be polite), and it
seems the unwritten law of the lane that
the younger wanderer therein should step
off into the dust or mud and give way to the
seemingly older one. The lane is unmade,
as yet, and can be muddy or dusty when it
likes.
The lane runs from George-street North
to nowhere in particular. You can climb
out of it by a green hill if you think you’re
wanted at the other end (there’s an old
pub on top of that hill, and many ways of
escape) ; but if you stick to it, it will lead
you to a dingy North Shore horse ferry, and
so may be said to lead jto all the Northern
suburbs. For the matter of that, it might
even be said to lead to Europe, Asia, Africa
and America, and a good many of the South
Sea Islands ; for there’s a short side-branch,
or bottomless pocket, a step down to the
wharves where the: principal liners lie. A
good deal, of our raw material goes out ot
the “country that way, so “bottomless
pocket” fits the case. The Lane itself is
boomerang-shaped, so you can’t see what s
at the end of it till you get there. It s like
Middle Age Lane in this respect. I know
of no other lane less frequented by ordinary
folk, or the aristocracy, or the demi-monde;
but the dead pass through it often. Also,
the deadbeat, and those who wish they were
dead —or drunk.
Occasionally, about nine o’clock on a hne
morning, you’ll see a fairly well-dressed and
apparently respectable citizen and business
man coming over on the horse ferry, 01 pei
haps he has left. the ferry and got into
Elder Man’s Lane. On the ferry lie holds
himself aloof, paces up and down slowly,
even nervously, or resolutely surveys the
beautiful harbor with his back to the world
and all its paltrinesses ; yet he seems to have
a definite immediate object in view. And
you may be almost sure (1) that that man
has been in very recent trouble, extending
over breakfast this morning; (2) that he
has eaten little or no breakfast; and (o)
that the definite object is to get a good
whisky and soda just as soon as lie reaches
a bar he. knows. I’ll tell you .how it was.
There’s my friend Johnson —or, rather,
there was. Better put it all in the past
tense. Johnson lived on the Shore, and, was
employed in a Government office where he
was indispensable. Say, draughtsman or
something. He was exceedingly clever in
his profession, and so, of course, lie drank
too much. When his “week” came to him
—generally at the end of the month—he’d
leave the office with his screw, and have a
few drinks with the fellows at the Exchange
or Empire; then, as repeatedly instructed
right out to, the gate that morning, he’d
conscientiously take a tram back and
down to Anthony Hordern’s, with a finger
and thumb constantly and anxiously feeling
a half-sheet of cheap, closely-folded note-
paper in his upper left-hand waistcoat
pocket—a scrap of paper lie had been con-
stantly losing, and hunting for, and finding
again all day, and which contained some-
thing as follows, written in a feminine hand
that was characteristic :
1 yard Black Satin for Piping.
J yard Fancy Cream Lace for Yoke.
2 doz. Black Satin Buttons.
1 yard cream gipuirieging (it looks like that).
2 reels of silk to match material.
2 doz. Pat. Fasteners.
2 cards hooks and eyes.
1 set collar supports.
Be sure you go to Hordern’s, and don’t forget what
I told you. And don’t lose this.
Then Johnson would have another drink
to clear his brain and brace him up; and
then a hurried, blurred recollection of
carved lifts, and vistas of varnish and
everything a woman knows, and lovely,
graceful, saint-like shop girls (or sales
ladies), and a smiling, sympathetic shop-
walker taken into his (Johnson’s) confi-
dence, and asked if he was a married man.
and informed in return that he (Johnson)
was a married man, too, and shaken hands
with, with the exaggerated warmth of
affected sympathy—and shown that list.
Then various stairs and different depart-
ments. The shop-walkers and salesmen on
different floors and behind different counters
would stick to Johnson like a brother and
see him through. Perhaps they were mostly
married men, too. Then, after a friendly
and jocular (on Johnson’s part) interval,
he’d be conducted gently to the right lift
and bowed in, after the last shop-walker
had smilingly declined an invitation to
come out and have a drink. And Johnson
would find himself on the level again, with
his bag half full of very soft and perishable
feminine rubbish in brown tissue paper par-
cels —and in urgent need of another whisky.
Then Johnson would charge two Circular
Quay trams, catch a third, and cling to it
tooth and nail, possibly with an instinctive
idea of putting as much of the jumbled city
behind him as he could in the shortest pos-
sible time, and regaining the beloved vicinity
of Circular Quay, where he would feel safe.
He’d drop off short of the Quay, have an-
other drink, and then go into Jack Sotero’s.
the Greek hairdresser’s, to have a shave and
a shampoo, maybe, to freshen him up.
and to collect his thoughts. (It’s all right!
We’ll get back to the horse ferry and Elder
Man’s Lane soon enough.) And he’d get
talking with Sotero about the Eastern
Question, and maybe argue, and they’d
both get excited. Sotero would, sure.
Then Johnson, partly sober, would retire
to a private place and take stock of his
finances. He’d abstract a few more shil-
lings, for current expenses, from that por-
tion of his monthly “screw” set apart earlier
in the afternoon for the use of his wife,
wrap the remainder up tight in another
half-sheet of newspaper, and button it up
in his hip pocket. Then he’d make for the
Quay, and get another drink and more in-
spiration on the way. After which the
world would begin to move.
Fate would have one (or, more likely,
two; they mostly go by twos) of Johnson’s
deplorable acquaintances loitering on the
footnath, opposite the Quay; and Johnson
would stumble into their arms: and they’d
go into one of the first-and-last hotels to
see if they could keep another down. And,
as likely as not, they’d find others of the
Johnsonian school there.
The vicinity would remind Johnson of fi«h
and another thing; so they’d adjourn, and
maybe there’d be stewed oysters and John-
son would make one or two purchases on his
own account. But the fat, futile, vacuously
smiling, oily faces of degenerate Italy would
exasperate Johnson b.v-and-bye, and the
trouble in Southern Europe would come to
the surface again, and Johnson would hold
forth to the edification of the cropped and
grinning Dagoes. He’d be all Turk now (he
was always for the weaker side, was John-
son). But his friends would steer him out
before Tripoli had a chance of being avenged.
Then they’d adjourn to keep another one
down, and Johnson would buy a “parrot”
(i.c„ flask of whisky for the morning), with

Do YOU get Pains in the Loins and Back?
Gravel? Dropsical Swellings? Rheumatism?
Kidney and Bladder Trouble is Serious!
Backache is generally misunderstood. Many people
neglect it because they believe it to be muscular weakness due
to overwork. So they wait for it to pass off.
But when backache does not pass off, we know that the
pain comes not from the back, but from the kidneys, which lie
beneath the back.
It is a warning, and it is a serious warning, for when the
kidneys are weak the uric acid poison they leave in the blood
begins to spread all over the system —
Makes your back ache, your limbs ache —settles into
urinary , sediment or gravel, which irritates the bladder and
keeps it in a constant state of discomfort —clogs the urinary
channels and stops the free passage of water, causing the
swellings of dropsy —forms into sharp, jagged crystals in the
muscles, joints and nerves, as in rheumatism and sciatica.
If you have any of these symptoms, begin with Doan’s
Backache Kidney Pills without delay, for the longer kidney
troubleds neglected the harder it is to cure. Doan’s Backache
Kidney, Pills gently lead the kidneys back to health and
activity. They relieve the urinary system like laxative medi-
cines relieve the bowels. They prevent waste water from stay-
ing too long in the body, and they drive out the uric acid
poison which causes the above symptoms and many more.

the fixed Intention of going right across and
straight home now. Ilis friends would see
him to the turnstiles, and he’d go through,
informing an unresponsive public, in a loud
voice and with curious inconsistency (con-
sidering the fish shop), that Her Naid grace
had brought him home, To the grandeur
which Was Greece, And the glory which was
Rome. He had the choice of two ferry
boats —Milson’s Point and Lavender Bay—
atid he was known eoually well on either;
but his Fate would put him on the most
crowded one of the evening. and the one with
the greatest number of his acquaintances,
friends, enemies, neighbors and those who
knew him by sight. And, of course, he’d
make a prize ass of himself. He’d surpass
all previous efforts. _ He’d hold forth on
politics, and he’d sing and recite revolu-
tionary verses. He’d be “Wae’s me for
Prince Chairlie” till his friends were heart-
broken, and he’d bellow while his enemies ap-
plauded. He was pro-Russ, pro-Boer, pro-
Australia, pro-liusband, pro-man, pro-beer—
all Pro at this stage : and he’d arise on the
upper deck and denounce the Antis with in-
dignation and enthusiasm that brought him
to the verge of tears. And he’d continue on
the tram, only interrupted when he refused
to pay a degenerate Government a fare, and
one of his friends, the tram guards, took his
name and address for appearances’ sake.
They used to have their books full of John-
son’s name and-address. He wrote it him-
self sometimes.
At the last pub on the Height®, near his
home, Johnson would pull himself together
a bit and seek a private parlor (or still more
private place) and once more review his
finances. (Elder Man’s Lane is very near
now.) He’d carefully distribute threepences
and sixpences through his pocket for his wife
to find when he’d be asleep. Then, if short,
he’d borrow another half-crown or ss. from
her share, to finish up the night with, and
have something in hand for the morning;
then he’d carefully wrap up the share again
and button it down in his hip pocket; then
he’d put one half-crown in one of the other
pockets, take off his boot, put the other half-
crown in his sock under the instep, put on
his boot again, and seek the bar. Where, as
likely as not. he’d meet yet another of the
Johnsonian Brotherhood and they’d finish
the night. Unless his Fate led him home
through the main street, singing.-just as the
majority of his fellow-suburbanites were
pouring out of the picture shows and down
to the tram line. Anyway, he’d go his way,
singing in the. moonlight, with two bottles on
top. of his wife’s forgotten vanities (all is
vanity), and he’d arrive at his own gate,
still singing and still flourishing the Crayfish
of Confidence or the Lobster of Faith.
(Those vain peace offerings!)
He knew, or thought he knew, his wife’s
tastes on all occasions. He’d tell her to nut
her hand in his coat Pocket, and see what
she’d find, and as like as not she’d find
nothing there but dampness. Also, as like
as not, she’d find It afterwards, under the
bottles, and on top of her precious dress
trimmings—a squashed and busted newspaper
parcel of very damp and stringy prawns. But
he’s utterly hopeless, now, lying on his back
on the bed, and singing, or rather roaring, at
the top of his voice, that He’ll vote for Andy
Fisher, No matter what he said! So she
undresses him hastily, gives him a stiff nip.
and puts him under cover. And silence
reigneth.
* * * *
Now you’ve already forgotten the fairly
well dressed and apparently respectable citi-
zen and man of business (or professional
man, or artist) seen occasionally on the
horse ferry, or in Elder Man’s Lane, on a
fine morning about nine. I said I’d tell you
how it was. It was Johnson, the Morning
After the Night Before. Clean-shirted,
clean-collared and clean-socked ; also cleaned
out and tray-bitless. Remember the Sock of
Precaution? The “half-caser” was in her
stocking now, along with the rest.
His wife knew Johnson’s ways. He
would humbly accept the copper "that she
spared (at great personal inconvenience, ac-
cording to herself) from that voracious little
instrument of unblushing capitalism (though
it is painted red) and demoraliser of good
housewives, the accursed gas meter.
Johnson had a season ticket on the passen-
ger ferry; but he’d be far too shaky and
ashamed to face it, so he’d slip unobtrusively
down by-streets and lanes, and along Blue’s
Point-road, and into the horse ferry. Then
he’d get a couple of good whiskeys in the
Bar that Knew Him, and feel his manhood
returning and go to work.
He’d slip out and have another'during the
forenoon and one or two during lunch hour,
and maybe one in mid-afternoon, to keep his
deft right hand steady. Then, be-
tween four and five, several with bachelor
mates or unprincipled husbands who
still held a fair share of their salaries.
The previous night would then be re-
peated, but on a grander scale and with
trimmings. Johnson, with no shopping or
shampooing to cut into his time, would make
a more glorious ass of himself than ever. A
patriot, an orator, a fine singer and reciter,
and the only possible saviour of his country;
and with loftier contempt for the alleged
spirit of Sydney (and more especially that
of North Sydney), he’d time himself to
catch the most crowded ferry boat across.
Sometimes he’d insist on taking a stray
mongrel home with him, in premeditated de-
fiance of Company and Government, even if
he bad to fight for it all the way. And rather
earlier that night he’d be steered lovingly
to his own cate by one or more of the
brethren. And they’d utter words of cau-
tion in whispers that could be heard at the
end of the street, and part with affection
and difficulty. Then Johnson would pull him-
self together, creep in, shut the gate softly,
and go down on his knees and hide two
half-crowns in the dirt under a geranium
bush, fixing the spot in his memory as only
a drunk can, while his careful little wife
watched him thoughtfully from the front
room window.
Then, next morning, the Blast of Repent-
ance on awakening, the horse ferry and Elder
Man’s Lane. And so the thing would be re-
peated to the end of Johnson’s Jag, with
perhaps an interval of a day or two at home
to recuperate. It was bad judgment; for,
whereas, the people of North Sydney might
have seen him sober once a day during his
week or fortnight, they never did. lie
should have gone home by the horse ferry—
But, there! What’s the use of arguing with
a drunk? They don’t see things-as you do.
HERY LAWSON.
The Barman’s Story.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
DIRTY DICK’S place,” said the
handsome barman, “was really a
cellar —or a series of cellars. It
was spotlessly clean for the first few years ;
and he made his reputation—his nickname
came afterwards —by selling the best wines
and spirits to be found in London. Every-
body of any account has been, at one time
or another, a patron of Dirty Dick. Royalty
has taken a drink and smoked a cigar in his
cellar. If he had been the kind of man who
cared for such things he might have owned
the best, or, at any rate, the most charac-
teristic autograph album in Europe. He
had a curious fad which every customer re-
spected. You might take a friend in with
you and have what you requii-ed. But if
your friend asked you to have Another with
him he would meet with a polite refusal.
Dick was an artist and a connoisseur, and
he seemed to think that anybody who had
taken a drink in his cellar should go forth
and turn the drink over upon his palate,
and meditate upon it, and thank God that
there was still such good liquor to be had in
the world, instead of swilling it down and
calling.for more as though it had been com-
mon beer. But you could go out by the
front way and come in by the back almost
as often as you chose. He did a great trade.
And if you had not been at Dirty Dick’s you
had not seen London.
“Well, he made up his mind to get mar-
ried. They said she was a very pretty
woman. Anyhow, he fitted up a suite of
rooms for her in a most elegant fashion. He
owned the whole building, although he con- I
ducted his business in the cellars. She was ,
killed in a collision on the way back from
the church on her wedding day. The famous j
inn was shut up for two days. Then Dick
opened it again, but the rooms he had fitted
up for her wei*e kept locked up. Nobody ■
ever entered them. They were the habita- |
tions of spiders. Gradually the spiders
made their way into the cellars and spun
their webs all over the place. Dick would
not permit them to be touched. They were
sacred. If a spider dropped into your glass i
he would fill up another for you, and care- j
fully rescue the spider. All of his customers
humored his fancy, and his trade became
greater than ever. I’ve seen members of
Parliament and prominent merchants with
tall silk hats duck their heads as they came
inside the door for fear they should break
or disturb the sacred spider-web curtain
that hung across it. And that was how he
came to be called Dirty Dick.
“I don’t know what was the end of him.
I had an engagement in Hamburg for six
months, and when I returned to London
Dirty Dick and Dirty Dick’s sacred cob-
webs and Dirty Dick’s house had vanished.
In its place was a fine new spick and span
hotel called ‘Dirty Dick’s.’ But the custom
had vanished also —run away like water out
of a sieve. It’s always the way,” said the
barman philosophically, “when a publican,
or a grocer, or a draper, or any other kind of
tradesman makes a big name in a small house
—an or’nery-looking old house 'with the
street creeping to its knees —he thinks that it
is up to him to keep pace with the times. So
he pulls down the good old house that
brought him his money and builds a fine
large new one of red brick with white tuck-
pointings and (if he happen to be a publi-
can) with a beautiful new bar, garish with
mirrors, and with never a room or a chair
or a bench in it that an old customer can
feel comfortable in. His troubles about old
customers. Their troubles about him. They
go somewhere else to some other com-
fortable old place where they can sit down
and diank and smoke and yarn without
of their old coats spoiling the new varnish
on the new dadoes or the new counters. I’m
talking about hotels now, because I know
about hotels, but I’ve heard that the rule
applies in other lines of business. What do
you think?”
* * * *
I was quite sure about the matter. There
was, some four or five years ago, a grocer’s
shop in a Sydney suburb. Tlie shop was
old and uncomely, perhaps, but solid-looking.
It had small, old-fashioned windows, which
were so crammed with hams and cheeses, all
of the best, that when you went into the
shop you found yourself in a rich, spicy twi- j
light out of which emerged a stout man in
snowy shirt-sleeves who would ask you in a
rich pleasant voice what he could do for you. j
The man who owned the shop made a for-
tune in it, and his views expanded. He
pulled the old building down gradually and
built a new one on the same site. Brass
rails and rosewood counters and plate glass
windows, through which you could see to the
very furthest end of the Academy of Gro-
cery. No mystery, no suggestion of rich
possibilities in the shape of Arabian Nights’
bargains. Everything plainly ticketed with
its price. The hams —or some other hams—
were still there, but they hung up high on
bright steel hooks, suspended from bright
steel chains, attached to bright steel rods
that crossed the Wholesale and Retail
Lyceum just under its bright blue-steel ceil-
ing. The men behind the counter were also
different in their ways from the assistants
in the good old beetle-browed unpretentious
building. They looked worried and almost
wolfish, and the proprietor, who used to have
a voice like old Burgundy poured from a
cob webbed bottle into a Mura no tumbler,
spoke in rasping tones, and had an anxious
Amei-ican hustling air about him that told
the whole story without a word being said.
The brass-railed, rosewood-countered, plate-
glassed window emporium had turned out a
failure. The old customers patronised it no
xnore, except to the extent of sending their
little girls or boys for bars of soar), or knobs
of blue, or tins of sardines. The grocer's
mana had gone with the house that had •
gone. I knew him very well, and he seemed
nleased to see me again when I spoke to him.
lie put his hat and coat on and we went;
across the road to the hotel.
“By God,” he said over his whisky, and :
shaking his fist at his emporium, “if 1 could
onlv pull that red-brick Jonah down and
build up the old house again what a happy
man I would be !”
If pojlljj ojnlj reconstruct our lives!
V. J. DALEY.
